THE RED PAGE.
[lt would be possible to collect, from newspaper flies
and MSS. in the hands of Kendall’s relatives and friends,
a, body of poetic work probably as large as that included
in the current edition of his poems—less valuable than
that we have, but much of it hardly less interesting.
This characteristic piece was contributed to The Empire .]
TO-
Ah, often do I wait and watch,
And look up, straining through the Real
With longing eyes, my friend, to catch
Faint glimpses of your white Ideal.
I know she loves to rest her feet t
By slumbrous seas, and hidden strand;
But mostly hints o! her I meet .
On moony spots of mountain land.
I've never reached her shining place,
And only cross at times a gleam ;
As one might pass a fleeting face
Just on the outside of a Dream.
But you may climb, her happy Choice !
She knows your step, the maiden true !
And ever when she hears your voice,
She turns, and sits, and waits for you.
How sweet to rest on breezy crest,
With such a Love, what time the Morn
Looks from his halls of rosy rest,
Across green miles of gleaming corn !
How sweet to find a leafy nook,
When bees are out, and Day burns mute,
Where you may hear a passion’d brook
Play past you, like a mellow flute !
Or, turning from the sunken sun,
On fields of dim delight to lie—
To close your eyes, and muse upon
The twilight’s strange divinity !
Or through the Night’s mysteri ms noon,
While Sound lies hushed am > lg the trees,
To sit and watch a mirror’d moon
Float over silver-sleeping seas !
Oh, vain regret! why should I stay
To think and dream of joys unknown ?
You walk with her from day to day,
I faint afar off—and alone.
HENRY KENDALL.
The Northern Traveller.
p r afternoon was dressed in sunshine, and
I from a corner in a balcony I could see the
JL Harbour winking in the distance. The
pure air, the golden light, and the blue
waters were as a demand, and there was a secret
desire towards concession within me. But, on the
other hand, a story moved within me for expres-
sion, and having denied it many times, I could do
so no more with any shred of decency. Setting
my pen to paper I began to write in this wise :
Austin wrote to certain of his Sydney
friends —“Do you rememberour last meeting
when webade farewell to So-and-So ? Well, on
that occasion, the wine of good-fellowship so
> genialised me that I long for another goblet.
A distinguished Northern traveller, an
Asiatic by birth, who for the present must
remain nameless, and to whom I have
spoken of you all, wishes to meet you. Like
the ideal manager that I am, I have
arranged a dinner where good wine may
redden what might only be an acquaintance
into something more deep and lasting.”
“So far, so good,” I said, and lit my pipe. It is a
pleasant thing to watch the play of puffed smoke;
now it whirls like the convolution of a sea-shell,
coming back, at times, to one’s nostrils like a beiii-
son, and at times marbling a fathom of sunshine.
But idle observation belongs to the leisured, and I
had work to do.
Having made Austin write that letter, what
came next ? Ah, yes ! they must all meet toge-
gether—artists and poets and actors. Each being
a Bohemian, good talk will fall from his lips
effortless, like ripe fruit from a tree. But stay, I
said; I set myself a mighty task. How, for
instance, am I going to make that poet—the poet
I have in my mind —speak as he is wont to do?
And that artist with the grip in his eyes—the eyes
that shed an ancient light—how am I going to
paint him ? And the man with ceaseless merri-
ment bubbling on his lips, where am I to get the
humour to make him-make the world laugh ?
In sheer despair I put a pause to these mental
queries.
A policeman has come to a halt a few doors up
the street. He is evidently off duty, because he
wears a pipe and is without gloves. He glances up
at a House to his right and then looks towards me.
I turn from him. I edwdys think that the mind
searching for a thought or word gives an inane
expression to the face, and while I write I do not
like to be studied. Presently the policeman moves
on and passes from my range of vision.
To. the gathering of Austin’s, I said, shall I
bring any editors ? If I do, I must make Such-and
Such a butt, or So-and-So, the Bohemian, will be
displeased. “It is not the things we accept, but
the things we reject, that cut deepest into our
time, ” said an editor ; and last week it fell to the
lot of So-and-So to have a sketch rejected. Since
then So-and-So, daggered and dominoed like a
bravo of old Italy, waits his chance to assassinate
a reputation. Still, having had no quarrel with
editors, I do not see why I should seek one to
gratify another’s spleen. For the most part, they
have reviewed my books in pretty terms and
sweetened with gratitude. I omit them from
Austin’s group.
Now, as to the politicians—what of them ? They
know little of Art and. Literature, and care less.
In the appreciation of most of them a prize pump-
kin outvalues a prize poem ; and so the brilliant
trifles of a Bohemian gathering would be hardly
likely to appetise them. Still, there are two or
three who might be capable of being educated into
an appreciation of ideals as far above those of
politics as a cross-topped steeple is above the
buried foundation. The young fellow with the
head that might have been dug up out of Roman
ruins is one of them. Let him stand among the
lightnings. '
The policeman has come back. His pipe is out.
Over his shoulder he casts a glance at the House
he scanned before. Is it a thief, or is it Mary
Jane ? Perhaps the people of the house have gone
out, leaving Mary Jane in charge. Presently she
may open the door and signal to her cavalier in
blue. I must not seem to be a spectator, or it may
spoil all. But no lit is not going to be a romance.
The policeman leaves the House and paces on his
way, and the asphalt pavement rings to his tread.
Austin and the Bohemians are gathered
in the banquet - room. The tables are
brilliant with silver and roses. Austin takes
the chairman’s place, and each one seats him-
self. The guest’s chair beside that of Austin
is vacant. Where is the guest ? Prom one
to another goes the question till it reaches
the chairman. The chairman is pouring wine
into a glass.
“ Claret gives a tooth to the palate,” he
says,
“But where is our guest?” enquires a
friend.
“ In time—in good time I shall tell you,”
he returns.
Someone says that Austin is a confirmed
trickster, that the Northern traveller is a
fiction—a social loadstone invented for the
centralisation of bright particles, but Austin
does not seem to hear.
The feast goes on. There are some who
have saved their appetites through two meals,
and these are, repaid of tlmir forethought-.
In due time the wine begins to flow and
cigars are tipped with red. An artist is
smelling a rose.
“ Have you a sense of perfume left ? ” asks
a friend. “As for me, the air is drenched
with the smell of chloride of lime.”
At the other end of the table a twin dis-
cussion is going on, and someone calls out,
“ I shall never be friends with red wine
again.”
“ And why, my dainty epicure ? ”
“ Because permanganate of potash is a
persistent vision, and the taste of it a weari-
ness on my palate.”
“That is true,” joins in another ; “the
whole city smells like a hospital.”
There is a thud of fists on the table and
a beating of boots on the floor. It is a signal
that the chairman is on his feet. The noise
dies, and the party wait in silence for his
speech.
“ Gentlemen,” he says, swinging a rose by
its stem, “ it is a trick I have played upon
you, but the pleasantest consequences walk
in its train. The Northern traveller of
Asiatic origin does not exist to the bodily
eye. But then, after all, why should flesh
and blood and bone be everything to us ?
We have imagination ! let him be a creature
of it ! See—he sits here in the chair—
Behold him ! ”
Then Austin goes on to show what an
ideal guest is he whom they have met to
honour, since he can offend no man and no
man offend him. “He is the pet and peer-
less child of each imagination,” says Austin.
A poet rises to propose the guest’s health.
He talks of the Northern traveller’s white
beard and aged eyes. He says that no
doubt in his youth their guest, lying under
Persian roses, drank wine with Omar
Khayyam.
An artist supports the toast. He hopes
that the guest of the evening, gazing through
glory-glutted eyes, will look leniently on
such small beginnings of Art as Australia
has to offer. He . . . but here the artist
begins to'cough. “ Open that window ! they
are burning that confounded sulphur again ! ”
he cries.
The policeman is again on his way up the
street . As he nears the House its door
opens. He quickens his pace —“ Ah, now for
Venus/” I exclaim. A very ladylike young person
comes out the door and lifts ' the latch of the gate.
She has a parcel in her hand and is evidently going
out. The policeman puts forth an arm and stays her.
She says something to him. His head shakes a
negative. She pleads with him. He is obdurate.
She offers him something —is it money? He
turns his head dWay with something of pride and
anger in the toss of it. The young lady lowers
her face , and with a handkerchief to 'her eyes
slowly turns, re-ascends the steps, and entering the
House closes the door behind her. The policeman
gives a hasty look around, and then goes quietly
away. What does it all mean? Is it a romance,
or . . .
Bohemia again—
No other has a mind to speak, so they
all rise and drink the toast. “And now,”
says Austin, with the gravest of faces, “I
must request your complete silence for five
minutes while the guest of the evening
replies.”
One and all fall easily into the moment's
mood. With most of them to be common-
place would be more difficult than to be
eccentric. They had scarcely one among
them of whom it could be predicted that he
would follow a given line of human action.
The trickery in hand suits their tastes, and
they lie back in their chairs and smoke and
are happy because they are unusual.
The policeman has come back again, and takes up
his station outside the House. Two young ladies,
trimly dressed, standing, on the near pavement, are
talking together. They look towards the policeman.
Is it of him they are talking ?
The Bohemians preserve a respectful
silence. The streets are nigh deserted, the
lamps burning whitely in the dry, wind-
swept spaces. The Northern traveller is
supposed to be talking, though no word
reaches the ear of anyone. The poet is
thinking of a poem that he will write on the
morrow, and suddenly a line flashes in on
him—
In Babylon I walked before Belshazzar saw the
writing!
“Yes, yes,” he says to himself, “ hat
will do very well.”
And again :
I slept with the Pharaohs !
“ A perspective opens up to me,” thinks
the poet. “ But who is this that walked
through Babylon and slept in the Pyramids ?
Let me see—let me see ..."
The silence seems pregnant with sugges-
tion, and a picture rises in the artist’s mind.
How restless the street below has grown! There
are groups of girls and men, here and there, in
different places, and they are talking together (how
pale they all are!), and looking towards the House,
in front of which the policeman stands. There is a
drawing of blinds and an opening of doors and a
coming in and a going out in the houses round about
that sends my wits astray and robs me of steady
thought.
The picture that the artist sees is that of
a girl in silk—pink silk—lying dead on the
marble floor of a palace balcony, while far
below, with no smoke rising from it, stands
a deserted city.
“Yes, that is part of an idea," says the
artist; “I may be able to make a picture
when I get the whole."
A man in a corner begins to shiver, and
he rises and shuts a window. The five-
minutes is nearly up, and an actor signals to
a brother actor to pass the'wine. The actor
does not heed him. He is thinking of an
old stage-triumph the night long ago that he
excelled himself in Mercutio. How well he
died then ! lying on his back and railing :
Zounds ! a dog—a rat .. .
A plague o’ both your houses ! . . . and . . .
There is a noise of feet descending the
stairs. Someone groans at every step. The
noise ceases at the landing, and a Bohemian
goes forward and opens the door. He says
something in a loud voice, and is answered
in whispers. A smell of disinfectants floods
the room.
Presently he comes back. He is pale,
and—
There comes a rattle of wheels up the street. Two
vehicles halt in front of the House. Men in blue
leap from the vehicles and run into the House.
The people—all of them white-faced now—draw
away to a distance. A woman answers a passer-by :
“The Plague is in the House !” she says.
“ My God ! ” cries the other.
The words, “ The Plague is in the House ! ”
stumble from the Bohemian’s lips as he
turns and eyes Austin.
“ Not that ! ” cries Austin—“ My God ! I
did not mean that as the Northern traveller,
and yet ”
“ It looks like a grim joke,” finishes some-
one.
My pen was trembling. 1 threw it down.
Sydney, 1900.
RODERIC QUINN.
"International Calamity.”
“DEATH OF THE SACRED IBIS.”
[W. O. Hodgkinson—a man known all over Australia—
who in his (52nd year, after many elbowings with good
and evil fortune, has just found a haven as editor of the
new Q. mining journal under Govt., was in his heyday a
writer with a lovely gift of satire. The follow-
ing specimen of his talent is drawn from the forgotten
flies of a Brisbane newspaper. It refers to the rhetorical
and exploring feats of Archibald Meston, a well-known
Queenslander now doing excellent service as Protector of
Q. Aborigines.]
What may be termed the gestative faculty of
birds is irregular, if not eccentric. Some breed
at one season, some at another, some in dry
weather, others in heavy wet. The Southern
Drake goes North in search of a nest, as his
native haunt is getting too hot to hold him. The
quack, however, is not esteemed in that quarter.
The Phoenix cannot be said to breed at all, but
when its fixed measure of life has expired, one
hundred years, it places a lighted match at the
foot of a hollow tree, creeps into the hole, and
evolves a successor out of its own ashes.
The reproduction of the Sacred Ibis hitherto
has been a sealed mystery. But one has existed,
and his birth is, like that of all celebrities, shrouded
in doubt. Circumstances have attached unusual
weight to the recent movements of this revered
bird. Ornithologists have been long puzzled to
know how long-legged birds incubate, and it is one
of the triumphs of modern science to have ascer-
tained that the gorgeous scarlet Flamingo makes
a hole in the top of an ant-hill and sits straddle-
legs thereon till its young appear. When,
therefore, the Sacred Ibis, whose importance could
not be satisfied with less than a mountain, though
a young Ibis is probably not larger than
a mouse, was noticed roving towards the summit
of Bellenden-Ker, it was surmised that he in-
tended to fulfil an ornithological function by
building a nest and laying an egg. We have it
on his own courteous admission that he succeeded
in getting material from the disused and aban-
doned covert of a nobler bird. There is, in fact, a
certain resemblance between the Sacred Ibis and
the cuckoo, the former having a constitutional
apathy to gather his own sticks. For some time
the Sacred Ibis was invisible, but on the 30th
ultimo he appeared jauntily perched on an abori-
final weapon and announced that he had dropped
is maiden egg in a crooked lane in the vicinity.
Little notice was taken of the fact, but exactly
seven days after, the Sacred Ibis dropped a second
egg, ran cackling about the cold wet streets, and
imprudently died. The eggs have been blown.
They contained no principle of life. .The eggs are
of a watery blue, strangely deficient in lime.
An autopsy shows the exact cause of death to
be verbal prolepsis. '
Yes ! the Sacred Ibis is dead. One falters as
one pens the words. Following so close after the
decease of the great tribune, this second loss is a
staggerer. The Sacred Ibis, to use a sporting
metaphor, is like a cronk pedestrian—a dead
bird. His plumage will never more be ruffled
by the dews of criticism. I write this obitu-
ary with a feather plucked from his vener-
ated wing. No ordinary goose-quill would
serve. I speak under correction, the parish
registers having long since been eaten by that fero-
cious moth, the “ Attacus Hercules, but I believe
the deceased bird was hatched on the Nile, in a
swamp formed by a disused brickyard where
Moses worked before he went to the bar. -tbs, the
Sacred Ibis’, education was accomplished at the
Sesostrian Grammar School, where he carried off
several prizes for tautology, though somewhat of a
flighty temperament. From Egypt he migrated
to Tyre and Sidon, watched the decay of the shep-
herd kings, and supplied Gibbon with personal
recollections of the “Decline and Fall of the
Roman Empire.” Like most aquatic birds, he
was omnivorous. It is due to him that “ Lem-
priere ” has been preserved in its integrity. When
the unspeakable progenitor of the lurk burnt
Alexandria, and the darkness of the Middle Ages
covered Europe, the Sacred Ibis swallowed the
only copy of the work, throwing up the indi-
gestible fragments as demanded by schoolastic
idiots.
The Sacred Ibis was a proficient in athle-
tics. In the modern resuscitation of archery
he was simply inimitable. He drew the bow
with harrowing force —nay, sometimes he over-
shot the mark. He could throw a hatchet with
accuracy to an incredible distance. As a scien-
tific naturalist, he possessed the nerve of Audu-
bon and Waterton combined. He was a skilled
whip, and drove alligators in double harness through
the columns of the press. But it was in descrip-
tive redundancy he shone. No matter how large
the subject he could find a colossal word to over-
shadow it. His coolness was only equalled by his
courage. On one occasion, when it was necessary
to enable the railway surveyors to take the level
of the bed of the Barron Falls, he damped the
roaring torrent with an ejaculation. He slew a
python, twenty-one feet long, with a supple
Greek root of three fathoms. 11 is loss cannot be
replaced. The jaw-bones of the rising generation
will lack due development. He was in his usual
state of health on the morning of the 30th ult.
He was, in fact, interchanging jests with the
Traffic Inspector, who playfully objected to his
blocking the footpath with derivatives. This first
attack passed almost without comment, but a
second issue determined to a bead, which was
weak, though full of pretty conceits.
The form of disease —verbal prolepsis—was, in
his case, chronic, lie suffered from it before he
assumed pen-feathers. The last song of a dying
Ibis is as sacred as the final whistle of a swan.
Grasping the hand of his amanuensih, for with all
his Ornithological ability he never mastered the
mechanical art of writing, he muttered, “It’sa
long lane that has no turning, and a crooked one
at that.” Then he died, and is now cateliing frogs
on the banks of the Styx.
Buskin inherited £200,000; bequeathed £lO,OOO
—liberal in everything but his theory of Art.
Q. People’s Newspaper prints a story of Sandy
Russell—greatest of Scots editors. He rallied a
friend on his grey head—the friend retorted on
Russell’s bald head. “Ah,” said Russell, “my
hair preferred death to dishonour.” But these
are not so well-known : A Free Kirk meenister
caught Russell trout-fishing on the Saw-
bath, and “ improved the occasion.” As a
preliminary to a homily on Sawbath-break-
ing—“ Ah pairceive, Mr. Russell, that ye
are feeshin’ ! Noo——” “ I’ve seen ye fishing
yersel’,” said Russell. “But I, Mr. Russell” —
with dignity—“am a Fisher of Men.” “Ay; I
saw ye at it this morning—and—there werena’ ower
mony fish in your creel?” Another: “Lord
presairve us ! Mr. Russell ”—cried a man rushing
into the sanctum one day—“what do ye think !
what do ye think ! The maist astonishing thing !
Ah was walking in the High-street, and a mon
cam’ up to me, and took off his hat, and ca’d me
‘ Your Grace ’! What do ye think ? He
—took me for the—Duke—of—Buceleuch !
The Duke of ■ Buceleuch /” “Pooh, mon!’ said
Russell, “what’s yon? I was sitting in this
chair—juist where ye see me—not five minutes
syne: and a mon cam’ in—juist as you micht come
in—and what do ye think he said ? What do ye
think ! He said, ‘ Lord Almighty !is that
you ? ’
Other “Australia” sonnets received from
G.W. —
Australia, wild and young, thou Bride of Fire 1
Queen Victoria sent her soldiers chocolate.
Why ? Because she read in Bernard Shaw’s play,
“ Arms and the Man,” Bluntschli’s utterance —
“I’ve mo ammunition. What use are cartridges in
battle? I always carry chocolate instead. You can
always tell an old soldier by the inside of his holsters and
cartridge-boxes. The young ones carry pistols and car-
tridges ; the old ones grub,”
Kipling has a Kaiser on a wire; Shaw has a
Queen on a string—-but Beauty draws A. Pope
with a single hair, as previously.
“Plymouth Sound;” Barbara Grey—
What is the Austral land ? It is a strain
Of mystic music ; life and death are there ;
’Tis bright with hope, ’tis sad with all despair ;
The conquering dominant notes rise high, and fain
Strange haunting wild-birds’ notes join in the air,
And winds and waters the same theme declare—
One race is doomed, and one has come to reign.
What is the Austral land ? It is a page
With but a line or two already writ ;
A canvas bare that waits the Master Hand ;
’Tis for the weary North the heritage
The Sunrise to'the South wind did commit ;
The dream of golden dawn ; the Morning-Land!

CG. HATTE, of King-street, Newtown (near Sydney,
• N.S.W.), has recently made great additions and im-
provements to his Tailoring Department, which is now one
of the features of his extensive business. He receives regu-
lar shipments, from his Home buyers, of all the newest and
most fashionable goods, and has one of the smartest cutters
in the colony Everything is made to measure on the pre-
mises; a perfect fit is guaranteed, and patrons may rely on
every satisfaction being given. Self-measurement forms
and patterns sent free bypost. Hatte’s suits of Indigoserge,
all-wool tweeds, and sergerettes ("with extra pair of trousers
given in) for 42/- are the marvel of tailoring and the talk of
Australasia. For 50/- Hatte makes a suit of tweed or serge
fit for a prince. Inspect or send for patterns of his new
woollens, comprising vicunas. West of England, Scotch and
Blarney tweeds, at 67/6. and 65.'- ; the great value offered.
Hatte makes a smart Chesterfield overcoat from meltons,
vicunas, and Venetians from 45/-, and the new paddock
coat, with full back, side vents, crescent pockets, silk-faced
and velvet collar for 55/-. Hatte’s speciality is in dress-
suits, starting at £3 3s, made of purple dye elastic coating
with silk-facing. For excellence of material, perfect fit,
latest fashion, and superior workmanship, Hatte is un-
equalled, whilst his charges are at least 30 per cent, under
those of ordinary city tailors.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ A hiss is what you always hear when the
waters of truth drop on the fires of hell. Henry
Clay.
The late Marquis of Queensberry was,
anyhow, a bit more respectable than his grandad,
the notorious “ Old Q.” One of the favorite dissi-
pations of that unvenerable nobleman was a
dinner-party (for males only) the “ attendance
being supplied by a choice assortment of nymphs
mit codings on. And yet the late Marquis felt
shocked at the fondness of his son (Old Q. s g reat-
grandson) for the society of Oscar Wilde. It was
a simply natural development.
Knights Of Chance.
Men do not live in cities. Between the narrow
ways
They walk as meek-faced merchants, dissembling
all their days.
Ours are the open places; ours are the Plain and
Sky ;
The clean, deep-hearted Ranges ; the Hills—which
cannot lie !
Men do but dwell in cities.- Yea, and the hollow
paves
Mock at their cunning makers, mock at their
master-slaves,
Breathing a tainted carbon, starved in a gilded
room,
Moving the round eternal, walled in a high-
housed tomb.
Sick unto death have we been. Pining in wan
distress,
Even as whited shadows, in crowded loneliness.
Sick of the garish highways, crushed by the
houses tall,
Sad of the fevered faces; sad, heart-sad of it all.
So, for the brave wide waters ! So, for the kick-
ing screw!
So, for the north wind racing over the white-capped
blue !
So, for the shouting breakers, the track of the
Overland,
The speech of our strong-thewed Brother, the
laugh that we understand.
We shall be no man’s servants, service to truant
trolls
Owe we, and pay we never, pay with our poor-
priced souls.
Green is the land about us, all by the roadside
fair,
Smoke of our oamp-fires rising—pillars of promise
there.
We shall be lords and masters, we shall be Knights
of Chanoe,
Barons of Bold Adventure, Kings of the stout free
lance,
Ours are the unwalled waters, ours are the West-
lands wide,
Rovers and rovers ever, sons of the Outer-side.
So were our sires before us —they of the ready
hand
Sailing to seas uncharted; faring in No-Man’s-
Land.
West to the open sunset! North to the open
sea!—
So were our sires before us, so shall their children
be !
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
The First Shift.
With song one gang moves outward
To the beaches of the Pole;
They curse within the tropics
That sweat to earn their dole.
Through lack or lust they wander
By bands, and roam alone,
With glamor-lights to lead them
Down by-ways none have known.
They find where leads lie buried,
For each is Fortune’s wooer;
They seek the reef-ridged ranges—
The little hills that lure—
The gold-strewn plain, and terrace
Where flume-fed sluice-streams fall:
And though the world may work them,
The first shift finds them all.
They toil, but sign no pay-sheet—
They take their luck for wage ;
And in the lists of Silence
Throw down to Death their gage,
That they who follow after
May find the paths made plain,
That shifts that follow after
The minted gold may gain.
And none may map the marchings
Their legion leaders led,
And all the round world over
They leave their careless dead ;
The dead that join the chorus
Wafted at evensong,
As mate cheers mate’s misfortune
When luckless days are long.
What though the roads be rugged,
And life a questing chance,
When theirs is Freedom’s revel,
Surpassing all romance !
The world’s wealth-wonders find"' \
True friends and comrades bold •,
Their ways are ever leading
Where lies the cloth o’ gold.
N.S.W.

SATURDAY, MAY 26, 1900.
National v. Local Option.
The brewers and publicans of Maoriland—
or some of them—are endeavoring to work
up an agitation for the abolition of the fcocal
Option vote which now disturbs the country
every three years, and the substitution
therefor of a National Option vote to take
place every nine years only. So far as the
dates are concerned, these astute groggists
have undoubtedly a keen eye to the business
aspect of the case. The .last two or three
Local Option polls have shown that the tide
of Cold Tea is steadily rising. If it con-
tinues to rise at the same pace for another
six years many a now prosperous beer-foun-
dry will only show a desolate chimney-top
looming pinnacle-fashion over the troubled
sea of tracts and ginger-pop. When it is
proposed that there shall only be a National
Option vote once every nine years, it is un-
doubtedly meant that the first vote shall take
place nine years hence, and that there shall
be peace until then, and the reverend
apostle shall be suspended in the interval.
In this respect the grog party is asking
altogether too much. No sane community
is likely to give up, for nine years, its un-
alienable right to make itself miserable and
dyspeptic and flatulent on lemonade if it
wants to, and it is not advisable that it
should. Moreover, it is quite impossible
that it should. To-day’s Parliament may
enact, if it feels so inclined, that the next
anti-liquor poll shall come off in 1909, but it
can’t enact that to-morrow’s Parliament
won’t repeal that enactment and enact some-
thing quite different. One person may tie
another person to something, but nobody
can tie himself to himself with any success.
He may pass a law that he won’t do some-
thing, but he can’t pass a law that will
prevent him changing his mind about it
later on.
♦ • * »
But in the matter of substituting National
for Local Option the idea is doubless a
good one. Local Option is a stunted,
half-hearted, illogical idea at best. When a
country tries to decide whether Protection
or Freetrade is best, or State banking or
railway ownership as against private banking
or private railway ownership, or a gold
standard v. a silver one, or whether it is
best to have land-tax or no land-tax, the
country gives its verdict as a whole. So it
does on every other matter of national im-
portance, except on the question whether
drink should or should not be sold. The
law doesn’t allow one electorate to establish
a State Church, and the next one to make
silver legal tender, and a third to carry out
ideas of its own about a standing army, and
so on. In all these matters it is recognised
that there should be uniformity. And the
drink question should be put on the same
basis. Local Option, at the best, can only
dot the country with teetotal townships
and districts, and however thickly they
may be dotted, there are always drink
townships and districts in between.
The natural result is that there is no
real Prohibition anywhere. The person who
used to get drunk across the street gets his
beer in the adjacent township instead, and
as he can’t go to the adjacent township three
times a day he gets all the drunker when he
does get there. Also, he carries drink home
with him to till in the desolate period ahead,
and if he doesn’t kill himself when he falls
off his horse en route as the natural result
of too much grog taken all at once, he is
probably severely cut and bruised through
sitting down suddenly from a great height
on the bottle.
• • * •
Also, liquor is surreptitiously brought into
a Prohibition district from all sides, and
though it may be reasonably pure and
white in the centre (if it is a large
district), it is a fine, deep-brown beer
color round the edges. In fact, Pro-
hibition can’t be anything like complete
under the Local Option system. Its beauties
haven’t a chance to develope sufficiently to
make other districts rush into the same line
of business, and, on the other hand, its
horrors aren’t sufficiently manifested to make
the local inhabitants swear off the system.
It proves nothing. Maoriland has, at Bal-
clutha, a rigid Cold-Tea settlement of some
years’ standing. It is a prohibition settle-
ment, where anybody can bring in as much
drink as it wants. Like other places, it has
its good and bad features. Its good features
are either owing to the fact that its princi-
ples are Cold Tea, or to the other fact that its
practice is largely Beer, or to something else.
Its bad features are either owing to what
Prohibition there is, or to the want of
more Prohibition, or to something else.
On the other hand, National Option, if
it led to National Prohibition, as it
possibly would sooner or later, would
prove something or other. On an
island province, especially, it would
prove a great deal—provided it was real
Prohibition. If for 50 years it was unlawful
in Maoriland to import, to sell by wholesale
or retail, to buy, to brew, distil or otherwise
prepare, to advertise, to own, drink, inject,
rub on externally, to give away, receive,
exchange or to smell of any kind of strong
drink, or to leave the same exposed in a
public or private place, or to incite any other
person or persons to do any of these things,
or to have the empty bottle in the back yard
or elsewhere—then at the end of that time
the world would really and truly know
something about the case for or against Pro-
hibition, always provided that the penalties
were heavy enough and the law was rigidly
enforced. But it can’t know anything worth
mentioning through the local, or spotty or
fragmentary variety of Prohibition. The
thing is so incomplete that it supplies no
evidence which can be sworn to.
* • * »
Local Option, as now practised, is, in
fact, a mean hypocrisy, and considering how
very strong the teetotal party is in Maori-
land it is difficult to believe that it couldn’t
obtain something better. Maoriland is .the
most democratic of the Australias, and there
the doctrine that the majority must rule is
carried most nearly to its legitimate con-
clusion. Yet in liquor matters this principle
is wholly abandoned. At the triennial Local
Option poll three issues are laid before the
electors. If three-fifths of the votes recorded
in any district (600 out of a poll of 1000) are
for “ no license,” then the retail drink trade
stops in the district publicly, at all
events. If more than half the votes (501
out of 1000) are for “ reduction,” then
the Licensing Committee refuses to renew
not less than one-twentieth or more than
one-fourth of the public-house licenses at
next renewal day. If more than half the
votes (501 out 1000) are for “ continuance ”
then the number of public-houses remains
unaltered till further orders. If no proposal
is carried the result is the same. So the
party which desires to keep things as they
are has two chances while the reduction
party and the abolition party have only one
each. It is a ridiculous thing to'so fix
matters that no proposal at all may be
carried, and that the public may find, when
the poll is over, that according to law they
haven't decided in favor of anything, but the
ridiculous law is fixed that way. Where
1000 voters go to the poll, and nobody votes
for keeping the number of public-houses un-
altered, and 599 vote for total abolition, and
401 vote for reduction, the cause which
hadn t a single supporter wins on the
ground that no proposal was carried.
It wins because the 401 are not an
absolute majority and 599 are not a
three-fifths majority. And, on the other
hand, the party which wants more public-
houses isn’t allowed any voice at all which
is another outrage on the democratic prin-
ciple of majority rule.
♦ • * •
And even if a district carries local prohi-
bition it is an absurdly inadequate kind of
local prohibition in other ways besides those
already specified. If, say, Wellington
carries absolute prohibition, that ought to
mean a great deal more than the mere aboli-
tion of retail licenses. It ought also to mean
that no brewers, distillers or wholesale
dealers shall be tolerated in the Wellington
district, that no imported liquor shall
come ashore at Wellington, and that
no liquor, local or imported, shall be
carried by railway or other convey-
ance through the Wellington district
in order to supply debased localities lying
beyond. If drink isn’t good for Wellington’s
body it isn’t good for Wellington’s soul that
it should make money by supplying grog to
Greytown or Carterton, and if these towns
still dream over the beer-pot their grog
should be imported via Napier or any other
unregenerate port. If all the sea-ports take
to prohibition then the inland beer districts
should make their own grog.
» * ♦ *
This paper strongly desires to see Maori-
land, or some other place, adopt a complete
and logical system of National Prohibition,
and keep at it for 50 years at least. It would
be about the most interesting social experi-
ment of modern days. It would decide
whether Cold Tea is a short road to the
millennium ; whether drink is really the
cause of crime, insanity, physical degeneracy,
curvature of the spine, and countless other
woes ; whether poetry is inspired by drink or
whether it is because poets drink that
their verses aren’t better ; whether whisky
helps or retards literature ; whether a tee-
total nation is a joyless one, which goes to
bed in profound gloom at 9.30, or whether
it is really brighter and happier than the one
which wakes up with a swelled head at
noon ; and many other things. Advocates
on both sides have been declaring for more
years than anyone can count that these
things are so ; also, that these things are not
so ; also, that they are partly so and partly
otherwise. Fifty years of absolute and
logical Prohibition, practised by a strong,
intelligent, and geographically-isolated com-
munity like the Maorilanders would settle
the above questions as they never can be
settled by a million tons of literature and a
million years of argument. The Bulletin
wants to try this great experiment on the
dog, and Maoriland looks the most promis-
ing dog to try it on.
The Early Closing Question.
It is time N.S.W. Supreme Court was spoken to
seriously. It seems to be following in the steps
of the tI.S. Federal Supreme Court, which, what-
ever it may be theoretically, seems to practically
exist only for the purpose of thwarting the
people’s will when the nation makes laws to com-
pel the rich to regard the lives and feelings of
their workers, or attempts to make the plutocrats
pay some fair proportion of national expenses.
Not long ago NtS.W. Supreme Court looked as
though it was going to wreck the Factories Act in
the case where a Sydney firm was prosecuted for
some breach of the regulations. In the recent
case the Labor Dept, against a certain druggist re-
ferred to by Brigham Brown (B. 12/5/’00) the
hair-splitting geniuses who presided at the Court
dealt the Early Closing Act a severe blow. Until
that decision the fact that a shop was open after
hours was a misdemeanor, and the casual police-
man along the “baate” was quite equal to the
maintenance of the law. Now that the Supreme
Court has succeeded in showing “ the fallibility of
words,” the four or five thousand small shop-
keepers of Sydney may open their doors and light
up their shops, and in order to get a conviction
the Crown will have to prove an actual sale, and,
presumably, to satisfy the court that the stuff was
actually paid for—or else it will be gravely an-
nounced that it is not proven that the quarter of
butter and tin of sardines were not meant for a
birthday-gift. To prove this will need the em-
ployment of an army of inspectors, and increase
the cost of government to the whole country in
order to cope with a few thousand possible
offenders in the metropolis. And all that
a few lawyers, who know perfectly well
what Parliament meant when it passed the Bill,
may prove how marvellously they can twist legal
barriers aside. Even if there is a huge army of
inspectors employed, the clumsy decision still
leaves a big gap of escape for the rapacious and
unscrupulous chafferer whose chief end in life is to
drive the law-abiding shopkeeper on the opposite
comer into the Benevolent Asylum. Brigham
Brown argues his case with a plausibility worthy
of a better cause. To doubt the utility of doing
good by legislation for a class which cannot obtain its
relief “by other means” is to fly in the face of
history and experience. What is the good of pro-
tecting by legislation a class that can protect itself
without legislative interference ? We don’t em-
ploy policemen to protect the flagship lying in
Farm Cove; we pay them to protect the people
who cannot protect themselves. The factory sys-
tem of England oppressed, with a hideous servitude
unequalled in history, thousands of children. The
babes were absolutely unable to “revolus'ie”—they
could only die. And they perished by thousands,
and could not alleviate their lot till legislation
alleviated it at a cost of words and ink only. The
shop-hands of Sydney were practically unable to
effect a combination sufficiently powerful to close
up the shops that insisted on selling by day and
by night. To have obtained the reform by “ other
means ” would have meant strikes, lock-outs, and
misery. Legislation did it with a pen-stroke.
Other means ” consist of strikes, or terrorism, or
both—and both are antiquated weapons. The
worker’s modern weapon is the ballot-box. His-
tory shows only too plainly that no privileged
class ever gives up any privilege without a
struggle, and the bourgeois class sticks like wax
to its right to work its helot to death.
* * * •
The Early-Closing Act does not sacrifice the
Thin Man to the Fat. The Attenuated one is at
the mercy of the Adipose right through the piece.
It is the old story of the Briton and Boer over
again. The small man who rents a house with a
shop in front, and in which he works_ when he can
get noticing better to do, and which is run by the
“missus and kids” when he can find other em-
ployment, is the Boer of trade. And the Britons
under “ Bobs ” Foy, Kitchener Hordern and
Redvers Hatte have him at their mercy all
the time. They buy in huge quantities and
cheaper, and can sell cheaper and deliver the stuff
in a nice brightly-painted van, which tickles the
vanity of the large-fronted lydy, who dearly loves
to see a bright equipage at the front door, even if
it is only the debt-collector’s sulky or the big
drapery delivery-cart. The poor Boer shopkeeper
can only hold the trade of a neighborhood till the
bulky one thinks it worth his while to open a
branch alongside and undersell him. Many of
the better class of suburban shops are merely
branches of the big city businesses. I hey stick
a trusted employe as manager, paint his name over
the door, do a thriving busin ss on weekly credit,
and the poor little Boer over the way dwindles
away and gradually dies. He only lives on suffer-
ance, and, as soon as it is really worth the big
man’s while, up the spout goes the Boer. Judg-
ing by the immense growth of the Universal Pro-
videry Emporiums in later years, the small shop-
keeper is going to be as totally extinguished as
the maker of boots-to-order. The small shop may
go on, but it will be run by men who are merely
paid servants of the emporiums, or simply drift
into the business and drift out again in a year or
two.
• m • •
The argument that a man has a right to keep
his place open all night if he works it with his
own family sounds well. But no man has a right
to enslave himself or his family. Both rights
have been abolished ever since the dark ages.
Theoretically it would be best to have a legal
limit to the hours of labor and the rate of interest
and many other tilings. But as long as an em-
ployer can play the trump oard of unlimited com-
petition men who are down on their luck will
permit themselves to be sweated. It would entail
enormous expense to safeguard the interests of
those who obeyed the law, and it is only by abso-
lute uniformity that each member of a trade can
be guaranteed that his competitors will not be able
to take advantage of his respect for the law. Ex-
ceptions are dangerous things, as Sydney has
found out by past experience. When there was
an Early-closing Act in force before there was
some plausible-looking exception by which each
municipality could vote itself right out of the Act,
or refuse by vote to have it applied to their par-
ticular municipal territory. And the man on the
east side of the street was in an early-closing dis-
trict while the man on the west drove a thriving
trade as long as people were walking about. Re-
sult, the law became obsolete.
* * * •
This is the age of big things. All over the
world the big emporium and huge manufacturer
with acres of factories have killed off the small
vendor and mechanic whose factory was his front
room. And perhaps it is best after all; for
socialism ever marches on, and when the colossean
capitalist has systematised everything the time
will be ripe for the State to take over the whole
system of production and distribution. Mean-
while the legislator has to devise a scheme by
which, in the face of the continual sup-
planting of men by machinery in production
and organisation, and system in distribu-
tion, every man shall at least have an opportunity
to work. And, apparently, the only way it can
be done is not by making exceptions to the laws
relating to the limitation of the hours of labor in
favor of the skeleton man, but by reducing the
hours still more and concentrating the country’s
business into a limited space of time, so that it
will need the services of every able-bodied man in
the community to cope with the accelerated speed.
Every community must buy a certain quantity of
food and clothing, and if it can only buy it within
six hours of the 24 the people who sell will have to
employ more hands to sell it in six hours than
would be necessary if they could keep going for 16
hours of the 24. Therefore, let every man who
calls himself a democrat work for cast-.ro i Pro-
tection against the outside world, minimum wage,
and reduced hours of- labor, and then Australia
will indeed be a working man’s Paradise.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Corporal Ben Harkus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The deeds men do of choice
Unurged by taunt or suasion
Of an all-pervading fashion
Craze or fever, true proclaim
Their heart’s desire,
And to the world their truer selves.
Ben Harkus, soldier, champion
Athlete at the games of war,
To England went to joust
And tourney, and in peace
To learn the arts of war.
Returning home,
The call to arms found Harkus
Unprepared to fight for England.
_ Erom his own land stronger
Voices called, and these Ben Harkus—
First a man, and then whatever
Might be—answered.
\V ife and children needed Harkus’ care
And Harkus’ love.
The cry of these was more to him
To answer than to satisfy
The bid for fame and notice of
Some man of State who offered
For the blood-thirst of rapacious war
Free lives and souls not his, and '
Strutted in his pride at
Having first conceived
A “splendid ” deed.
So Harkus home came.
And the crowd, that crowd that
Never fights but jeers and urges on
Others to the fray,
At Harkus jeered, and sent
White feathers.
And the heart of Harkus,
Brave and true, rebelled
That men should call him
Coward.
The first had Harkus been to fight
For hearth and home,
Became he loved them so.
But hearth and home were safe ;
And safe through all the vears
While soldier Harkus stood
Beside to guard them.
So his truer self said :
“ Stay ! ”
But everywhere the war-cry grew
No cry, of reason wanting more.
So Harkus, for his children’s sake /


That Judicial Intrigue.
Little wonder that Kingston characterised S.A.
Chief Justice Way’s back-door conspiracy
directed against clause 74 of the Commonwealth
Bill as an “extraordinary, extra-judicial and
secret intrigue.” Chief Justice Way has now
handed his “letter” (that is hit description of the
document) to the jpress, and it turns out to have
been a 12-page printed pamphlet, marked “ Con-
fidential ” and sent, without doubt, to wherever
in England it was likely to do most harm to Aus-
tralian Nationalism. It is a new thing for a Chief
Justice, appointed and paid to interpret and
administer the law, to attempt to dic-
tate how such law is to be framed. The
greasy little Chief Justice has the effrontery
to reflect on the manner in which the Federal
Conference settled Clause 74 and says that “the
abolition of the appeal to the Privy Council has
not had the support of any widespread expression
of public opinion in Australia. The majority of
S. J. Way’s judgments “have not had the sup-
port,” &c., but that doesn’t carry the inference
that he is an incompetent Judge. The delegates
who agreed to Clause 74 were elected by the
people (which S. J. Way wasn’t; for he was
appointed Chief Justice over the heads of
the other Judges in 1870 by the Ministry
of which he himself was Attorney-General)
and their work was endorsed by the people at re-
ferendum. In his “ confidential ” letter Mr. VV ay
suggests a now clause in place of clause 74, and
tliis seems to be the very amendment now made by
the Imperial Government, viz., to leave it to the
choice of litigants whether they they shall appeal
to the Federal High Court or to the Privy Council.
Way seems to have been eminently successful as a
conspirator. We have now a clue to the persistent
opposition of the London Times. Did Way send
a copy of his 12-page pamphlet to Miss Flow a
Shaw, the charming Jameson Raid lady who pens
editorials for that paper, and does the petticoat-
government of this bleeding country ? r l his is
necessarily mere speculation, as the S.A. Guy
Fawkes doesn’t take the public into his confidence
as to who are his fellow-conspirators. There is
one consolation, after all—Mr. S. J. Way will
never be chosen to represent the Australian Com-
monwealth on the Imperial judicial bench.
Effective Voting.
Adelaide’s lively septuagenarian, Miss C. H.
Spence, has been explaining her life-hobby of
effective voting to a Sydney audience. The Bul-
letin also has explained effective voting several
times ; but as Miss Spence lias been explaining for
41 years, perhaps one more explanation will not
do harm. The ideal of democratic government is
that every adult citizen should have a voice in the
government —based on the belief that a man called
upon to obey laws has a right to share in making
the laws. So, in so-called democratic communi-
ties like the Australian provinces, every man is
supposed to have a vote which he can use to elect
a representative in Parliament. And, where
there is a poll for representatives, the representa-
tive of the majority of voters is supposed to be
elected. As a matter of fact, throughout Aus-
tralia at the present time, not every man has
a vote, and not every representative is elected
by a majority of voters. Because, when there
are several candidates, the votes are often split so
that the successful candidate represents only a
minority, and the majority go unrepresented. It
is the fashion to call a member of Parliament the
representative,” but when there are many voters
who have had no share in electing him—who even
prefer that somebody else should oe elected then
he plainly does not represent them and t/ieir
opinions. To-day, in N.S. Wales, some 21M s.L. A.
are elected by minorities, with the practical
result that the defeated majorities (who split
their votes) are disfranchised, and have no voice
in the Government. It is more or less the same
with every other Australian province ; since m
Tasmania their rather cumbrous system of effect-
ive voting extends only to two towns, and in
Queensland the modifications are so peculiar that
a minority representative may still be elected.
Effective voting changes all that. It provides
that the man whom, on the whole, most ot
the electors prefer to represent them, goes ,
to Parliament. In this way. You have a one-
member constituency which polls, say, 1500 votes
in electing one of, say, three candidates Bbown,
Jones, and Robinson. When you get your
ballot-paper with three names on it, instead of
crossing two names out, you write 1,2, 3, against
the names in the order of your preference. If you
want Brown to get in, you vote for him—l. it
you can’t have Brown, and prefer Jones to
Robinson, then you vote Jones— 2, Robinson 3.
Well, a majority of 1500 votes is 751. If. any
candidate gets 751 votes or more, straight-
away, he is elected, and the majority is
represented. If no candidate gets 751 votes
straight away, then the second preferences re
counted, and the candidate who gets most firat
and second votes— i.e., who has most I s and 2 s
opposite his name on the ballot-papers is elected
andthe majority is represented, lhat is simple
enough. And that is effective voting as it would
apply to secure majonty : representation in the
single-member constituencies of JN.o. vv.
“Social Purity” and the Judicial Bench.
• « Brehon ” writes from Melbourne :
The vacancy in Vic. County Court Bench has given rise
to an outbreak of pustular purity among certain members
“A *he local legal profession. Some ostentatiously God-
fiarinsr aspirants for the vacant position, even, are said
i ‘ afflicted almost to tears at the prospect of certain
fAthariorotic personages having better chances than
themselves Not, of course, because they are rivals, but
because they are Lotharios.
There is much in this crusade that savors of cant and
smells of THOMAS-rot. While there can be no doubt of
the duty to blockade judicial candidates who are likely
to prove incompetent, unfair or corrupt; and while the
moral character of Judges, so far as infringements of the
criminal law, even on the under-16 platform, are concerned,
may be taken into consideration ; there is little doubt that
the application of standxrds of non-legal morality as
a criterion of judicial fitness will ultimately result in the
community being deprived of the services of some of its
best judicial minds, and even to the application of other
non-legal standards such as those of religion, art, science,
and philosophy, or membership or non-membership of
secret societies. It makes one sick to see tlie public go
raving mad with “purity” when a capab’e man with a
weakness for more than one woman as t .Ves to a public
position, and to be utterly and pathetically indifferent
when some “ sweater ” enemy of the human race, or well-
known sycophant for Imperial— i.e., anti-Australian
“dignities” is angling for popular honors or judicial
positions. . ~
The consideration of the sex question seems inseparably
connected with hysterics. It was these hysterics that
led the Irish to commit the one un-Irish crime of their
history—the deposition of Parnell —for the punishment
of which, by the way, H late speech of Salisbury indi-
cates that Karma is on thp job. And if the atavistic
Puritanism of a section of the Vic. bar can have its way,
in the very much smaller sphere of the Vic. County
courts, these unbalancing hysterics will deprive the
already-weak bench there of the best available talent.
Moreover, it is highly undesirable in the public interests
that A, who has a private personal grievance against B
should be encouraged to rake up before Minister C all the
blue stories floating about concerning B, as reasons why
the public should not have the benefit of the superior
learning and judicial ability of B. This is just the kind
of thing which such a crusade as that now going on m
Melb. will tend to make painfully frequent in Victoria.

Concerning Liquor Legislation.
As the result of recent disclosures by Mr. Percy
Wilkinson and Prof. Blackett the Victorian
Govt, will, it is hoped, pass an amendment of the
Health Act prohibiting the use of salicylic acid
and other anti-ferments' by vignerons jand wine
merchants. At the present time fully 45 per cent,
of the Victorian wine on the market is much
adulterated with acids, from which European wine-
drinkers are protected by stringent legislation.
To an intelligent Frenchman or German, accus-
tomod to drink light wines and lager biers with
the oomforting conviction that a paternal Govern-
ment has guaranteed their wholesomeness, the
tricks of the Victorian trade must appear as
further proofs of the stupidity of the Anglo-Saxon
race in matters pertaining to its unenlightened
stomach. Under the Union Jack dyspepsia
flourishes abominably for the reason that the
“children of the Empire” eat and drink without
thought for their internal arrangements. Victorian
wine is “readied up” with salicylic acid simply
because nobody protests against the outrage. The
same thing would be done with new vintages in
Europe hut for public insistence on a careful State
supervision of wine manufacture. It is not only
that the Law exacts a heavy penalty from
the sinners convicted of putting anti-ferments
into grape-juice. Health laws avail little where
the people take little or no trouble to compel the
Government to enforce them. In the. case of the
Victorian wine scandal the authorities may bo
expected to show a little less indifference than
usual, seeing that an important national industry
must depend for its success upon a stern enforce-
ment of the Act, but all the same the local con-
sumer, who has selfish motives for hoping that the
local product will soon be popular on its merits
both at home and abroad, will do well to take a
livelier interest in the liquor question. Vile
whisky is nominally an unlawful tiling, and Cus-
toms officers are supposed to take proceedings
against licensed victuallers who keep the perni-
cious stuff in stock, and the consumer ougnt to
want to know why the yellow-haired girl behind
the bar is allowed to sup «ly him with clumsily-
rectified methylated spirit in lieu of old Scotcn.
But the consumer takes the poison uncomplain-
ingly as though he were lined with zinc. Legis-
lation is thrown away upon him.
In the natural course of things the overwhelming
British forces are better led as time goes on,-also
the Boer letreats grow more and more wily. Each
side is discovering who’s who among it? leaders,
big and small, but as the larger army has by far
the larger number of officers who can make or
mar success within their spheres of administration
it stands to reason that Roberts increases his ad-
vantages by every fresh sifting of the wheat from
the chaff. Bennett Burleigh wrote, on March
6th :—“judging from the reports brought me, and
from the number of prominent officers now
‘ marking time ’ along the lines of communication,
there have been many ‘ found out ’ upon this side
as undeserving of the positions they occupied and
the trusts they were incompetent to discharge. 1
am satisfied that at least in one instaiice a
certain leader by his tactics allowed 2000 Boers
to slip free when they should have been caught to
a man. For that he and others have been
transformed into ‘ forwarding agents,’ directors
general of ‘bully beef and biscuits,’ instead of,
as they aspired to be, leaders of men in the field.
Gen. Kelly-Kenny remarked to me the other
day, ‘I have been wofully undeceived in many
things in this campaign. Men I had thought
were splendid fellows, who would have gone any-
where and done anything, have betrayed them-
selves as the rankest frauds, and those had ex-
pected little from have done great things. It is
not generally known—or not remembered, at any
rate—that a third, or more, of Cronje s crowd
escaped from the Paardeberg “trap’ five days
before the others surrendered. Commandant 1)e
Beers, with 2000 men on the best nags, made a
dash through the darkness and got away quite
comfortably. De Beers has been in another
/‘trap,” or two, since then, but he is still roaming
at large.
Behind Premier Seddon’s health visit to Rara-
tonga is said to lie a scheme for the establishment
of a South Pacific federation embracing Fiji,
Tonga and the Cook Islands with headquarters in
Maoriland. If there is anything in the rumor,
the idea is probably dictated by a desire on Sed-
don’s part to hold a few bluff cards should the
tariff of the Australian Commonwealth threaten
Maoriland’s commercial interests. Australia s
export trade to M.L. is hardly worth talking about,
but its exports to the South Sea Islands are con-
siderable, and Seddon doubtless thinks that if he
is in a position to tax the Commonwealth s Island
trade the Federal Parliament will hesitate ere it
excludes M.L. from free access to the Australian
markets. The idea of an island federation is not
bv any means new, as it was entertained at different
times and in different forms by Hori Grey and
Julius Vogel.

Judge Gibson, being assured by counsel for two
accused miners at Broken Hill that it had always
been a common practice for the men to take speci-
mens, and that nearly every house in the place
had more or less of a collection, said that in that
case he must pass such a sentence as would show
the workmen that there was no difference between
taking specimens and stealing ore. bo he sent
them “ up ” for two years.apiece. But the principle
is bad. When men do a thing which they know
is illegal, which they know is a crime, and for
which they know they will be punished if their
crime is discovered, and especially if that particu-
lar crime is growing, it may be a fair thing to pile
on the punishment. That is, if the light punish-
ment previously inflicted has not deterred, a Judge
may be justified in trying what a stiffer term will
do. But when, after years of official inaction,
during which a practice has established itself with-
out protest, and when the new hands see the
old hands do it without molestation, it is
obviously unfair (obviously, anyhow, to one who
doesn’t happen to be a judge) to give the first
chance victims a heavy sentence because of the
frequency of the crime. It isn’t surprising, then,
that these two particular miners are reported to be
getting a great deal of local sympathy, which has
already materialised into the form of a petition to
the Minister for Justice.
“ A. J.H.” (an Englishman) to The Bulletin :
“ England,” says Rev. Hugh Price-Hughes, “ is now
fighting against the slavery of the African race as truly as
the Northern States fought against it in the Oivil War.”
Also various other jingo parsons talk to the same effect.
On the Rand the “ boys ” get £4 10s. a month, while in
Rhodesia it is illegal to pay them more than 255. a month.
This is “ to prevent wealthy syndicates monopolizing the
labor supply.”
In ’97 the Chartered 00. required 100 boys for telephone-
line construction work in southern Rhodesia. The Native
Commissioner for the district notified the indunas, but
no boys offered. N.C. reported to Buluwayo and received
instructions from the Government to compel indunas to
supply boys in proportion to the strength of their kraals.
I saw the letter, and was present when one induna
(despite his protest that, through rinderpest and bad
seasons all his men were required to furnish food for the
kraal by hunting and fishing), was ordered to send in 20
boys by a certain day. The required number was in this
way secured, and received £1 per month, with 2Ubs. of
mealies (maize) and a little salt per day. At that time
20s. in Rhodesia was equal in purchasing power to about
12s. in Johannesburg, or to about 6s. in Sydney. I fear
that the Transvaal Kaffir, forced to work for the equiva-
lent of Is. 6d. per week, 100 miles from his home, while
his family are half-starved through want of the food he
should be procuring for them, will hardly appreciate the
“ glorious freedom under the British flag ” that the par-
sons are helping to thrust upon him.
We hear much from parsons about individual cases of
Boer brutality. I am perfectly certain that many of these
stories are absolutely false, and that 90 percent, of the
balance are greatly exaggerated. As for any genuine
ones—l can, f rom personal exoerienee, parallel them with
equal or worse cases of brutality on the part of Britishers.
Also, if the Boers treat Kaffirs so savagely, how is it that
their “ boys ” remain with them for years, on their farms
in the Transvaal, on hunting expeditions into the heart of
Africa, or while transport-riding in Bechuanaland and
Rhodesia, where the boy who wishes to leave his “ baas ’
has nothing to do but simply vanish during the night.
I know of many cases where boys have spent nearly their
whole lives in the service of the one Boer family, and have
usually found that two or three of a burgher’s boys (he
requires several to see to oxen and waggons) havo been
with him for some years.
I have no sympathy for maudlin Exeter Hall sentiment
concerning the “ poor heathen,” for I know that firmness,
even harshness, is necessary in dealing with them ; but
after such speeches as those of Engineer Hammond and
millionaire Rudd (8., 5/5/’(>0), who can imagine for a
moment that the Kaffir will pain anything by this war ?
And the jingo jiarson who cries out that this is a “war
for humanity ” is merely, in Chinese phrase, “ saving his
face ” while, forsaking Christianity, he bows down before
the great god Gold.
Mow many of the Ministers, how many even of
the Premiers, who have lately cabled to Chamber-
lain telling him not to leave off the war till he has
got the Boers right under his feet and the repub-
lics ended absolutely—how many of them could
give a fairly correct account of South African con-
ditions ? How many of them know jthe relative
proportions of Englishmen and Dutchmen in
South Africa? How many know, or havo a fairly
intelligent idea, of the feeling in the British S.
African colonies on the subject ? And, if they are
not well-informed on these subjects, what is their
advice worth ? Hardly to be wondered at, in view
of this obvious jumping at conclusions, that Cham-
berlain and his officers view our Commonwealth
Bill—the work of the same and other hands—with
so much suspicion and search it so thoroughly !

A Maoriland person named Haybittle or Egg-
bottle, or something like that, advances this idea
in a Wellington (M.L.) paper: —
Kindly allow me through the medium of your widely-
circulated paper to lay before the people of New Zealand
a plan for the perpetuation of the memories of those New
Zealand heroes who have fought, bled and died for Queen
and country on South African battlefields. My suggestion
is that all New Zealand newspapers or mayors open Is.
subscription lists for the purpose of erecting a marble
monument in Wellington to their memories. Should this
be responded to by every adult in New Zealand, as I
certainly think it would be, there should be sufficient to
erect four monuments, one in each of the principal centres.
Of course, the war is not over yet; but I think the end is
not far away. A considerable time would elapse before
the idea could be carried out, as details would have to be
procured, plans drawn and monuments designed I
would suggest that those who have died on the battlefield
should have their names engraved on the front of the
monument, and those who have died through sickness on
the side.
Now why in the name of wonder should those
who died of sickness be shunted away at the side
of the monument, as if they were inferior cattle ?
Fancy putting the name of James 111. of Scot-
land, who was killed in trying to got away from a
battlefield in record time before the fight had well
commenced, on the front of a monument, and
Wellington, Napoleon, Du Guesulin, Suwar-
row, Osman of Plevna and Washington on the
side ! Besides, a man isn’t always a hero because
he goe3 to war; many a man goes to war because
he doesn’t know what it is like, an 1 is very sorry
indeed when he gets there. And the wild demand
of Mr. Strawbottle for four monuments— four
monuments for a few dozen men out of a whole
force of a very few hundreds who went to war with
about the smallest Power on earth, and didn t
come back ! If Maoriland ever has a real war it
will take, at this rate, about 1700 big monuments
to commemorate her dead, and it wouldn’t be a
very great war even then.

complain that the methods of N.S.W. Works
Minister O’Sullivan fan strong prejudices against
it. Instead of one Minister going alone, the
Cabinet should have adopted the principle or sub-
mitted it to Parliament for discussion. At pre-
sent, certain members of the Cabinet speak dis-
paragingly of the innovation, and it is taken for
granted that Premier Lyne and Minister See are
directly hostile. Lyne and See are, at all events,
connected with private industrial occupations
where the principle is designated rank heresy.
The position, therefore, is that O’Sullivan seems
to be allowed to use the minimum-wage in his de-
partmsnt as bait to capture the Labor-party, but
if he fails to capture that party or a serious
political row ensues the minimum-wage will be
repudiated by the Govt. If the latter, the prin-
ciple will receive a set-back from which it will take
long to recoven
“ Ignotus ,” on Prayer :
Charged with praying for the dead, the Archbishop of
Canterbury replied that he only prayed for the faithful
dead ; and that, as we have a distinct promise that such
will be saved, we may pray for them, “ knowing that they
will be ” just as we pray “ Thy kingdom come,” knowing
that it will come. But then . . . Well, if it is unfair to
bet on a certainty . . . what is it to pray for a certainty ?
Suppose I pray that the offspring of my ewes, if any, may
be lambs, and not weasels. Would not Dr. Temple regard
this as indecent, and call me a profane jester ? May I not,
then, ask if he is serious? Perhaps the only answer is
that widespread psychic delusions occur, that they are
caused by bacilli, and that his Grace has got the bacillus
religiosus, rar.Christianus, and consequently cannot reason
or be reasoned with on the subject of his delusion. To
get away from religion : when people believed in witches,
it was useless to argue with them. Swarming with the
bacilli, women actually confessed to Satan as their para-
mour. Now that the witchcraft bacillus has died out, and
become extinct like the Dodo, we regard “ witches ” as
conscious impostors. The bacilli of the different religions
unfortunately affect so many that the sane minority have
to lie low. Otherwise, surely a man who draws £.15,000
a year as a kind of ambassador to Heaven, and, as part of
his duty, prays for what must necessarily happen (in his
belief) whether he prays or not, is guilty of obtaining
money under false pretences, and should get a month
without the option.

At the very moment when N.S.W. Govt, is buy-
ing back the Darling Harbor frontages—buying
back at a cost of millions what was sold for hun-
dreds only, two miles of deep-water frontage
parative song—comes a proposal to alienate for a
also in the heart of the harbor—doubtless to be
bought back for more millions by the next
generation ! And North Sydney Council
ass - concensus brays, “By all means. Cer-
tainly. Why not ?The transfer grab is being
engineered on behalf of the Milson family
of Crown grantees, who control no less than 75
acres of land at Cremorne Point, not far from
Circular Quay but on the opposite side of Sydney
Harbor. They offer to surrender about six acres
of land without a water frontage, worth some
few hundreds an acre, in exchange for over
ten acres of water-frontage land with a
prospective value of hundreds of thousands
of pounds! An interesting daily-papor corres-
pondent shows that the first grab at the water-
frontages was made in 1877 —and missed ; but the
Milsons have kept on grabbing at intervals ever
since—in the hope that some day a Govt, will
arise which can lie persuaded to negotiate the
exchange. This tenacity in pursuit of private
interests at the public expense is truly monumontal
—and the monument should be erected on
Cremorne point.
Pastor Jefferis, visiting from Adelaide, and
Speaker J. P. Abbott achieved an accentuated
difference in Pitt-street Congregational Church
(Sydney) one night last week on the question of
retaining the Privy Council right of appeal. Dr.
Jefferis whole-h ear tod ly championed the Aus-
tralian'High Court, while the Speaker argued for
the maintenance of tlie P.C. One new point and
a valuable one was made by the Adelaide parson
—namely, that it is against Australian national
policy for a Judge to sit in Parliament, and inas-
much as members of the judioial committee of the
P.C. may sit and vote.in the House of Lords, that
healthy principle is there disregarded while here
it is an article of political faith. It is a fact that
in the early days Australian Judges sat in Parlia-
ment, but special statutes were enacted disqualify-
ing them from Parliament while they retained the
office of J udge.
When the Stuatit-D alley Administration came
into power in N.SAV., Teece, M.L. A., thought
the democratic hour had struck, a,nd ho submitted
to the Assembly a motion declaring that fees for
elementary education in N.S.W. State schools
should be discontinued forthwith. The word
“forthwith” created trouble, and on its being
dropped the motion flew through with a majority
of 40 to 2G. The Administration thereupon pro-
mised to introduce the necessary legislation on the
first possible opportunity. But Ministerial jun-
keting and general prodigality drained the
Treasury in the early stage, and the Soudan war-
orgy dried it up in the latter, so the beggarly
threepennies wore still needed to keep the State
going. Since then Parliament after Parliament has
expressed itself favorable to the remission of
those fees, but Ministry after Ministry demands
the money. The House now sitting under Lynk
is as favorable to the remission as any of its pre-
decessors ; more so, for a motion such as Teeoe’s
will any week carry a two to one majority ; but
the Treasury yawns. Boer-baiting, like the
Soudan nigger-baiting, holds out a capacious,
rapacious paw, and plague and other miseries yell
for monetary comfort. Is, then, the discon-
tinuance of these scandalous fees to be indefinitely
postponed under the Lyne Govt, as under its
predecessors for seventeen years past ? Members
with the faith of the 46 to 26 should reply “ No.
Whatever way the Lyne Govt, provide for the
future (and there are many ways), this iniquitous
impost must be removed.
When Mommsen, the German historian, wrote in
the North American Review concerning the
situation in South Africa, Prof. Sonnenschein, of
Birmingham, wrote asking for the foundation of
the accusations made in that article. Mommsen
replied as follows :
Allow me to answer you in a few words. Withoiri
England not a voice is heard in defence of your South
African war. It is the case of Dreyfus directed against
England. Can you believe that this general disapproba-
tion is unfounded ? Many of your countrymen concur in
the opinion of the continent, but they must be silent:
right or wrong. “My country !” The Boer Government
may have given reasons for complaint; but that is not the
cause but the pretext of the war. Whosoever remembers
the history of the diamond fields and the annex-
ation of Kimberley, will find it impossible to take
Lord Salisbury’s assurance seriously : “We do not
desire extension of territory, we seek no goldfields.”
Jameson’s raid is now generally deprecated, but will you
dare to assert that, at any rate, a part of your Government
was not concerned in that shameful crime ? Will you dare
to assert that that crime was punished as it deserved by
Parliament and the Court of Justice ? You would wish to
distinguish between England and Cecil Rhodes and his
gang, but in vain. Can England claim any belief in her
good faith after these occurrences, and after the manner
in which these were officially treated ? Can you seriously
believe that a nation like the Boers could have desired to
carry on a war of conquest against the British Empire ?
They remembered only a century of injustice and crime,
and defended themselves of necessity—alas, in vain.
Henceforth the Dutch at the Cape will form a second
Ireland, and the avengers will arise sooner or later.
Believe me, every friend of England mourns over such
victories as these.

to be fulfilled right away. As a start Govt. i 3
procuring 80 acres at the Hutt, a suburb of Wel-
lington, and trains are to be run to suit, railway-

This reduction in workmen’s fares eomes into
operation in the four big cities—Wellington,
Auckland, Christchurch, and Dunedin—imme-
diately. The area of the suburban plots to be
offered will be from one to three acres. Three
acres and a cow !
That none might send the feather
Unto them in after years
As emblem of their father's state,
Rode out, his house full set
In order.
And soldier Harkus now is dead.
Nor in the rush of battle either,
But in throes and ravings
Of a fell disease that follows
War as vultures follow death.
And, strange it is,
The news comes tardy home
As if in Harkus’ death
Some men might see what
Those who ory for war would hide.
Sleep deep, Ben Harkus, sleep !
Men true do mourn thee as a martyr
To a curse.
Not true unto thyself thou wert,
But in thy very failure to maintain
The cause thy heart deemed right,
More truth is taught than
Hadst thou been so.
Sleep deep, Ben Harkus !
Sleep !
Thy monument sure no unveiling
Needs.

AthemiEum Hall War Biograph.
“East Lynne” is playing to pretty full
houses at the Criterion, and for those who like
this sort of amusement, the death scene wherein
Lady Isabel squirms on the floor in an unnaturally
seductive tea-gown is all that can be desired.
After the first act or two this stout lady seems to
subside on to the floor almost entirely, and spend
the most of her time on all-fours calling for her
cheeild and repenting of her sins. As she has only
one sin these remarks of hers sound somewhat
ostentatious. But as Archibald Carlyle also refers
to it in the plural number (in the scene where he
nobly forgives her for not understanding without
being told that he chased the other girl
merely as a lawyer and a brother), there may
be some good reason for this mysterious
multiplication. The curious difference between
the old and new literature on the gone-astray
woman is seen from the first act, where a common-
place scion of nobility nurses his gouty heritage of
dissipation while he calmly lets out that, owing to
his extravagance, Lady Isabel will never inherit a
penny. A modern playwright would harp back
on this disreputable old gentleman as the prime
cause of the after-trouble, as, according to the
book, he undoubtedly was, but in “Lynne,”
written by the estimable lady once described as
an “educated housemaid,” Lady Isabel takes her
one large sin on her own plump shoulders, without
any outside assistance whatever.
