The lachrymose drama at the Theatre
Royal is now well in its second week. This scribe
has referred to it as one of Brough’s best, and the
fact that the public don’t seem to take to it very
well is confirmatory evidence as to this writer’s
soundness of judgment. The public’s opinion has
to be taken upside-down, as it were, as a drama
which has neither a fallen woman nor the relief of
Ladysmith in it has no great literary value
in these present times. There is no fallen
woman in the “Tyranny”—only a fallen
teacup and an outraged "photograph. Also,
there is no impossible situation in the play,
except perhaps that of the girl that munches cake
during a proposal. Most girls find proposals
sweet enough to chew by themselves. But the
audience rises with a strong desire to hear more of
that girl and the home of the colossal rice-pudding
and the vasty mutton-stew. By the way, one small
slip in Act 2. Gentlemen in good society surely
don’t inform their host that they have had an ex-
cellent dinner. It is taken for granted that he will
not give them boiled horse.
** * *
“Oliver Twist,” an ancient play, so old as
to be practically new, was staged at Her Majesty’s
on Saturday night. It is redolent of the days
when men were hanged in public, and cockney
dialect consisted in using w’s for v’s, and thieving
was a profession, for which the Fagin light-finger
exercises were dutifully practised by all such as
were deemed worthy to graduate in that branch of
education.
*** - *
The Dickens’ drama wanders backwards
and forwards from the plaguey, dirty-looking
garrets where these scholastic courses are given, to
various street scenes in which the large London
population is always so remarkably absent. Also,
there is much unnecessary fuss over a good, but
rather imbecile boy, who fluctuates between the
unreal legs of stage-poverty and the black Sand-
ford and Merton costume in which he looks much
nicer and plumper than small good boys ought to
look. The pursuit of this uninteresting kid
through five acts, and the weary prologue-
exoneration of his unmarried parents, is light-
ened by some original love-scenes between a cer-
tain Nancy and her Bill, who are understood to
be married through their tendency to project the
furniture at one another.
* * » •
The only interesting items are when this
Bill wearily arises and stoushes his Nancy, com-
plaining bitterly when her impudence in daring
to eat or move in his presence compels him to
this duty. The play is badly strung together.
It doesn’t begin at the beginning, and it leaves
off before Sikes is caught. It leaves him coaxing
an invisible dog, and then moves abruptly off to
the prison cell of a certain Israelite who has been
arrested, tried and convicted in five minutes for
no very well explained reason.
* • * *
As Nancy, the American comes up to her
Elizabeth standard, which goes to prove that
Nance can do anything except frolic, and as there
are plenty of other artistes to act the giddy lamb,
this paper hopes Miss O’Neil will be warned and
never again be giddy except in a large and
majestic way. The slouch, and the skirt lolloping
into the heels, and the ungainly attitudes of
Nancy are all as they ought to be, and
even in the murder scene where she travels
along the floor on her battered face this
actress just manages to escape the grotesque. It
is a common Whitechapel murder, and for those
who like their murders without any frill it is just
right. Kingston does well as the Jew who is
hanged on general principles, and M‘Kee Rankin,
in his make-up as the world-famed Sikes, justified
the remark of a dress-circle spectator who evi-
dently didn’t remember the story and hadn’t
looked at the programme: “ He’s a regular Bill
Sikes sort of fellow, isn’t he?” It is certainly
oreditable when a man can be said to belong to the
same type as himself.
** * #
At the House of the Dreadful Curtain,
otherwise the Tivoli, the warble of Tom Browne,
the whistler, is heard no more. But the small
infant, Boy Cashiu, still holds on his triumphant
way in company with the Newsky Family, who
are rendering the songs and dances of all nations,
and Madame Cordelia, who is adroit enough to
always sing her best songs in response to an
encore. Her rendering of “ She was bred in Old
Kentucky ” is very charming, while the lady’s
dressing is a sartorial rebuke to those misguided
damsels who believe that beauty consists of a large
expanse of dull, white flesh. The latest arrival is
Miss Irene Franklin, whose speciality is said to
be a song of gestures. This scribe has not yet
looked upon the lady’s songful hands, but hopes
to do so next week.
** * *
At the Palace, that gorgeous house of
Arabian arches, Victor plays fantastic tricks with
an infant in a box, said infant appearing suddenly
from nowhere back beyant. He also talks in six
or more different voices while doing several other
things with his mouth, and performs a number of
other tricks more or less brilliantly or indifferently
according to the taste of the beholder, and con-
cludes each with the Babylonian gag, “Now, I’ll
show you how it’s done !”
* *
At the Lyceum, Charles Holloway opens
his 60th week with a revival of Walter Beynolds’
dreadful melodrama, “The Shamrock and the
Bose.” The edge of the dreadfulness is somewhat
blunted by the beautiful scenery, where moon-
light, glittering ocean, ruined abbeys and Sarda-
napalian dairies make up a combination of loveli-
ness, and which applied to the production of the
short story drama, such as the Broughs produce,
would be altogether charming. But the “Sham-
rock and the Bose ” is a drama that barks round
the four corners of impossibility at once, and is
all tangled up, and leaves the mind in a state of
chaos, through which whirl and flee Irish evic-
tions, wicked Irish land-agents, infamous mort-
gagees, gorgeous scenery, criminal courts held in
the front garden, sudden murder, loot, secret
societies, matrimony, and general imbecility.
Somewhere about 11 p.m. the audience dodders
into the street and don’t know where it are, and
when it finds out, all the pubs are closed and the
price of oysters raised to 9d. per small plate. The
undesirable character of blood-sucker Flint is
played for all it is worth by George Chalmers,
while Hugh Power as the billy-goat Englishman
who finds things in Ireland different to what
they are in England, and Miss Deorwyn who, as
Peggie O’Bafferty, comes to an untimely mar-
riage in the 4th act, do good work ; but the gem
of the show is the laugh of Mixy Mulcahy, alias
J- P. O’Neill, the jovial Irish informer, which far
outshines even the gorgeous scenery.

Donald M‘Dougall and his brethren in
kilts continue to battle for the Flag at Melbourne
Boyal. Also the pair of base conspirators persist
with their awful foolishness as regards the illegiti-
mate mother from Bussia, and the steam-hammer,
and the mihsing codicil of the gallant geezer who
gets murdered in the first act. At time of
writing a large force of dauntless patriots in the
stock-broking line were under orders to enjoy
a great Demonstration at the Boyal on the
night when Mafeking got relieved by cablegram,
but, owing to a forward movement in The Bulle-
tin’s publishing arrangements this paragraph can
give no actual details of the event. All the same,
it feels certain that the ardent brokers sang
“Buießritannia” with unfailing energy, and were
“heard to advantage” in a new song entitled
1 Marching with Bobs to Pretoria.” These things
were promised, and nothing but an earthquake
could have prevented the wet function from being
duly carried out.

Queen’s Birthday is also accountable for
The Bulletin’s bare mention of the fact that
Pinafore” was produced at Her Majesty’s (late
Alexandra) on Saturday night. “ Pinafore ”is an
old favorite which carries its own recommendation,
anyhow. Moreover, Kenningham, Brownlow,
Harry Shine, Bathurst, Miss Carrie Moore,
and Miss Vane are guarantees of the William-
soman management’s good faith. “Pinafore”
will reign at the renovated theatre this week, what
time the smell of newness and fresh paint is wear-
ing off. Next Saturday (26th), “Pirates of
Penzance ” will come along, and a week later Miss
Nance O’Neil is to mak£ her first appearance in
Melbourne.

appalling Queen Victoria countenance will cele-
brate an S2nd birthday to-morrow (24th), when the
reverent admirers of that famous British institution
will be expected to sing their Anthem with par-
ticular earnestness. And why not ? Old age is a
thing to be encouraged in all persons, queens, or
otherwise.

Benno Scherek’s Orchestra, which earned

new series of Saturday matinee concerts at the
Town Hall on the 26th—next Saturday. Miss
Una Bourne will re-appear with the Orchestra
and play the pianoforte share in Rubenstein’s D.
Minor Concerts, and the prices will assume the
businesslike proportions of “ three, two, and
one.”

“ Steff ” : Lecturer Charles Clark did not do quite so
well in M.L. this time as formerly. In Wellington he ran
a concert with the assistance of vocalists of note. One
Mrs. Howie, is about the most Junoesque contralto alive.’
She makes the stage look like a candle-box, and the piano
like a typewriter. Amongst other little morsels she sang
“Ma Curly-headed Babby” and I devoutly thanked the
great Joss of Earthquakes, that I wasn’t her curly-headed
babby. Anyhow, I enjoyed Rev. Haskett Smith better
than Clark. Haskett started by turning the gas out, and
in this subdued light I fell into a delicious slumber. And
next day I told my friends that it was a beautiful enter-
tainment with such awe-inspiring classic pictures When
Haweis was round this way, he drew poor houses’, and he
used to stand on his longer leg for quite five minutes,
scowling at the empty benches before he started his
lecture. Most of his musical jokes fell as dead as frozen-
mutton, and I am sure the recollections of the Maoriland
audiences hastened the little man’s death.
One prominent, popular, and experienced theatrical
manager here views with exceeding apprehension the
growing interest of American firms in the Australian field
for tragedy, comedy, history,” &c., &c., and thinks
local dramatic enterprise will have sad occasion to regret
the Musgrove-“ ’Murkan ” combine. Practically all the
Australian theatres are available for a scoop, and the
Yank is going in “ big licks ” in this branch of business as
in all others, and invokes a “trust” to dish up drama,
legitimate and bastard ; opera, comic and solemn * bur-
lesque, farce, and the tommy-rot of the varieties to an
omnivorous people. The above prophet foresees the early
day when dramatic art will be dealt out to Australia in
job lots by a “ shmdykit.”

Jefferson made his first success as a star actor in Aus-
tralia. His former essay in this line at ’Frisco was a

The success of Australian actresses in London is no new
..thing. Mrs. Chas. Young, who married Mr. Herman
Vezin, and Julia Matthews did well in the old country in
the early sixties. Miss Cleveland, though of English
training, established her reputation in Australia, and
after the death of her first husband, Vincent, she wedded
a leading English actor.
G. V. Brooke, who went down in the ill-fated s.s. Lon-
don in the Bay of Biscay, always had a horror of being
drowned at sea. Among the papers left behind in Eng-
land was a poem of some 70 lines on the gruesome subject,
written by himself. lie behaved heroically during the
disaster.

Concerning Nigger King, a Bulletin cor-
respondent writes :
“Professor" William King, the notorious nigger con-
vict, was released from Pentridge a month ago, and is
now back in gaol awaiting trial on two charges of auda-
cious burglary and one of attempted rape. King, 10
years ago, was only a tenth-rate pug., but on his dis-
charge he threw out challenges to all the top-notchers,
and was billed to fight Fred Preston on Saturday night.
The d’s, however, put him in on Tuesday night, and,
according to indications, the fight with Preston won’t
come off for at least 10 years yet. Chiefly owing to
well-written and pathetic letters' of ill-treatment which
were smuggled out of gaol, Marshall Lyle and others
persistently agitated for King’s release-for the past
six years of his incarceration were due to alleged miscon-
duct in gaol. The penal authorities having once declined
to interfere with the sentence, would not back-down;
but, on the recent change of Government, King was re-
leased on a special order, as a way out of a dilficult situa-
tion. King’s latest crimes show him to be a man who
should never be at liberty again. lie is obviously a
criminal lunatic of the most dangerous type, and the
question as to whether his character was not developed
by the yearn of solitary confinement he underwent, will
have yet to be answered. King’s letters from gaol
eternally reiterated the fact that lie was an American
citizen. He is lucky not to be in the Southern States
with the present charges against him.
The American rolled gold jewellery that
Mick Simmons is offering to the Australian public
is a revelation in prices and beauty. The hand-
some alborts, sleeve-links, collar-studs, ladies’
bangles, necklets, rings, pins, &c., &c., wear equal
to best gold and are guaranteed for five years.
Their cost is within easy reach of everybody.
Mick Simmons is making a speciality of clocks
and watches, too, at a price that beats everything
in the southern hemisphere ; and he has a stock of
fancy goods, ladies’ purses, bags, photo, frames,
dolls, toys, haberdashery and stationery, that is
attracting the public from near and far and boom-
ing business even in these dull times.*
Concerning last Saturday’s S.A. Legisla-
tive Council elections: —
S.A. Leg. Council elections practically settled for some
time that the Fat Man majority in S.A. Upper House is to
be maintained. At present (apart from the result of the
election) in a Chamber of 24, the Tories have a solid
majority of three (not counting President Baker) and that
majority has succeeded in blocking Liberal measures for
the last five years. Awhile ago the S.A. Tories
used to spend their money in trying to control the local
Assembly, but the attempt was unsuccessful and expen-
sive, and it was soon found that progressive legislation
could be blocked far more easily by maintaining a majority
in the Council. The National' Ass., therefore, never
worries about the Assembly now, but spends its cash over
the periodical Council elections, and so far, it must be
admitted, with great success. The heavy property fran-
chise (£25) shuts out the great majority of metropolitan
voters, and the provincial electors, being mainly Conser-
vative, favor Tory nominees. Kingston, when' Premier,
long since recognised that the only way to break up the
Council Ass. majority was by liberalising the franchise,
hence his Household Suffrage proposals, and he spent the
last three years of his Ministerial life in trying to get
such a measure through. His Bill was twice carried in
S.A. Assembly, by large majorities, only to be entombed
in the Council. The latter body even rejected the Holder
Govt.’s compromise of a £l5 franchise. This election had
long been looked forward to by the Progressives as pro-
viding an opportunity of at least reducing the Tory
majority. When Kingston resigned his Assembly
seat and announced his candidature for the Coun-
cil on the test question of suffrage reform, select-
ing J. L. Stirling, the Tory leader, for his opponent,
it was expected that the battle all over S.A. would be
furious, but the absence of the Democratic leader in Eng-
land made all the difference, as he has been beaten “ out
of a place,” Stirling and Gordon securing the coveted
seats, while the National Ass nominees, Lucas and
Pascoe, beat the Govt, candidates, Moody and Russell,
nearly two to one. For the Central Division, though
Yardon (Opposition) headed the poll, Labor candidate
Kirkpatrick won the second seat by a small majority over
Parsons, the other Opposition candidate, who, in turn,
just “pipped” Robinson (Labor). For the Northern
Division, Bice (Ministerial) and Duncan (Opposition) were
uccessful The result of the contest thus still leaves as
small Tory majority in S.A. Leg. Council.

• • * *
The Great Boulder Co., Kalgoorlie, have just
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.,
Ltd., South Melbourne, to build a pair of compound con-
densing engines of 700 h.p. to drive their extensive mil-
ling plant. These engines will be of the latest type, with
Proell valve gear, The flywheel is 20ft. in diameter,
grooved for 16 ropes, and weighs upwards of 22 tons. The
total weight of engine will be 85 tons. The engines are to
be completed by the end of the year and will be the finest
and largest plant on the field. Three engines under con-
struction at the Otis Works for the Kalgoorlie goldfields
have an aggregate of 1800 horse power.*

Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, Mining Machinery
Merchants, 606 Little Oollins-street, Melbourne, report
that the Luhrig system of concentration is gradually es-
tablishing itself in the principal mills of Australia. The
latest Luhrig vanner has a large capacity, and owing to
important improvements the life of the belt, Ac., is greatly
increased. Vanners and spare parts are stocked in Melb.
and prompt delivery is thus assured. A small testing
plant is available hear Melbourne. Upon receipt of
necessary data, estimates for complete plants, including
batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the

works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, andallsortsof iron-work.*

Dear'BuLLETiN, —In the interests of those who
have labor to sell over on your side, let me draw attention
to the fact that the Chillagoe Railway and Mines Co. is
advertising abroad for miners and other laborers, notwith-
standing that there are enough men available on the field
to supply the mines twice over. It is pitiful to see men
arriving here every day without a shilling in their pockets
and no work to be got. There is no wage-work here—all
contract, and that under most unfavorable conditions.
Also, considering the prices you have to pay for every-
thing here and the way you have to rough it, 7s. 6d. in
Victoria or N.S.W. is as good as 12s. here.—A Miner, Mun-
gana (Q.). , # .

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
What marvellous strides copper-production has
made ! The total world’s yield for the first decade of this
century was 91,000 tons ; basing the estimate for 1900 on
last year’s figures, the output in the last decade will be
8,613,000 tons. That is, as much is won in nine days now
as was won in a whole year at the beginning of the cen-
tury. In each of two decades the production has almost
doubled itself. The increase in this present decade has
been just normal; in its predecessor it was more than
that. In prices there have been remarkable variations—
from an average of £6O in the first decade to £l3O in the
second ; down as low as £BB in the fifth, and then up to
£lll in the Crimean period. But from that point (’sl—
there has been a steady fall this way:—£lll, £B7,
£79, £6O, £52. In that time production has gone up from
505,999 tons to 3,643,000. From all of which it is arguable
that we need not get so desperately fearful of a mighty
crash in the market on the opening up of enough mines
in this country to add (say) 5 per cent, to the world’s out-
put. And we have not reached anything like that figure
yet. Australasia’s whole contribution, in fact, has
amounted to 4£ per cent, of the total in the current ten-
year period.
And there is more consolation. Copper-mines
are not, it seems from the century’s experience, long-
lived. Most of them go off in 25 years or so, and thus
new discoveries are constantly called for. For instance,
the English mines have yielded 5000 tons in the past ten
years; yet between ’5l and ’6O they turned out 142,000
tons. Now, if other countries were making copper dis-
coveries at the rate Australasia has been doing the out-
look for prices would be bad. But, though we only give
4J per cent, of the world’s supply, we produced in ’99
2750 tons more than in ’9B, and that is 7 per cent, of the
total increase.
It is now quite evident that the Amalgamated
Copper 00., which controls the American group of copper
co’s. (Amerioan production is 12 times greater than
Australasian), is not after rocket business. The “ com-
bine” is backed by Standard Oil 00., and apparently the
same end is to be sought as that huge concern has kept
after —the maintenance of permanently remunerative
prices, with no attempt at manipulation for speculative
purposes. Which means that it has come to stay.
Mt. Lyell records to 31st March last : —Number
of tons of ore treated, 610,659; average assay-value-
copper 3'52 per cent, silver 3’470z., gold 0'1270z. Pro-
ducts : copper, 20,353 tons; silver, 1,968,6170 z. ; gold,
79,9470 z.
The directors of Mt. Lyell Co., in their half-
yearly report, state that “ the copper market has again
been’characterised by high prices, and from all sources
the evidence points to a maintenance of the present satis-
factory position.” On such authority this ought to be
cheering information for owners of copper mines generally.
The new arrangement for the sale of the Mt.
Lyell product is stated by the directors to be that the
Baltimore 00. simplv receives a fixed payment per ton of
blister copper for the separation of the metals contained
and returns to the co. the entire contents in refined
copper and silver, which are sold through a leading N.Y.
house. The gold is also returned in full and is sold to the
U.S. Mint at its full value. The contract, which is des-
cribed as a verv advantageous one for the co. compared
with the old ‘arrangement, is for three years. It cer-
tainly seems to be a common-sense commercial arrange-
ment. The refining co. get definite payment for specific
work. Still the refiners must make a good profit, and it
may be asked why does not the Mt. Lyell Co. erect a re-
finery and retain this profit ?
Manager Sticht, of Mt. Lyell, is a man of
extreme caution, and a positive statement from him may
be relied upon. In his half-yearly report, written at the
end of last Sept., he was specially guarded as to the pros-
pects of the mine at a depth—so much so, indeed, that
people not knowing his temperament might have been
excused for assuming that he had serious doubts on the
subject. In his last report, however, there is no sugges-
tion of doubt; he commits himself to the statement that
the developments in No. 6 level give positive proof of the
existence of high-grade ore in the lowest depths of the
bod .V- ,
The cost of producing blister copper at Mt.
Lyell per ton of ore, is £1 35., made up as follows :
Mining, 2s. lid. ; removal of overburden, 2s. ; smelting,
15s. lOd. ; converting, 2s. 2d.

Memo, for gentlemen who write Copper Co.
prospectuses and booming reports after the co. s are
floated (the extract is from the annual report of the big
metal firm of Aron Hirsch and Sohn, whose Australian
buvings are now probably bigger than those of any other
firro) it is to be noted that mines containing a very
high percentage of copper very seldom turn out profit-
ably In the best paying mines like Rio Tinto, Mt.
Lvell Lake Mansfield, and others, the ores run only 21
to 5 per cent, copper. ” Interesting, by the way, to compare
Rio Tinto, Europe’s mammoth, with our own Lyell. Its
year’s profits were £1,515,000. Quantity of pyrites treated
was 1,649,844 tons ; the average copper contents, 2-72 per
cent. * * *
Blayney mine maintaining a steady output of
about 25 tons of copper per week from one blast-furnace.
Curing the last month 100 tons have been sold. At the
ruling price of copper there ought to be a large profit on
this output. Another 120-ton furnace to be erected at
once.
Broken Hill Junction shareholders now get
officially the whole truth, which they have had to look
elsewhere for in the past. Fact was that it couldn t be
hidden any longer, even if the new board and new general
manager were inclined to hide it, which they aren t. r or
there has to be a reconstruction ; and shareholders could
not very well be asked to hand over ss. a share without
bein" told why. To say that past management was
execrable is to say nothing fresh here ; what ought in
fairness to be said is that the manager (who, as usual, has
been the sacrificial goat) was not wholly to blame—he had
to mismanage in order to meet the demand for diva, quick
and lively. The co.’s profit for the half-year was £725 ;
at any rate, the directors say so, but as they do not seam to
have made any deduction for wear and tear the true profit
was half of nothing. Worse, instead of gettingbetter things
are going the other way. The average of the ore handled
has been 14'4 per cent, lead, lOoz. silver, and 10'5 per
cent, zinc; but taking the ore for the last eight weeks
only, the average was 13‘4 per cent, lead, 10'6oz. silver,
and 9 7 per cent zinc. The manager declares he cannot
do economical work unless he has close upon £15,000 to
jpend in straightening up old workings, extending new
ones, and providing plant for re-treating portion of the
present product. The directors will therefore try and
raise this money by offering for each present share
another one at ss. ; the remaining £lO,OOO (there are
100,000 shares now) will be used to wipe off the long-
standing debt to the bank. It is a humiliating position
for a co. which only lately was paying £60,000 a year, but
it has to be faced, and the board’s proposals seem as good
as any.
• * * *
Young gen.-manager M6ulden, whose report on
the B.H. Junction is one of the most exhaustive put before
Broken Hill shareholders for years, goes to some trouble
to show why the present ore-dressing plants fail to save
so much of the metal. (The Junction’s own extraction
amounts to 52 per cent, of the lead and 30.7 per cent, of
the silver—that is, nearly one-half of the lead and more
than two-thirds of the silver go to waste.) Large quan-
tities of the ore consist of garnet sandstone, with fine par-
ticles of lead disseminated through it, and often the actual
grains of garnet are coated with lead as fine as gold-leaf.
The useless garnet is heavy, and the lead is impalpably
fine ; so between the two the losses are very great—a
flow of water sufficient to carry away the garnet from the
heavier particles of silver and lead carries away also all
the fine silver and lead.
• • • •
In view of the constant complaints that mine
contracts at Broken Hill have been so cut that fair wages
can no longer be made, it is interesting to note that the
manager of the Junction asserts that the average wage of
the miner on contract has been 10s. 3Jd. a shift. But a
liberal deduction must be made, anyhow, on account of
lost time between contracts. Also, in the Junction, on
account of the extremely treacherous nature of the
ground, the worst places are filled by wages men only.
* » » *
After the cables have told us all about the divs.
there is not much left in the best of reports to get exeited
about. This is one reason why the British B.H. accounts
for the half-year, made up to Dec. 31, hardly keep one
awake. Summing up the whole year’s profits, the figures
are £72,088, of which £45,000 went in divs., 110,000 to a
reserve fund (this is the beginning of the fund), and the
balance forward. This was after writing down smelting
and other plant rather severely. The directors own up
that the big lead prices have worked this remarkable
change in the co.’s position ; but they also show that the
cost of breaking ore has been brought down from 11s. Bjd.
per ton, the average of the previous two years, to 9s. 10d.,
while milling costs have been reduced to 4s. sd. In view
of all of which the directors “ consider that the scale of
remuneration of the board should be raised.” And Tub
Bulletin, having declared a motion to that effect carried,
draws attention to the fact that British mine-contractors
on tlie whole have had a rather bad time, too ; and it
proposes that they, also, be treated with a little-more
liberality.
• • * •
There are three proposals for the purchase of
B.H. South Oo.’s southern blocks. Hal Sheppard, of
Melb., who floated the B.H. North, first offered to float a
c 0.—200,000 shares of £l, 100,000 going to the present co.,
other 100,000 to the public at 3s. 6d. (shareholders, how-
ever, to have a prior right to take half of them. Of tins
3s. 6d. the new co. was to get 2s. 6d. Next offer came
from Adelaide, through A. S. Fotheringbam—same pro-
posal, except that the new co. would have £15,000
instead of £12,500 to go on with on account of the shares
being issued at 45., Is. of which would be taken by the
syndicate. Sheppard made another bid then —same pro-
posal, except that the shares would be issued at 55., of
which 3s. 6d. (total £17,500) would go to the new co.
Proxies are being vigorously whipped in for the meeting
early in June, and at present it looks as though the
Fotheringham crowd will win. As a side-show, there is a
proposal to add a sixth director on purpose to make room
for another Adelaide man. And inasmuch as only two or
three months ago the co., on the invitation of the
dominant section of the board, reduced the number of
directors, and thus kept out a Broken Hill and Melbourne
nominee who promised to make trouble, writer is forced
to the conclusion that you have to get up early if you
want to have breakfast with the South people.
* * * *
Somebody asks for The Bulletin’s candid
opinion of the Hercules (Mt. Reid) show, seeing that the
price has dropped from 425. to 15s. within the last two
years. Well, the Hercules appears to have heaps of rich
ore with a distressing proportion of zinc. Given a cure
for the zinc trouble, there would be great hopes of the
Eatient doing well. The Sulphide Reduction 00. of
ondon is erecting a plant at Mt. Reid, and, if these
operations prove a success, the Hercules ought to make a
move in due course.
“W. J.C. ” : The North Mt. Lyell Co. recently
bought the King Conrad Silver mine (N.S.W.) from
Messrs. P. Alliwell, J. E. Palmer (Boggy Camp’s big
diamond man) and George Grove, for £60,000 —£10,000
cash and 50,000 paid-up shares. This mine adjoins the
Conrad, belonging to John Howell, who, by the way, has
commenced to build smelters. Bora Creek is pronounced
by Father Curran, the geologist, to be, as a silver-field,
far ahead of anything in Australia so far, except Broken
Hill.

Baker’s Creek mine, Hillgrove, is “sick” and
may be expected to be worse before it’s better. Battery
ahead of mine ; by and bye the bank may be ahead of the
battery if present indifference to working-cost is per-
mitted by shareholders. In one item alone a possible
clear saving of £6OO a year is absolutely ignored. How-
ever, the present holding of the Adelaide board won’t dis-
turb their sleep o’ nights.

A miner writing from the Peak Hill mine
(W.A.) says that it is a “ better property than the Peak
Hill mine N.S.W.” Seeing that its market value is pounds
as compared with pence for the N.S.W. mine, it ought to
be. But it is doubtful whether the statement is correct.
Should Mr. Taylor make a success of the smelting process
recently started at Peak Hill (N.S.W.), the mine will not
be one to be despised. Reported that the matte from the
first run was worth £3O per ton, which did not accord
with ore assay values, there being an unaccountable dis-
crepancy between the assays and recovery in copper.

Stated Mount David in two months will have
that much-talked-of “ Monkey Winze ” run of gold on tap.
To be hoped the supply will suffice to slacken the thirst of
shareholders, who’ve had a dry time of late.
The N.S.W. Govt, metallurgical works at Clyde
have been closed. They never served any useful purpose,
for the simple reason that the plant made that impossible.
The huge tin building at Clyde will remain a monument
to the folly of the bucolic Sydney Smith. By the way,
what has become of him ? Reid promised him the billet
of commercial agent in London, but Reid's political bosses
—the Labor-party—told him straight-out that they would
not tolerate such an appointment. Shall we hear of him
next as the Hon. Sydney Smith, M.L.C. ?
• • * •
Three or four of the members of the best-known
far west N.S.W. squatting families gave the fight with
rabbits and drought best a few years back and went to Wes-
tralia. After some battling they got the Waverley ; they
have already dollied out several thousand ounces from
rich shoots, and now have 2000 tons of medium- grade
stuff at grass and 5000 tons ready to send up.
“ Kitten ” asks what has become of the “ Yamble
(N.S.W.) Gold and Diamond Dredging Co.” ? When the
glowing prospectus appeared, I applied for 200 shares.
Have not seen it advertised since. My shares have not
been yet allotted, and enquiries result in the information,
that “ it may be floated.” I think five (5) months rather
long for such meagre information.

The N.S.W. colliery proprietors have decided
to advance the price of coal Is. per ton and the hewing-
rate 4d., thus probably averting a general miners’ strike.
* » • «
A trial working of the Cocks Pioneer Co.’s
electric sluicing plant was given in Melbourne, last week,
before an interested audience. The plant—the first of
its kind built in Victoria —consists of two vertical engines
(250 h.p.) with a 500 h.p. generator and four motors.
Unity (Bendigo), after 23 years of calls, re-
appears in the div.-list. The profit is being earned from
sdwt. “dirt.” The Unity was the famous Bell and Wat-
son’s claim which started the unexampled boom of 1870.
» * • *
Dear Bulletin.—Strange that several of the
Melb. banks can give a higher price for gold than the
local Mint! A party of eight miners working a reef at War-
randyte (Vic.) took their gold to the Mint for three
months, receiving £4 per oz. Then someone laid them
on, and they have since been getting £4 Is. 6d. to £4 3s.
from the Bank of N.S.W., which pays cash up to within
£1 of the value and sends the balance a week later. As
none of the party has an account, the institution’s profit
must come out of the gold. So what about the Mint’s
£4 ?—F.O.
• • * *
Long Tunnel, the record dividend-mine of Vic-
toria, seems likely to maintain its premier position, rich
stone having been met with in the deepest level.
* * » •
Victorian mines department has agreed to spend
£4OO0 —as a national undertaking—on proving the Elles-
mere goldfield, near Bendigo, at a depth. When will the
N.S.W. department adopt the same sensible principle in
place of the wovse than absurd policy of maintaining a lot
of tucker-earners sinking grave-holes in localities where
there is not the remotest chance of mineral or metal being
found in payable quantity?

From Westralia : Mt. Margaret district forges
ahead. It is now easily second to Kalgoorlie. April
figures: Kalgoorlie 61,7090 z., Margaret 73400 z., Cool-
ganlie 70020 z. Then Broad Arrow, Kanowna, and Norse-
man. The co. registered in London to take over the
works of the Western Australian Smelting Co. is the Fre-
mantle Smelting Works, Ltd. Capital £200,000 in £1
shares. Kaufman, Rose, and other big Boulder men form
the board. The local Govt.’s substantial reduction of
freight on rich ore will help these works a lot. Formerly
ore assaying over 2oz. was charged lid. per ton per mile ;
now anything up to lOoz. goes for id. Which is as good
as any t’otherside railway dept. does. The reduction
follows the tardy recognition of the fact that big assay
values don’t always mean high net profits ; all depends on
how easily the metal is got out. Others besides railway
managers would gain by recognising that. A few mining
co.’s also gain by the freight reduction —Hannan’s Oroya,
for instance. It had got a good pile of ore at grass. And
the Oroya needs a lift. Its returns for this year show
a diminishing average month by month —lOdwt. 12gr.
in Jan. to sdwt. 17gr. in April. Bellevue Consolidated,
which Metallurgist Kohler is getting straight, has made its
final call ; this, like several other far-out mines which are
waiting for machinery, is stuck-up owing to the recent
unaccustomed floods. Meantime, reckoning on a probable
outbreak of hysterics and untying of money-bags in
London when the war ends, the correct thing is to get a
six or nine months’ option over every decent-looking
show which remains open. Scores are already held that
wa y-
Westralian co’s. go on reconstructing ; but, as
in the case of Burbanks recently, the common crowd of
shareholders show a strong disposition to see that the
directors take their bundle of new scrip at full prices.
Burbanks, which is a low-grade proposition, and, of
course, “ on the improve,” wants £15,000, and will get it
—and deserves to more than most. Block 45 also wants
another £22,000, or so. The co. has been dipping freely
into a fair-sized capital lately, spending largely on plant
as well as about t'6oo a month on development. It is a
case of buck-up-or-forfeit, with the prospects none too
rosy in the end. ...
Three or four recent isolated attempts to cut
wages on Westralian fields, joined to the common belief
(since supported by the experts’ report on the Associated)
that an influential London crowd would gladly see a re-
duction, brought out a rather imposing crowd at Boulder’s
first Eight-Hour procession. As usual, the engine-drivers
made a fine show ; and, as usual, the miners didn’t.
About the most serious of the labor troubles is at the
Paddington Consols; surfacemen, truckers, bracemen,
battery-feeders, and some others were notified of a cut to
10s a shift. A poor attempt to fill the places with miners
failed, and the works were shut down. Much the same
happened at Fraser’s S. Extended. The truckers were
asked to take 9s. instead of 10s. ; they declined, the miners
refused to do their own trucking, and fires were banked.

The Perth Mint ran a close race with the one at
Melb. last month. Totals of local gold received—Melb.,
39,7720 z.; Perth, 35,6460 z. These Vic. figures are the
lowest for three years. Still, just upon two-thirds of

Davies Karri and Jarrah shareholders met in
London lately to hear the old story of over-production
and ruinous competition. Yet the co. had made a small
profit—£soo2 for one year. It was just enough for pre-
ference shareholders to go on with ; an outlay for mill-
extensions and railways was passed on. to be covered by
debentures lately issued. “Karri” Davies, who has
been covering himself with gore and glory in S’africa, got
a cheer ; but there seemed to be more body in the cheer
that the old man got when it was announced that he and
his family, instead of selling, had added £20,000 to their
holding in the co. during the year. Some people think
that it is “Karri” Davies’ business in S’Africa to be
arrested by Kruger, and imprisoned and released, and im-
prisoned again ; others, that it is to wear a major’s kharki
and say, “ Up, boys, and at ’em ! ” But they are wrong.
His business is to insist that no timber is quite the
best unless the name of Davies is upon it; and the man-
sticking is his playful way of recreation—or of pushing
business. Anyhow, the directors say they are hopeful
that, with the gallant major to keep the name in front,
the co.’s business will presently boom in S’Africa. The
Bulletin suggests that the board forms a syndicate to
pray hourly that Kruger may blow up the mines and so
provoke the necessity to re-open and re-timber them.

The red-white-and-blue emblem which was,
on Saturday last, donned in Sydney in honor of
the relief of Mafeking, was in at least half the
instances worn with the red uppermost; and it
was sereechingly funny to see so many Jingoes
stalking proudly around in blissful unconsciousness
that tliey had thus decorated themselves with the
Dutch flag.
* * * *
A British soldier, writing home from S’af-
rica: “One of our niggers who was a waiter at
the Crown Hotel, Bloemfontein, says that he was
so insulted by the Boers that he hopes we will kill
every one of them.” Reminds Bulletin of certain
advice of Dumas plre’s to his son: “Alexandre,
never insult the gargon ; if you do he will spit in
your soup. ”
• * * •
Private letters from Capetown state that
the first Australian Bushmen’s Corps, per steamer
Atlantian, arrived there in a totally disorganised
state, and almost mutinous. “During the 24
hours the vessel lay alongside no fewer than 40 de-
serted, in spite of a heavy sentry on board with
fixed bayonets. In two cases, the men slipped
over the opposite side, and swam ashore, under
cover of darkness.”

Not hitherto published but absolutely
true that Lord Roberts caught some of the Q.
Contingent performing what he called “barefaced
loot ” near Bloemfontein, and threatened that a
repetition would mean to the extreme rear for the
remainder of the war.

Whilst proceeding to volunteer Easter
review at Wangaratta (Vic.), owing to an over-
sight the Melb. Scottish Corps was prevented
from entraining at Spencer-street Station, Melb.,
until somebody put up a cheque to cover the
amount of their fares. Bulletin is now asked by
a correspondent if the cheque referred to was ever
presented—and how the transaction ended.
* • m m
An R.C. chaplain for the Australian naval
station is a probable outcome of a recent recrimin-
atory debate in the Commons. The British Govt,
makes provision for Protestant chaplains in the
navy, but none for R.C. chaplains. Cardinal
Logue threatened in retaliation to instruct Irish
Bishops to warn the young men of their flock
against joining the navy. The Admiralty
threatened in return that if he did so ships of war
would be withdrawn from the Irish coasts. Latest
reports, however, indicate that the Admiralty will
consent to send one R.C. chaplain to each squad-
ron stationed in various ports—among the ports
named being Sydney.

“ The narrowest escape I ever
had,” said an inveterate cyclist the
other day, “was near the top of
Bourke’s Hill, the scene of many
a nasty accident. I was returning
from Gisborne with a lady friend,
and as we dismounted for the
steep descent, quite out of breath,
she lurched against me as if in
mock exhaustion, face flushed, eyes
bright, and lips inviting— all very
tempting. I was just about to take
her in my arms when a sense of caution beyond
ray years deterred me. Later knowledge 1 of the
lady has convinced me that it was one of the nar-
rowest escapes a cyclist ever had.”
* * • *
The petty-spirited “ Council of Churches ”
sends a characteristic whine to Melb. Hospital com
mittee anent the recent Hospital Art Union. The
Council repeats the holy old tarradiddle about the
amount it subscribes to the Institution—meaning
the Hospital Sunday collection, which would be
just as big if professional religionism were utterly
blotted out, and complains bitterly that visitors to
the bazaar were compelled to take their chance in
the gamble whether they wished to or not. The
Hospital committee sensibly declined to give the
Council the advt. it wanted and received the letter
without discussion. By the way, the Grandiose
title “ the Council of Churches ” is a very clums /
attempt to deceive, for neither the Anglicans nor
the R.C.’s, who compose together two-thirds of the
community, are represented on it.

Dear Bulletin.— Your par of 6th May
says that the very latest Adelaide Hospital diffi-
culty was caused by Dr. Ramsay Smith’s appoint-
ment to the Hospital staff against the Hospital
Board’s wishes. Now, when S.A. Govt, proposed
to transfer I )r. Smith from the Hospital to the
Public Health Dept., the transfer was agreed to
by the Hospital Board on the understanding that
lie would continue his connection with the Hos-
pital in an honorary capacity. That he would be
appointed was perfectly well-known to those who
were acting on behalf of the outside staff. So the
situation at the present moment is distinctly
peculiar.—S.A. Medico.

* * * *
N.S.W. Full Court more or less promises
to reinstrte ex-solicitor Meagher on its rolls when
the Dean case has been forgotten. In one sense
the Dean case will never be forgotten : it is an
almost immortal story. But practically, it is for-
gotten now ; not one person out of a hundred who
was in Sydney at the time and read the reports of
the trial could give you a correct broad summary
of the details without referring to the papers of
the day. The Bulletin makes bold to say that
even the Supreme Court Judges themselves would
make many mistakes in the matter if they relied
purely on memory.
ear * n Mger’s application drew a
packed house to Court. Mr. O’Connor, Q.C., for
the applicant, put his case calmly and well—based
it on ad raisericordiam grounds : a man’s punish-
ment should not be eternal; Meagher had com-
mitted a great fault—Mr. O’Connor called it a
“mistake”—but had suffered terribly for it, and
had so comported himself during his years of exile
from his profession as to win much sympathy, as
evidenced by the influential and widespread manner
in which his petition to the Court was signed.
Justice Owen made copious notes of the points
taken, and the Chief Justice was placid and atten-
tive ; while Justice Stephen appeared somewhat
ill at ease. The Bench conferred in open Court,
and everyone seemed to understand that Justice
Stephen was holding out and their other Honors
were arguing with him. In a little _ while
unanimity was arranged. The Chief, speaking for
the Court, refused the application on the ground
only that it was “ premature.” Then he gave the
applicant this measure of encouragement, that if
he appealed to the Court “in a few years—a very
few years,” he might not appeal unsuccessfully.
Which must have lifted a load from Meagher’s
mind. In delivering judgment the Chief alluded
to -Meagher no less than six times as “ this gentle-
man.”
* • * •
“Eller”: I know of a large Sydney
boarding-house, all the inmates of which were
sent to quarantine on account of a case of plague
occurring on the premises; but, though the house
has 50 rooms, only that in which the patient had
slept was fumigated. Now, if only one room in
any house is to be thus treated, where’s the sense
in packing off all the inhabitants to quarantine ?

A passenger by a recont steamer from
Sydney to Brisl>ane states that dozens of rats died
during the voyage. The officers professed not to
see them, and the passengers, “ who weren’t taking
any quarantine,” quietly threw them overboard as
they demised, and held their peace.
• • * •
An elderly maiden lady residing at Balmain
(Sydney) is just now bewailing the dastruction of
six tame white rats, which were her daily com-
panions, and for which, in her will, she had pro-
vided £2 a week for maintenance and care, in the
event of the creatures outliving her. A near
neighbor had raised a plague-scare about them,
v • • •
The medical officer in charge of the Auck-
land, M.L., plague-hospital is a lady, Dr. Alice
Woodward. When the boy who was bitten by a
rat was isolated as a suspect, the lady volunteered
to take charge of him and any other cases that
came along; and, as the male medicos did not
seem to be burning with ambition to rush into the
breach, the lady was very properly appointed
when the position became lucrative. None but
the brave deserve the loot.
* * • •
In Narrabri (N.S. W.) on one occasion this
year there were 50 cases of typhoid in hospital at
one time. The town drainage is allowed to run
into a creek, the water of which accordingly
causes typhoid in the “campers” who use it.

Melbourne’s grand health panjandrum.

nothing to do with possibly bubonic “natives.”
Anent which an old fisherman :—“I expects rats
don’t eat hyster-shells ; or if they does bite through

as nobodv can skoff wot a rat’s swaller?d,
nobody’ll be hurt, so far as I sees.” This may not
be science, but it looks very like horse-sense.

were worth £5 in S. Africa, till a pat-
riotic British firm wishing to fill a
want on the part of the adoring,
flooded the market with an ac-
curate imitation of the tins.

broken rifles, and Boer

gem or thereabouts.

Dear Bulletin. What an effective
medium for germ-dissemination is the wrapping of
provisions in old newspapers! Consider the
newspaper—lying on the floor, under the bed,
stowed away m outhouses and musty cupboards,
and then gathered-up and sold to the provision-
dealers to come back round the sausages or the fis
or the bread ! And the Health Board is giv
away disinfectants !—S.O B.

Wanganui (M.L.) recently held a Jingo
corrobboree, whereat was recited a distinctly
precious “patriotic” effusion by one W. R. Tay-
lor, M.A., which gives the interesting informa-
tion that ‘ ‘ Madock’s name shall long resound,”
and thus in lofty strains concludes —
But llatrick, thou untiring Mayor,
The work was planned ana done by you.
“ Hatrick, thou untiring Mayor !” Here indeed
breathed the true spirit of heroic verse. “Tay-
lor, M.A.,” is a poet worthy of the occasion.

The latest thing in Sabbath crankery
comes from Hawkes Bay (M.L.) A woman when
locking her upstairs room on the inside broke the
“ANOTHER CASE."
“ Would yer he sh'kind'sh call n'ambulance, m'dear!
I feel a hit bu-bu-bon-hic! ”
key in the lock, making herself a prisoner. So
strong were the husband’s Sabbatanan principles
that he refused to allow the lock to be removed not
even to enable his wife to go to church ; and she
had perforce so stay where she was until Monday
morning, when he liberated her. The crank
supplied her with food by ascending a high ladder
and handing it through the window !
Justice Simpson, of N.S.W. Divorce
Court, suffers from increasing importunities to
reduce the interval between the decree nisi and
absolute in divorce cases. Women petitioners, in
particular, regard three months as an intolerable
stretch of suspense, and with tears plead to have
the period shortened to a month. This, in most
cases, is with the object of a second marriage. In
a couple of instances, lately, women re-married on
the day the decree absolute was pronounced.
* * * *
Said a man on the ferry-steamer t’other
evening:—
Down there near Sussex-street there was a round block
of a cove who had a rare way of doing business. He
had a refrigerating room and when you went down
to buy, he led you gently into the cold chamber and
seating himself on a block of ice commenced hag-
glingwith you about the price. You had a chair,
of course, but you didn’t sit on it too long. That
chair had been frozen for the past five years, so
you left it and leaned up against your umbrella
if you wanted to, for the temperature was so
rare that the umbrella became as hard as
the surroundings in a very few minutes.
This fellow’s game was to haggle with you
until you became so cold that your blood
froze in you and the power of thought
gave place to numbness and semi-coma.
When he had you as done as that he
would get up with a block of ice frozen
to his pants and insist on showing you
the fish and of course for very shanie
sake you had togo. You couldn’t give
in ignominiously to a man whose lower
extremities were half ice. There was
only one thing to do : to close with
the wretch at his own price. Any-
how, when the plague-scare started
and the coves come round who knew
all about it, we kidded them to go
down to this fellow’s store and look
for rats. They found ’em. He got
’em down there and argued with
them till their teeth chattered at
a gallop. Then he slowly showed
them round the store, showed them
every nook and corner. The thin-
nest bloke, whose circulation had
been stopped for half an hour, was
dying to get out. “ Spare me days,”
he said, “ I feel os cold as if me father
was a snowstorm and me mother an
iceberg. For ’Haven's sake, let us out,
old party I’’ “ Out!” says the dealer,
“ why, we can’t get out for another two
hours—l told ’em up in the office not to
open the doors for three hours ; 1 thought
you fellows came down to look for rats
and wait till you got ’em. So then they
tumbled how the land lay, and they offered
all they had on ’em for him to let ’em out
straightaway. He mode live quid odd out of
them and very nigh froze ’em to death as well.
Oh, there was no doing business with him in his
store except on his linos.

Dear Bulletin.— Re inoculation. Might
not plague-prophylactic be infectious under cer-
tain circumstances ? I was vaccinated once, and
when the calf-stuff had got to work I used another
fellow’s razor and cut myself. As he was sus-
picious of the blood-stained razor, we asked a
medico, who admitted that the localised malady
might from a cut communicate small-pox in
its full strength.—T.D.
A Wellington (M.L.) society lady, wife of
a high official dignitary who once figured as a
prominent parliamentarian, has got into bad
odor with the Noblest of that windy city
because of alleged Boer sympathies. She
was indiscreet enough to shrug her shoul-
ders at the horrors of all war and to ex-
press a hope that this war in particular
would soon be ended, which is quite
enough evidence in the present mad-
dened frame of the public mind to con-
vince all loyal subjects that she is a trai-
tor to the Empiah.
, * • *
A loquacious Maoriland M.H.R.,
addressing a country crowd lately, inci-
dentally congratulated them on the fact
that there had been no use for the
cemetery he’d got them. During the
next two days there were four sudden
deaths, and the local joiner got his
chance at last.

A Napier (M.L.) hotel-keeper recently
made a pet of a baby-pig, and Isaac (that was the
porkerette’s name) became a great favorite with
all the hotel-frequenters, but some wags laud an in-
formation against the prop, for keeping pigs with-
in the city boundary, and Bung was fined. Ihe
fair mistress of the inn earned Isaac—who had a
blue ribbon round his neck-to court in her arms
and when the case went against them, dropped
copious tears on to the little porker s pink snout
Nevertheless, Isaac appeared the same evening at
the head of the table—on a dish with parsley
trimmings.

his understudy, Dr. McDonald, by her fine taste
for microbes, takes charge of Adelaide’s sol. ary
plague case.

In the Western Grazier, a publican ad-
vertises for “ a good Baremaid.”
The wreck of the Sierra Nevada, near
Port Phillip Heads (Vic.), resulted in a most
magnificent whisky-spread. The ship had a
hold full of “Scotch,” and when she broke up,
cases were washed up in loads, and bottles littered
the sands for half-a-mile. Those fortunate resi-
dents who went first to the scene in a deplorable
state of funk suddenly plucked up spirits, and a
great drunk hit the nation like a tidal wave, and
ran inland for miles. A batch of artillerymon
sent later to guard the wreckage found wreckers

sleeping amongst the dead-marines all over the
place. They discovered one ambitious settler who
had put up a stack of seven full cases, and he was
sitting on top with an uncorked bottle in his hand,
drinking the health of the universe. Ihe soldiers
interviewed him, demanding his name and his
intentions, and he informed them in inebriate
English that he was the man m possession. 1 hish
ish my lil’ lot,” he said; “ there’sh plen y more—
he’p yerselfs.” He was waiting for his missus, he
said—she was coming with a barrow. When that
man was plucked off his little lot, and even robbed
of his uncorked bottle, his disgust was piteous.
They left him.gibboring on a rock, and when his
wife came with the barrow she wheeled him home.
Sydney takes its plague more cheerfully
than did the people of London in 1065, the great
year of the pestilence. In that year broad-sheets
were issued with wood-cut borders made of death s-
heads, cross-bones, skeletons, hour-glasses, and old
Father Time with a scythe in his hand and cloven
hoofs instead of feet. Under the title-page of one
of them is a representation of London, with the
inscription, “ O London, repent, repent ! An
angel hovering over the city is darting arrows,
daggers and fiery tongues on the devoted metro-
polis ; while in the foreground are corpses, hearses,
people kneeling in prayer, and Death digging a
grave. These sheets teem with remedies for the
disease, compounded of extraordinary mixtures of
herbs; but they contain not a word as to the
necessity of washing as well as praying, and of
cleansing things generally. It was the great fire
of London that proved to be the true saviour of
the city and secured its immunity from subsequent
serious visitations of. the plague. One of the old-
time broad-sheets thus quaintly sets forth a special
means of preserving health : “First fast and pray.
Then take a quart of Repentance of Nineveh, and
put in two handfuls of Faith in the Blood of
Christ, with as much Hope and Charity as you
can get, and put it into the vessel of a clean con-
science. Then boil it on the fire of Love so long
till you see by the eye of Faith the black foam of
the love of this world stink in your stomach ; then
scum it off clean with the spoon of faithful prayers.
When that is done, put in the powder of Patience,
and take the cloth of Christ’s Innocency and strain
altogether in His Cup. Then drink it burning
hot near thy heart and cover thee warm with as
many cloths of Amendment of Life as God shall
strengthen thee to bear, that thou mayest sweat
out all the poisons of covetousness, pride, idolatry,
usury, swearing, lying, and such-like.”
FATHERLESS—MOTHERLESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By my window they are wheeling
Little Olay and little Feeling,
Through the long hours of the sunlight oft-repeated they
go by ;
And I watch the true caresses
Of the girl with streaming tresses
Till I think a girl was dreaming but where Worm and
Beauty lie.
No—the dirt was built above her,
Built beneath the hearts that love her :
Sister to the streaming tresses—mother to the tender
Olay ;
In the tomb they gently laid her
What a single sin had made her—
Little tender Olay and Feeling ushered into light of
day.
WIL JARRATT.
THE WOMAN SPEAKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So you think because I’m a woman
I was made but for pleasure and tears —
You ! who smile and sneer at the sex I claim
With the savoir-faire of your forty years.
Ah, yes ! I’m a woman, and human, too,
I can laugh and weep, and—pity me ! — love.
That’s the part of me made for the play of man.
3lan ! No, thanks, I can manage my glove.
We shall meet to-night at the dance, perhaps ;
You’ll see me flirting behind my fan,
With arms a-gleam and shoulders bared
To the critical gaze of men, O man !
And you’ll come to me claiming a waltz, perhaps-
I’ll grant your wish with a grateful smile ;
And your arm will clasp me a moment or two,
And we shall be lovers a little while.
But 0, the thought that my smiles suppress
(For I am strong, quite strong, O man !)
The measuring, searching, judging thoughts
That I hide as only a woman can ?
I’m only a woman, whose passionate heart
Is made for laughter, and tears, and love,
And that is for men ; but soul and brain
I keep for myself and the gods above.
AMBROSE PRATT.
