A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 22, 1900.
My Dear Mtee.—
Saturday’s races—Tattersall’s meeting at Rand-
wipk —were a very small affair, Sydney’s Best
being at the Blue Mountains and her Second-
best waving flags over themselves in George-
street. . The Third-best sported tri-colored
ribbons, but no one seemed to be wearing khaki
except one small boy whose mother wasn’t certain
of the right color, so gave him a little of every-
thing from yellow to deep-brown to make sure
of it.
The news of the Relief of Mafeking in sections
gave everyone time to be spontaneous, though
patriotic delirium set in 48 hours before the P.O.
clock chimed furiously. The public—men,
women, and children—decorated itself with
medals, and everyone looked as though he had
done it himself; while men with the thin, cada-
verous, Baden-Powell visage foresaw themselves
the season’s fashion, and rejoiced accordingly.
On Friday evening a strange man wept publicly
on the new panne cloak of a lady next him in
the theatre. “ Maf’kin—S’Africa—Relieved—
awfu’ sufferin’ —and fell asleep on her shoulder.
On Saturday a wooden, cow-like atrocity purport-
ing to be a lion and labelled “John Bull,” nodded
its head in a draper’s doorways, while the small,
kind-looking cub at its side bore on its forehead
the name of Baden-Powell. One lady said it
brought the tears to her eyes, while the others
rewarded the draper’s loyalty by swarming in-
doors and buying acres of patriotic ribbons. In
the morning 500 cadets rolled up to the Post-office
square. They God-Saved-the-Queen for an hour
or two, and then dispersed to enact the great Feed
at Mafeking in the various tea-shops.
Lady Mary Lygon made her first public appear-
ance, and looked unusually well after her trip
Home, and His Ex. made a nice little open-air
Jingo speech. Many of the leading musicians
stood outside the music-shops and tuned up the
air which doesn’t require further mentioning.
People who hadn’t spoken for years made it up
publicly, and with enthusiasm. Great interest
was manifested when a lady drove into town
dressed in a magnificent royal-blue gown, with
ribbons of the British colors streaming. It was
Lady Francis Hope, whom we used to know as
Miss May Yohe.
It is reported that Lord Beauchamp is to be
invited to take the chair at a meeting of the Uni-
versity Debating Society, where the question is to
be discussed—Why a Governor should not sub-
scribe to public hospitals or other public charities.”
But one thing must be said : if the public looks to
the Governor to subscribe in lordly style, or even
at all, to everything he is asked to help, it most
assuredly cannot intend him to live within his
official income of £7OOO a year. For, counting in all
allowances, it is hard to see how Government-
house can be maintained on less than £2O a day.
Mr. Harris, who is twin-brother to Lord
Malmesbury, is the new A.D.C. to his Ex. He is
a mild-seeming young man, who does not look as
if he would suggest any more social revolutions in
the shape of parting a man and his wife by means
of blue and white tickets at any future vice-regal
shivoos.
However, shivoos are not the order of the day
at present, owing to the recent vice-regal bereave-
ment. We are told that on July 11 his Ex. will
return to cheerful life by taking dinner with the
Chamber of Commerce, instead of in his own rat-
proof mansion.
There is a terrible rumor that, owing to a dis-
pute, Lord Beauchamp did not invite General
French to his Queen’s Birthday dinner, and that,
owing t<p several things, the Lancers don’t (or
didn’t) intend to provide a Queen’s Birthday
escort for His Excellency.
One version is that the latest fashionable
victim of the epidemic, a Woollahra girl, caught
the plague from trying on cloaks sent on approval
from up town.
There will be a dreadful disappointment to two
best girls in this city now that young See and
Lyne, jun., are reported to have left their superior
second lieutenancies behind them because their pre-
ferment was objected to as favoritism. One lias
departed from the force altogether, while No. 2
more sensibly has merely stepped down and con-
sented to herd with the ordinal y soldiery.
It is said that Lieut. Dowling, the first wounded
hero back from S’Africa, is engaged to a young
lady relative of General Richardson.
There is little dressing about Nance m her
namesake-part at Her Majesty’s, Dickens’s
heroine belonging to that section of society which
commonly regards a change of wardrobe as super-
fluous as a marriage-certificate. So Nance keeps
her lopsided dress and dubious pinafore, only
changing out of her curl-papers occasionally. By
the way, we shall have the tall American with us
till the end of the year, Williamson having
arranged with her to stay in Australasia for that
period. She will then make her final appearance
as the Shakespearian lady who washes her hands
publicly in her nightgear, and thus disappear
from our midst in a blaze of volcanic glory.
The Woman’s National Council held a little
meeting at the Town Hall, while slow, religious
music was wafted up from the organ below. A
placard on the Town Hall door, “No More Men
Required,” was understanded of this scribess when
she found two thin, white-haired men forming an
unostentatious background to the crowd of ladies.
Evidently two men, and old at that, to 50 women
js the correct thing among Advanced ladyhood.
There was something about the drains in the
second part of the programme, but this
scribess did not wait for that, having-
had enough of sewers and sinks and other
plaguey items in the daily papers. She only
wanted to hear nice, restful things about women
being a sisterhood, and always helping one
another, while she matured plans for paying out.
several other cats, and otherwise being a dear,
sweet girl.
The lecturess—Miss Spence, of S.A. Hare-
Spence system of voting fame —reiterated the
usual cheerful prophecy that women would go.
straight for moral politicians, though why women
should be expected to consider morals in politics
when they don’t consider them in marriage is a.
mystery. A moral man, it is said, is a man that
keeps one wife and beats her, and the immoral
man is the man who lives peaceably with many
women. This curious liking for morality—in
other women’s husbands—doesn’t mean that
women should not have the vote. They should
have it because it means double votes for every
married man—and married men deserve double
votes.
By the way, what are the religious opinions of
the convention ? It sounds curious to hear a
woman one moment advocating woman’s influence
in politics as a religious factor, and the next gibing
because one man said that woman never looked
more beautiful than in the act of devotion ?
The secretary read a number of notes, which
as well as could be heard (under the loud pealing
of the organ) were to the effect that delegates were
invited to represent the council in foreign lands—
Holland and France more particularly—and it
was proposed to immolate Miss Margaret Win-
deyer on the holy shrine by sending her to foreign-
speaking councils, as she has already had
experience in the land where ’Murkan is spoke.
Went fossicking round charitable institutions
the other day, don’t know why, perhaps
because it was plague-time, and it would there-
fore be hard to get in. Interviewed a home
for girls, a large home, acres of beds, expensive
boilers that are never used, room, in fact, for a
hundred and fifty girls ; and the actual girls num-
ber 13—all because of a stupid law which allows
only criminal girls under 16 to enter. If she be
a day over that age when she annexes a piece of
ribbon from the shop-counter, then gaol is her
usual destination. The matron expresses a yearn-
ing desire for really bad girls to work upon ; but
ho man gives unto her. Curious institution the
State. It wants to take over the Rand wick
Asylum, because there a handful of children lose
themselves daily amongst a pile of masonry and
have to be looked for with a microscope, as it
were, when they are to be shown to visitors ; yet
it does not seem able to manage over well with
what it has already got.
The Parramatta Industrial Home for Girls is
taking excessive precautions against the Plygue.
A hairless-looking girl opened the door to us, and
left it two inches. open while she went and con-
sulted somebody invisible. She informed writer
and friend that no one could see the institution
except when the Superintendent was in, and the
Superintendent was out. The Matron, like
Royalty, was never seen. We implored her to
ask some baronet to ask some nobleman to ask the
Matron to telephone to a certain personage in the
city who had officially guaranteed us free from
Plague for two weeks; but she was mute. We
had asked her if this was the Industrial Girls’
Home, and she had answered she did not know.
The girls in this institution are not permitted to
have any information ; they are not allowed even
to know where they are. We shook the plague
germs off our feet, and left this mysterious man-
sion.
The State Cottage Homes for old married
people are situated in the same town ; white, clean
buildings with flower-gardens in front. These
form the aristocracy of pauperism ; but it isn’t the
aristocratic pauper that necessarily gets there.
We interviewed one lady ; she was very dirty and
very religious,. with bits of moustaches knocking
about her face in places where they shouldn’t have
been. When we asked her who scrubbed her floor for
her, she said God did. Another old lady was in a
great state of gratitude because the Government
gave her food that was fit to eat; she told us as a
real, genuine fact, that the bread was never raw
and that walk about meat had never walked in
her direction. The Government is hereby
counselled to continue in its noble work. In fact,
the only grievance to every cottager is that he has
to live among other cottagers who are dependent
on the State. He does not like to associate with
people who are paupers. When he feels very bad,
however, he remembers that one of the pensioners
is a clergyman’s brother, and his self-respect is
restored. If the Govt, could get hold of a stone-
broke English Earl (Scotch would pass) and dump
him into the centre cottage, it would be doing
another noble action. The cottagers have a library
and a miniature park in common, but they don’t
seem to hobnob together on the benches or in each
other’s parlors in the slightest degree, nor do they
lend each other newspapers or talk war or good
old times, or do anything that such people are
represented as doing in novels. Poor people—the
better class of them—never do. There is far more
reserve and love of privacy—the sacredness of the
family frying-pan and all that sort of thing—
among “common ” people than among those of a
“ higher ” grade. And yet it is to those people that
we hope to introduce the kitchen in common, and
it is those people we thrust into gloomy crowded
workhouses in their sorrowful old age.
The musical phenomenon is herself a fairly
harmless person, but usually possesses a pushing
ma to pilot her to the great ear of the public.
Therefore it was pleasing to notice at Miss Elsie
Hall’s recital a conspicuous absence of aggressive
parent. It takes a thrill or two from your pleasure
in listening to somebody’s brilliant s’nata to have
flattering comments in a staccato whisper tickling
your off ear. “My daughter played before all
the ... and the Duchess said—” But the bril-
liant Elsie’s mother had sense enough to stay at
home while her daughter entertained a fraction of
Sydney’s noblest.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The little mahogany Japs, of the visiting war-
ship clustered close together beside the dais, at the
Homoeopathic Hospital ball, on Monday night, and
shyly watched the whirlers. All the Naice girls
in the vicinity gazed seriously at them in turn,
and vainly sought for distinguishing features.
One might as well look for individuality in a
bunch of grapes. At length introductions broke
up the cluster, and Miss .Ruby Madden fell into
conversation with the dapper little brownies.
Miss Lorna Price and a few other smart girls fol-
lowed suit, and the Japs. made merry. By the
way, the gem of the vice-regal party was a bonnie
figure in white—a net covered with full-stops over
a silk slip. But in spite of Jappy attractions, the
dance jogged along as heavily asa pro vision-van over
a back-country track. One missed the Mayor (who
is travelling with his wife on her trip ’Ome as far as
Albany) from the dais, and probably the Guvment
House party regretted S’Malcolm’s absence also,
as they steered their way into the common or
garden refreshment-room for their claret-cup. Sir
J ohn’s light goes out in a ball-room. A moustache,
a button-hole, and a beaming presence are not in
it with capable tootsies, and the Waxellency up-
holding the honor of the dais is a pale glory beside
the fast-walking Lord Brassey, whose collisions
with various couples left many impressions behind.
“ Medical men taboo homoeopathic hops, hence
the absence t’other night of the Qollins-street clan,
who make up a fair proportion of sassiety. The
only prominent doctor in evidence at the ball wore
a dinner-coat and his hair parted in the centre.
He enjoyed as much distinction as the Jappies.
“To revert to the attention paid the nutmeg
brethren by our girls, perhaps the best token
was a kimona-like white-satin toilette and raven
hair dressed d la Geisha, affected by a pretty,
dark little charmer just out from ’Ome. As she
circled past, the visitors nudged each other and
sent approving glances after her swathed and
waistless form. Several sirens sported Japanese
fans—which attention was not lost.
“The Japs are nice little men, but Mel-
bourne girls keep forgetting they’re not chil-
dren, and offend them by instinctively pushing
them into chairs, &c., as if they were shy small
boys. ‘Too large and loud’ is the Jap sum-
ming-up of local charmers.
“The little brown Japs off the visiting. war-
ship showed themselves to be extremely inquisi-
tive. They invaded the Melbourne shops in
troops, fingering all the goods, and jabbering over
items of interest like a school of cockatoos. Five
of them strayed into the “ladies’ department ” of
a city drapery, where a staff of nice girls was in
charge, and, not comprehending the protestations
their conduct provoked, answered only with coy
celestial smiles, and began pulling the stock about,
and investigating the displayed lingerie. When
the show-women fled the place, squealing, the
proprietor and male assistants armed themselves
with yardsticks and routed the Japs, who were
cackling amazed ly over mysterious garments.
One of their number, for the enlightenment of his
brethren, had drawn on over his arms a sample
article of apparel that is not worn a bit like that.
This is put down to Jap innocence, but with the
experience the little brown men have had they
really ought to know better.
“Mrs Burrell, the ‘blonde, massive, and forty’
matron whom all Melbourne knows by sight,
appears to have been getting a divorce from her
husband, Tom, on the ground of desertion. The
Herald report gave the thrilling news that the
vast petitioner, Helene Ernestina Louisa Dorothea
Burrell (what a name to go to Court' with !) ‘was
dressed in black satin, a Gainsborough hat with
ostrich feathers, a lynx tippet, and considerable
jewellery.’ Her tale of woe sounds not too shock-
mg. Hubby Tom was a mantle manufacturer,
and she a saleswoman when they joined hands and
hearts in London. His business failed, so they
came to Melbourne where he started to make some
more mantles, but next year Mrs. Burrell was the
manufacturer, whilst the other fellow took to drink.
Then he went away to England and America for
six years, and the only consolation his wife got
from the absentee all that long time was £l3 paid
by instalments. On his return to Melbourne she
gave him all the comforts of a home for a year or
two but complained of his pro-whisky tendencies
and finally paid his third class passage to London
with a view to following him thither if he ‘did
well. In 1898 Mrs. B. went thither to see if Tom
was doing well. She met him coming downstairs
from his office and he said he was glad to see her.
At any rate he celebrated the event by taking
lunch’ dinner and supper in the lady’s company.
Those three meals must have hardened Tom’s
heart, for his manner grew chilly, and instead of
taking his wife to the theatre when she wrote to
ask him for the favour, he replied by sending her
a couple of tickets. Mrs. Burrell’s only communi-
cation with her liege lord after that was a notice
of her divorce proceedings, which doesn’t seem to
have worried him. The Judge was sympathetic,
and the fair-haired German lady in the Gains-
borough hat got her decree all right. This all
shows how advisable it is for a married woman to
go into business for herself.
“ Lieut. Liiley, wounded in the Paardeberg fight
.s the tall, handsome Italian-looking militaire'
whose swaggah was so imposing a feature of Melb’
block. Lilley is the Adonis who was the centre of
a huge political fakement about a year ago. The
authorities suddenly suspended their regulations
so as to block young Skene and run the naice
Liiiey into a permanent - artillery commission.
Ifortunately, the job was exposed, but nevertheless
the military gang tried to rush Lilley through
An exam, however, stood in the way, and the
Lilley that toiled not neither did he spin, returned
to the militia.
“ Now that the war-rush is over, an exodus to
Pans Exhibition is all the rage with the Vic
moneyed classes. Among the travellers 40 young
ladies who have long been saving up their spare
com intend to form a contingent and visit the
show on their own. They will travel under the
auspices of a chaperone of most approved morals.
Just discovered that Speaker Mason of the
Vic. Assembly has been calmly living in Melb.
Parliament House since the end of last session.
V hen the members moved out, ‘King Charles'
apparently shifted in, planked down his lares and
penates in the Queen’s Hall, hung up his big fur
coat on the mace, and settled down for a quiet
smoke in the Council Chamber. People are now
wondering whether they can send Mason a bill
for rent or put in a bailiff to distrain on his art
furniture.
“The local plague-scare has given opportunities
for paying off old scores to several malicious
persons. At Brighton a highly-esteemed com-
mercial gentleman was subjected to a surprise
visit by the Health Board, accompanied by police,
who commandeered the premises and took posses-
sion of the inmates. It finally transpired that a
lately-dismissed servant-girl had thought fit to re-
venge herself by an anonymous letter reporting
the existence of the plague in the domicile ; the
fact of the ‘ missus ’ having recently returned
from Sydney being made use of to give an air of
verisimilitude to a bald and otherwise unconvinc-
ing narrative. On the discovery of the hoax the
blockade was raised, and the assailants trekked
home again in disgust, but the public don’t accept
the explanation in a proper spirit, for the house
and its occupants are boycotted on all sides, and
the master’s business has dwindled to zero owing
to the dread of clients to venture near a contact.
“ An ‘ unfaithful servant ’ recently run to earth
by the police was accustomed to advertise himself
as * a young Christian gardener ’ under the
pseudonym of ‘Snow-drop.” This blossom’s
bower, as testified by victims, was habitually
decorated with photographs (presumably souvenirs
of former billets) of the most fashionable girls in
Melbourne, whom he described as his ‘ sisters.’
As the brother of such really ‘ Naice ’ damsels, he
invariably conducted himself with unbearable
insolence towards the ordinary suburban woman-
kind of his employers. He got his ‘ walking-
ticket,’ of course, as the price of his disdain, but
he never went empty-handed away, as shown
by his present accumulation of bikes, &o.
“ Visitors from India testify that if we could see
the Famine sufferers we’d live on bread and water
and “pay, pay, pay” to help them out of tor-
ment. It’s like a horrible dream, to see living
skeletons pointing voicelessly to their parched
tongues. Those with strength enough sift the
dust in the street in hopes of finding eatable
grains.
“The Sierra Nevada, which went down the
other day in sight of Melbourne lights, after more
than 100 days’ voyage, had a hero on board, Craw-
ley, the chief officer, who lost his life, but not until
he had fought and won that of a bed-ridden sailor.
One poor lad, washed ashore, was haunted in his
delirium, by the drowned Captain’s hoary head.
He kept wailing “O blessed sacrifice of Calvary !
It’s the Captain, I know his grey hair ! ”
“ The photograph of herself, sent by Madame
Melba to her friends in Melbourne, and taken
together with Haddon Chambers and Bertram
Mackennal in Madame’s garden, is now explained.
Mackennal has done a bust of the diva, and the
brilliant playwright is going to marry her. ‘ The
Book of Life,’ says a man in a comedy of the
period, ‘ begins with a man and a woman in a
garden.’ ‘lt ends,’ says another character, ‘ witli
Revelations.’
“Despite old Coroner Youl’s dictum that
you might as well dress a child in gun
powder, as in flannelette,’ nothing but the
imposition of a crushing duty will, it seems,
stop fond. mothers from endangering their
children’s lives by garbing them in this terribly
inflammable material. When fire touches flan-
nelette it bursts into flame instantaneously. The
other day a housewife of my acquaintance sought
to buy a youngster’s flannel night-dress, and after
visiting six shops was compelled after all to buy
the flannel to make the garment herself. Each
shop produced a diverse array of flannelette
nightdresses, with the stereotyped excuse in
resentful tones, ‘ We’re never asked for flannel,
you know.’ Yet the morgue returns of the last
three years show that over 20 deaths by burning
were directly attributable to the wearing of the
rubbish.
Y ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
At latest there were 100 plague patients
at Sydney Quarantine Hospital. Total case to
date, 239; deaths, 82; discharged as recovered, 52.
Out of the first 238 cases of plague re-
ported to N.S.W. Board of Health 81 ended
fatally. The proportion of deaths keeps curiouslv
close to lin 3. Yet despite this high mortality
Sydney’s April death-rate is the lowest for a
decade, undoubtedly due to the careful and sus
picious manner adopted by the citizens towards
the smelly drain and diseased cat.
Premier Seddon’s health begins to seri-
ously worry his friends. The short, sharp sessions
of Maonland Parliament, when all the legislative
work of the country is crowded into three months
places a tremendous mental and physical strain
on public men who try to keep pace with things.
It killed Atkinson, apparently a man of iron, and
sent Ballance and quite a number of others to
untimely graves. And in the press gallery the
death-rate of the 14 or 15 regular journalists used
to average about one a session. Longer sessions
and shorter sittings is an urgently demanded!
reform in Maoriland Parliament.

A/>ropoB Bishop Thornton’s recent glorifi-

Mr. William Dwyer, sub-inspector of
N.S.W. State schools, retires on pension after 44
years’ service. Mr. Dwyer is father of Coadjutor-
Bishop Dwyer, of Maitland, the first native-born
Australian appointed to the R.C. episcopate.
The death of Signor Simonetti seems to
have addled Queensland Byrnes’ memorial com-
mittee. Their latest is to invite a sketch model
from sculptors showing the ex-Premier 3ft. high *
and in a frock-coat! The thing grows more and
more ridiculous.
W. R. Boothby, S.A.’s chief returning-
otticer, -has been controlling that province’s elec-
tions since 1856, the year when S.A. had its first
Constitutional Government. Boothby is the
author of the present mode of voting by ballot—
which has spread all over the world.
The recent big batch of admissions to
Vic. bar included pushful Hourigan, formerly of
SA. and Westralia. It is understood that the
peripatetic lawyer will try next to wedge his way
into N.S.W. bar, with the ambitious idea that
wherever the Federal judicature is fixed he will
be on the spot ready for briefs.
Rumored that Vic. Justice Williams will
probably retire from the bench at the end of his
12 months’ trip. Justice Holroyd is also men-
tioned as likely to quit soon. Both would be en-
titled to pensions of £l5OO, and successors would
probably be found in brilliant Isaacs or solid
C ussen.
Lord Roberts’ official organ at Bloem-
fontein offers a prize for the best suggestion for a
name for “the new colony of our greater Empire,”
and in doing so prints a long list of already-
suggested names. Among these is “New Ireland! ”
The editor of Bobs’ organ must indeed be a dull
person.
Edmund John Eyre, first of the Australian
explorers in point of time, is still living in Eng-
land ; last month, he celebrated the 50th anniver-
sary of his marriage, which took place in Auck-
land. But that was years after his historic
expedition in the country lying between Spencer’s
Gulf and Albany. Eyre that time—it was in ’4O
—set out with five whites and three blacks. Only
one solitary black got through to the West with
him ; the rest were either murdered or deserted,
or were sent back. Eyre was but 25 at the time.
The band which marched through Mel-
bourne the other night to advertise the Town Hall
concert in aid of the widow and orphans of John
Hancock, M.L.A., played,“Sons of the Sea” and
“Soldiers of the Queen” all the way down
Bourke-street. John was the man who “hated
the sight of a soldier,” and never feared to admit
it, but militarism has got even.
Kipling’s Times articles in which he des-
cribes his experiences with a hospital train—
printed also in Melb. Argus and S.M. Herald—
approach perilously near to mere twaddle. The
stuff read hard, labored and theatrical—like Kip-
lingese by an impoverished disciple. It is sudden-
jerk literature, every jerk giving a jar and sug-
gesting the stertorous efforts of a well-paid patriot
to live up to his cheque. The Kipling mannerism
is there in all Kipling’s African stuff, but it is
machine-made, and one feels ashamed of the un-
avoidable suspicion that it is Rhodes who is turn-
ing the handle.
A certain “calico” Vie. M.L.C.
once said in the course of an address in
the House : “ I owe my success in busi-
ness to my adherence to the motto
‘ Never miss a chance.’ ” Which was also
Dick Turpin’s precept.
The Marquis of Norinanby, sou
of the anecdote-spotted Governor of
Queensland, Maoriland and Victoria,
seems to be a chip of the old block.
Money is scarce with him. He is a Canon
of Windsor and runs a sort of boarding-
house for bachelors in Mulgrave Castle,
and is besides head of a boys’ boarding-
school ; and still the income doesn’t keep
his titles in countenance. Although a
bachelor of only 54, the soap and petroleum
girls of America appear to let him go by.
Mr. Jacob Goldstein (who, at
latast, was understood to be a Rabbi
somewhere in England) has, it it alleged,
a recommendation from Julian Salomons
for the office of what may be called
Journalistic Assistant to N.S.W. Agent-
General. Mr. Goldstein, who originally
hailed from Melbourne, was a few years
ago a political wire-puller in Sydney,
and was an important factor of ex-Pre-
mier Reid’s election committees. He
was placed on the estimates for £2(5 ss. for
some service or other, after which he
started for London. There he again met
Reid and was commissioned to report on
French charities, with the result that the
estimates were loaded with £l3O on
this account. Jacob appears to know his
way about among politicians.
The Hist N.S.W. member secured in a
State seat by the transfer of a powerful rival to
the Federal arena is the venerable Archer, of Bur-
wood. W. M’Millan formally bids his old con-
stituency goodbye, and looks higher. Archer is a
Londoner, was an old Palmer digger, a contractor,
a colliery owner, a sheep and cattle salesman, and
has be&n many times alderman and once at least
Mayor.
An extremely respectable and
haughty manager of a great Victorian
financial institution created a profound
feeling of insecurity in a Melbourne
suburb, the other day, by bounding
along the main street with a red face, a
belltopper Stuck on anyhow, and his
coat-tails streaming in the air. As the
railway-station hove in sight he laid
down his ears and sprinted, cutting out
the last 100yds. in record time. As
he lurched up against the barrier with
his tongue on his chin, the station-
master stepped out with a smile. “Ah !
Mr. Midas, I know what you’ve come
for,” he said, lifting a bag from his
office at the same time. “ Porter Smith
found it in the carriage you travelled
in.” Midas snatched the bag, tore it
open frantically, and, fervently breath-
ing “ Thank God ! ” sank into a seat to
mop his flurried brow. “ I had a
month’s salary in that bag,” he gasped
at length, in a sudden burst of confi-
dence, “over £100.” “ Indeed,” said
the station - master ; “ Porter Smith is
at the station now.” “Is he, indeed!”
rejoined Midas, arising; “I must be
getting away home.” The S.-M.
interposed, “Yes; Smith’s just on
the platform now; I’ll call him.”
“ Don’t bother him, now he’s busy,”
said Midas, as he picked up his
treasure and scooted, “but give him my
best thanks ”

J, C. Syme, part-proprietor of
Melb. Age until a few years ago, gets his Christian
names from Joseph Cowen, the recently-deceased
distinguished English Parliamentarian. Ebenezer
Syme, J.C.’s father, was connected with Cowen’s
Newcastle paper in 1851. Cowen’s printed
volume of speeches is as good as John Bright’s,
but he was what is called in politics “impractic-
able”—i.e., honest —so he never reached Cabinet
rank, though a rich man.
Gov. Lamington (erstwhile Premier Salis-
bury’s private secretary) is said to be most
ambitious of the Federal Governor-Generalship,
but he has “ put his foot into it ” so dejply that it
is unlikely Australia will take any Lamington—
any more than she will take any more Way than
he can help under Federation.
Out of 125 members of N.S.W. Assembly,
113 signed R. D. Meagher’s petition for reinstate-
ment on the roll of attorneys, from which Meagher
was struck off after the Dean episode. Of the
remaining dozen, executive reasons, withheld
several signatures. The remainder—with one
exception—wished to sign, but would not return
to Sydney on account of the plague, and Meagher
feared the postal miscarriage of the document.
Certainly a remarkable instance of unanimity, in
fenerosity at least. The petition was also signed
y 113 attorneys.

a us^ce Bundey, the most popular of
B.A. Judges, has issued a booklet entitled “The
Conviction of Innocent Men.” in which he
warns juries not to give too i -h credence
to policemen, and not to reiy over much
no circumstantial evidence. He endorses
the view put forward by the editor of
Taylor on Evidence ”; “ That all men are
guilty till they are proved to be innocent
is naturally the creed of the police, but
it is a creed that finds no sanction in a
court of justice.” Then the Judge proceeds
to relate a case in which all the circum-
stances alleged in evidence seemed to point
conclusively to a prisoner’s guilt, with the
result that he was convicted and sentenced
*-° years’ imprisonment, whereas in the
end it was proved he was entirely innocent,
and that the incriminating circumstances
were only so many extraordinary coinci-
dences.
In N.S.W. law-courts the most
shabby, old, yellow, disreputable-looking
wig is worn by a pugnacious Q.C. who pro-
bably earns more than any other man at
the bar. Q.C. Reid wears his wig on the
side over his left ear, as he does his hat.
“Jack Want” has a new wig, bought
in London during his recent visit. Emu-
lous young juniors are having their
seldom-used wigs altered in imitation of
Want’s, which will be regarded as the
latest style—till Salomons comes out.
N.b. W. Public Service Board transfers
R. P. Sellors, first assistant in Sydney Observa-
tory, to Lands Dept., and fills his place with
three junior clerks. Sellors is credited with a
talent for astronomy, and with some good original
work in his line. Where’s the sense of robbing
the Observatory of its brains if it is to do any
useful work ? And if it does no useful work,
what’s the use of keeping its extensive equipment
(the 11 fin. refracting telescope alone is worth
£10,000) to provide a sort of free celestial raree-
show for straggling pleasure-seekers?
The “ Dutch Seer ” who (as per cable)
when consulted by Kruger, predicted that the war
would end in June and that within three months
afterwards Oom Paul would die, may make a
reputation on his prophecy, but all the same he is
a man who travels more on his horse-sense than
on his second sight. On present appearances this
war must soon end, and as poor old Kruger is in
an advanced state of Bright’s disease, and is
probably being propped up by excitement, he is
likely enough to collapse utterly when the smash
finally and irrevocably comes.

The young Sydney pressman who is
understood to have gone ’Ome to a position in
N.S.W. Agent-General’s office, reputedly en-
gineered things with characteristic audacity.
The original idea was that he should go as private-
secretary, to Edmund Barton on the Federal
jaunt, but the N.S.W. delegate had someone else
in favor, and nobody was appointed at all. Then
the pressman impressed on Premier Lyne that as
all arrangements for his journey were completed,
something would have to be done for him. And
Lyne “came over.”
Contended by George Meudell, the beaten
candidate, that his rival, James Sadler, M.L.A.,
was not lawfully elected for Grenville (Vic.) the
other week. The Law is said to require a Par-
liamentary candidate's personal signature to his
nomination paper, and as Mr. Sadler had not come
back to Victoria (from a visit to W.A.) when his
nomination paper was lodged, his election
is invalid. This raises a solemn question as
to whether Duncan Gillies and President Zeal are
members of Vic. Asssmbly and Leg. Council.
Their last nom. papers were signed by proxies.
The real boss of Victoria is Guppy Robin-
son, city editor of Melb. Age, who is the power
behind the throne of D. Syme. When Guppy
talks, David obeys, and such is the former’s in-
fluence that when he took a year’s holiday recently,
A ZOO ITEM .
David temporarily instated Robinson, jun., as city
editor. In appearance Guppy is Hop’s typical
fat man reproduced in every particular— snort,
sauat, bell-toppered, and with little grey side-
whiskers. Lots of people believe him to be the
original Robinson who thought the world would
hurry up when he hollered out “Gee ! ”
Melbourne’s sewer-bard Fitz Gibbon has
just discovered a shameful deficiency of “ morality
of character ” amongst the Yankees, and puts his
large pragmatical finger on the spot first try. This
lack of spiritual beauty is due to the fact that
“ There is no royalty in America, no nobility.”
Writes Mr. C. W. Barlow, lion. gen. sec.
of the Australian Socialist League:—
Dear Bulletin.— Re your par. (5/5/’00) with regard to
11. M. llyndman. Hyndman lias not been to South Africa
since the war started, and has since and before the com-
mencement of the war been continually on the platform in
England denouncing Chamberlain, Rhodes and 00. As
to his being “ a faddist who does not stick to things,”
about 1880 he was one of the founders of the Democratic
Federation of Great Britain. This, which has since
become the Social Democratic Federation, is the head and
front of militant socialism in G. 8., and Hyndman remains
one of its ablest members. His writings on economics,
too, will live when many of the pseudo-economists of to-
day shall have been forgotten.
As to Hyndman not having visited S’Africa
since the war started, Mr. Barlow’s information
may be correct, but all the same it was circum-
stantially set out in a newsletter from the front,
the other day, to a Sydney daily paper, that Hynd-
man was there with his gun, and that he had
found a new way of “ levelling the masses.”
“ Thorold Dalrymple ” on The Bulletin
sonneteers: —
It would be wrong to say that the making of sonnets to
order is a waste of literary effort when such an excep-
tional piece of work as Fenton’s “ Australia ” is thus pro-
duced. Indeed, with a “ continental ” theme for in-
spiration it was expected that your sonneteering geysers
would give us something to remember them by in at
least half-a-dozen cases. Yet, out of a 60-fold frenzy we
have one sonnet, and one only, which is worthy of its
source, several respectable failures, other failures' which
are distinctly not respectable, and quite a number which
are not so much failures as crimes. This verse-to-order
business is lamentable. Give the gentlemen their own
time, let them choose their own occasion and wait for
original notions to come to them fortuitously as they
dream in their garrets. As it is, nearly the whole of your
chaffering sonneteers present us with the one thought,
badly digested. They all speir fortunes for gold like
toothless beldames in the market-place. One remarks
prettily that “ From ageless sleep the island continent
woke into life another, that “ I.ong ages hidden from
the world she lay another, that “Once she was left
alone amid the sea;” another—but enough ! One only
has properly masticated the idea, and he calls her “ Last
sea-thing dredged by sailor Time from Space.” It is my
idea that he had the whole thing stewing in his mental
cauldron long before the unusual Maicenas crossed his
hand with coin.

Aboriginalities.
The other day two sailors, who had run
away from their ship in Adelaide, made due north,
eking out their scanty supply of biscuits with
“ game” caught by the way. On their arrival at
a far-away-back squatter’s, the latter looked at
them suspiciously, and asked them what they had
been living on during their long tramp. “ Well,”
said one of the tars, “we had some biscuits, and
we used to kill wild sheep.”

“ Von K.” : Animal chivalry (8., 7/4/’00)
seems to be a virtue (is chivalry a virtue nowa-
days ?) acquired concomitantly with domestica-
tion. Male dingoes will on occasion kill tame
sluts (even during the cestrual period). Apropos,
it is curious that in bullocks the protective in-
stinct is strong enough to bring them rushing to
the rescue of a bleating calf, whereas (here the
woman-franchiser will smile) the bull doesn’t care
for anything but his own sacred person.
“ The Lyre ” : Re animal chivalry. T have
an old cock saddle-pigeon who, when his missus is
away, makes violent love to a lady Antwerp,
recently widowed, but, when Mrs. Saddle returns,
the base deceiver enthusiastically assists to chivvy
Mrs. Antwerp out of the loft.

The Weekly Telegraph (London) has
been publishing accounts of experiences in the
Australian bush. Here’s a hunk :—“ On the way
from Paroo River to Cooper’s Creek we left the
beaten track for the sake of better grass. As we
rode over a sheltered little box-flat we came to a
tree in the centre decorated with a skeleton hanu-
iny in chains. Back-country camps are a bit
lawless, and this had probably been a victim of
Judge Lynch.” After all, Boer-land isn’t worse
libelled than our own Bakblox-—as far as the old
country press is concerned.
“‘NOBODY ASKED YOU, SIR,’ SHE SAID.”
“ Yarrum ” : The other day I asked a
South Coast dairyman how many cans of milk he
got daily. He said : “ Ten when the boys do the
milking, and twelve when the girls do it.” He
explained that cows always give their milk better
to girls. There may be something in it.

Singleton, N.S.W., Argus has
been boasting lately over a “find” in the shape
of an “ oldest resident ” named Thomas Parmeter,
who, out of his 78 years on earth has spent 75 in
that district. I once worked on a paper in that
arcadian locality and found men and women nearly
50 years of age, who had been born there, and yet,
though Newcastle was only 50 miles away by rail,
had never seen the sea. " "

T..S. : 1 he Australian fox has not lost
any of his old-world cunning and caution. A fox
was in the habit of crossing a stone wall near my
place (in western Vic.) early every morning. One
day the wall was taken away to a stone-crusher,
and a temporary wire-fence put in its place. Next
morning I saw the fox come up to the fence, trot
carefully up and down it, and then go back. Later
on I found him crossing about a quarter-mile fur-
ther down, where the wall still remained, although
he could have walked under the wire anywhere.

Six unmarried sisters in the backblocks of
Nelson (M.L.) province are reported to have this
season grown and harvested 24 tons of raspberries,
which they retailed at 3d. per lb., and to have
notched a further £3(l by hop-picking. This beats
small sheep-farming.

The Merton (Vic.) Wild Dog Association
lias been paying £2 10s. for each full-grown wild-
dog destroyed within the area of the association.
Better than shooting Boers !

Dear Bulletin. —lsn’t the present N.S. VY.
Public-school system of making a child attend
school 70 days in half a year responsible for the
fact that so many country “kids” can hardly
read or write ? The cocky sends his youngsters to
do the 70 (or even the 140 days for the year)
straight off, and keeps them at home for the rest
of the time; so that when the “kid” goes to
school again it has forgotten so much that it ha ?
practically to make a fresh start, and thus never
gets educationally ahead. If children were sent
to school 15 days in each month they would have
a show. —Synd.
« * * *
Curious that, though Parliament and
public are always being assured that pastoral pur-
suits nowadays mean insolvency, it is always the
seasoned squatter who buys whatever runs change
hands at auction. True, prices have been low
lately, and perhaps the old squatters have been
“ averaging.” Ediacara, of 400 odd square miles,
once reckoned among the best of Northern S. A.
squattages, has just been taken, with its 5000
sheep and improvements, for £3126.
“ W.J.” : That recent ploughing skuich
in The Bulletin was misleading about our agri-
cultural methods. I don’t refer to the woman
plough-women are too common —but to the plough.
Who ever saw a “single-furrow” out-back? I'oo
much like a good season, or other impossibility,
after our huge experience of stump bounders and
exterminators, with seven or eight horses to them.
In the old days, on Kararaburn
station (N.S.W.) was—this is a chest-
nut —a manager familiarly known as
“Jacky” Dow, to whom all the
tramps in the province used to make,
because lie had a barracks built
especially for them, where they could
remain one night and replenish their
tucker-bags next morning. One day a
swaggie came and asked for work.
“Any kind of work, Mr. Dow; not
partikler, any kind of tiddly-winkin’
work, you know.” “Ah,” said Jacky,
“just so, my man, just so. Well, you
see that grindstone over there ; you go
and keep turning that till I come
back, my man.” The follow started,
and turned and turned, every now
and again lifting expectant eyes
towards the house, but Jacky was
in a hack skillion all this time watch-
ing him. Swaggie kept on turning,
every now and then knocking off to
walk round the grindstone, look at it,
then up at the house, then more turn-
ing. At last, realising that he had
been “ had,” he went up to the house.
Jacky came out and Swaggie com-
menced. “ What do yer mean by
keepin’ me turnin’ that —' grind-
stone like that?” “ Well, my man,
you said you wanted work, you
know—and you yot it.” Another
time Jacky had a fellow emptying a
cask of water with a spoon, and,
when ho finished, Jacky coolly told
him to “put it back now, my man.”

“ R. W.” : Let me tell “ Maori-
hinder” (8., 14/4/’00) that farmers
on the Northern (N.S.W.) rivers and
iti Tumut district do not consider they
have had a good crop unless they get
80 or 90 bushels of maize to the acre—and the
other cereals also give very high averages.
S. Aus. Police-Commissioner was lately
urged to put down the supply of liquor to the
blacks in their various camps, especially on the
Murray ; hut the Com. got home heavily on the
busy-bodies with the statement that if they
wouldn’t form mission stations alongside town-
ships, the work of the police would be a lot easier.
“ Patpnie,” who was sent to gaol last week,” one
officer reported, “was a teacher at the camp, and
a Bible reader.” The same officer, illustrating
some of the difficulties of the police, mentioned
that one of the blackfellows is married to a white
woman, who buys the stuff—they drink crude
wine mostly—and whacks it with her lord. Still,
the lord is a black, even if he is her husband; so
being caught lately in the act of handing over a
flagon to him she was gaoled for a month. Which
shows that it isn’t all wine and skittles to be a
blackfellow’s wife.
Lately, when a young Murray Mouth black-
fellow went the usual way to work to get a gin
and got her, the good people of those parts waited
upon the couple at their mia-mia and told them
how sinful it was to live like that. Lucy didn’t
quite grasp the sinfulness of it; but when it was
explained that Christian marriage meant wedding-
cake, she said she would take any quantity of it.
They took over a proper parson and some cake to
the camp next day, and were met with—“ Oh, you
come too soon. My ole man wants his grub ’fore
he get married.” So the wedding-party had to
wait while Lucy cooked the old man’s fish and
fowl; and then he made an honest woman of her
while she eyed the cake and tried to reach it with
her toes. Afterwards she was anxious to know
whether it would be repeated after every picca-
ninny.

“ L.S.” : In answer to “ S.” (B. 28/4/’00).
It won d be a very hot day indeed when a man
could un bunny down. Tlie average lady-rabbit
could rear a family while the smart man was
chasing her—in the open. The two-legged animal
would go grey in the attempt. I have seen fox-
terriers coursing, but with bunnies that had been
cooped-up for weeks, some of which wouldn’t run
at all. [Nevertheless, it’s true that a smart
pedestrian can run even a hare down, if there’s
just snow enough to indicate the track.—Ed. B.]

have been very young indeed, as there
was no sign of quills and the eyes were not opened.

which was why it lived a fortnight, as I could not
get it to eat anything. By that time the quii.s
were making an appearance.

Dear Bulletin, —I have a bitch—half
Gordon setter and half collie —who gives proof of
a spiritual side to her nature. One day I set her
on to a cow that had strayed into the garden.
Presently there was a yelp of pain, and the slut
came hopping back on three legs, evidently suf-
fering much agony. One of her feet had been
trodden on by the cow. My grief was demonstra-
tive, but in the middle of my caresses she suddenly
ceased yelping, and, although tears were running
down her muzzle, began to frisk on three legs, gave
some would-be joyous but falsetto barks, and fled
to the stable. I followed and looked through a
knot-hole. The dog was limping about moaning,
but the moment I went in the paw was lowered
and she again began to act “ I’m not hurt.” Now,
that dog felt my pain more than her own. To
save my feelings she disguised her’s. —A.C.O.

“CMB”: Re box-trees minus their
bark (‘ ‘ Wils,” 8., 12/5/’00). The following may
not only explain the mystery, but be of service to
some scientific electrician. In Wellington dis-
trict (N.S.W.), where there are no galali parrots, 1
was startled during a dry thunderstorm by a
blinding flash of lightning immediately followed
by a deafening peal of thunder, and guessed scime-
tliing was struck in the vicinity. Next .day 1
noticed near the house a yellow box-tree with its
bark stripped entirely off one side though the
wood was not splintered, and . the sap was
scarcely dry. After vainly searching some tune
for the missing bark, found it strewn all around
but so finely shredded up as to be almost invisible
among the grass.

“ Broadaxe ” : At the Isis (Q.) I M " i s
working up an ironbark that had been felled for
sometime. I. had knocked off the bark, cut a
length, and was having a spell, when I noticed a
peculiar looking fly prospecting the grub-holes. It
was black, about 5-Bm. long, and slender, with a
tail as long as itself, conqiosod of three sections,
and with a droop at the end. After going partly
down several grub-holes it at last found one that
suited, and dropping its tail thereinto, it backed
like a draught-horse and began to screw its
latter-end round as if trying to work it round the
grub. Next it started to pull, and I took hold of
it to give it a lift which it didn’t like, for it
emitted a distinctly audible squeak. 1 let go, and
it backed into the hole again, and after screwing
as before got another pull on. But just then a
friendly cloud came along, and 1 took advantage
of the shade to do a bit more work.

“W.M.F.”: Have frequently witnessed
the “ rain-making ” of different Queensland tribes,
but have never heard of anything like what is
described by “Nell Cook” (B. 4/4 ’00). On the
border the ceremony is very peculiar. Generally
an old chief, accompanied by his gin and a few
followers, makes for the most available river or
creek, into which he wades up to his middle, and
sets to splashing the water about with a big stick,
If the cods (had it not been cloudy the excur-
sion wouldn’t have been made) are slow in work-
ing up the chief conies out, and starts heaving
boulders into the water.

“Robbery Under Arms.” A few years back a

masons, and had the lonely grave covered with a
plain marble slab, and enclosed with stone sur-
mounted with a neat iron railing. Then she dis-
appeared as mysteriously as she came.

“T.G.F.’ : : Can any of your readers tell
me how the white opossum is bred, and why two
“FAT TOMMY,” of Tibooburra., N.S.W.; 21
yearn of age, 25st. weight. Supposed to be Aus-

is it possible to cross the ordinary ’possum with the
flying-squirrel ? [The white ’possum is probably
an albino, and albinism, being an individual de-
fect of pigmentation, does not seem to be trans-
missible to offspring. The flying-squirrel is not,
as “T.G.F.” seems to think, a variety of ’possum.
The species (Petaurus) is quite distinct, and a cross
would therefore be extremely improbable.—Ed. 13.]

“T.G.”: On an out-back Barwon station
is a full-blooded aboriginal woman with a milky-
white complexion—as far as lam aware the only
known N.H.W. aboriginal albino. She is about
40 years old, has never married, and is a close
friend of the boundary-rider’s wife. This gin
comes of the almost extinct Naroo tribe, and is
known as “ Worra Wirra,” or “White Devil.”
At Ellengerah Station-, near Warren, GO years
ago, there lived the only other albino aboriginal I
have heard of. It is a tradition among the Terra-
lnungamiue blacks that this albino’s mother was
chased by a white emu some time before the freak’s
birth.
« • * *
Harry Stockdale writes :
A very striking physical peculiarity of the wild Aus-
tralian black is the power and flexibility of the “ big toe."
This is used in many ways as easily as a thumb and in
some respects more so. In the climbing of large trees a
notch is hacked out with the tomahawk about an inch
deep, and from 3 to 4in. wide, and into this is inserted
the big toe, which has then to support the whole weight
and balance of the body until another has been cut at a
different angle, and from 4 to sin. higher up for the sup-
port of the other foot. This is necessarily slow and
laborious work, sometimes as many as 20 cuts having to
be made before a friendly branch is reached, The greatest
strain occurs in the effort to insert the toe of the opposite
foot in the higher notch ; during this and the cutting of
the notches the body of the man appears to veritably
cling to the tree. Yet I have never known a native to
lose his hold either from fear or from a giving-way of the
toe. It is marvellous how a single toe can support such a
weight under such difficulties, but the development of
tlie digit is of course much greater than with a European.
ls.v the aid of the great toe also, a black, whilst standing
perfectly erect, can deftly pick up from the ground a pin,
a knife, a stone, &c., and, so slyly as to escape detection,
pass the article into his hand. In stealing upon an
enemy or upon game, a black will crawl upon his hands
and knees or on his stomach, his “ billetta ” or spear-
thrower in his hand, but the long deadly spear held and
dragged between the big and second toes until he gets
close enough to throw it. In making boomerangs and
other weapons also the two great toes are employed to
hold the article steady to be worked upon with the
hands.

“ Chas. J.” :In reply to “ J.M.R. (I>-,
5 5 ’00). John Gaggin, in his recent boo.*,
“Among the Man-Eaters,” writes:—“. • • I
once gave some ’gator’s eggs to a lady, who put
them in a box in her bedroom and forgot all about
them, but some 10 days afterwards, hearing a
grunting in the box, she opened it, and out came
half-a-dozen young ’gators open - mouthed !
Young ’gators are about six or seven inches long
when born, and it is a puzzle how such big things
come out of such a small egg.” “Alligators (in
X.Q.),” Gaggin further writes, in effect, “make
their nests as the birds, in sheltered places on
banks of rivers, and dark, silent lagoons. Ihe
eggs, which are about the size and shape of a
(luck’s, but rougher in the shell, are laid in mounds
of clayey soil and hush debris—2oo to .300 in each
nest—the mound being from three to four feet
high by about three square at the base. ... Ihe
eggs seem to hatch without external heat, but I
cannot sav in what period. The parents remain
in the neighborhood until the young are born, and
the loving father eats any of bis children lie can
catch.”

“ Les. ().” : Re animals drinking salt-
water at Broadmount, Q. (“V.,” 8., 12/5, 00).
Have seen cattle drink the salt-water of George's
River, eight miles below Liverpool (N.S. \\.)
dam. This was during dry-time last year, when
the water was so salty that sole, whiting, flathead,
and jewfish were plentiful within three miles of
Liverpool. The cattle have regular tracks to the
water, and wade in knee-deep. No other per-
manent water on the estate.
“Hecate”: In 1854 Mi.ska Hauser, an
old-time Hungarian violinist of renown, visited
Sydney, and contributed to Chambers’s Journal
of 18th Aug., 1855, a particularly lively account of
his exp ‘riences. He mentions a gambling-house
which gave free dinner-parties twice a week, and,
after describing the citizens as being abandoned to
mammon-worship and gambling in its lowest
form, this veracious chronicler proceeds :
A few days after my arrival I paid my visits to the dif-
ferent editors of Sydney. At my first call I came to a
palace-like house, the ground floor occupied for the print-
ing office. On the first floor, among other advertisements,
1 found a tablet informing visitors that the editor cannot
be spoken with unless paid for his valuable time ; accord-
ingly, everybody, without exception, is advised to buy a
ticket of admission at the door of the waiting-room
—one hour costing 10s., half-an-hour 65., fifteen
minutes 3s. Such were the contents of this singu-
lar price-current of time. I went into the wait-
ing-room, and buying from the Australian negro in
red livery an hour of his master’s time, I entered the par-
lor with a strong feeling of curiosity. The editor received
me in a very unprepossessing and sluggish manner. “You
are an artist, and come from Europe to make money ? ”
said ho in a not very friendly tone. But when lie under-
stood that I had come from South America and California
his face lighted up and his voice became less abrupt, lie
ask d me, without longer preface, what pecuniary sacri-
fice I was ready to make in order to be puffed by his
paper. I was startled by this bluntness, and replied that,
in case of success, 1 would surely give him material proofs
of my gratitude ; but he did not find my answer precise
enough, and requested me to come at once to a definite
understanding, and to pay a certain sum, without which,
according to him, it might be impossible for me to
succeed. Telling him that I wished to adjourn
the conference, as 1 could not at once come
to a decision, I left this temple of editorial
integrity and public spirit. The other editors were less
rapacious and more friendly : they gave me, indeed, the
best advice about iny concerts. ... I could not feel
sympathy with the population of Sydney, and did not
expect to meet with any from them. Everybody here
being immersed in the cares of profit and loss is coid and
reserved, and in society dull and stupid. Political meet-
ings alone are apt to draw out their eloquence, and
nothing but drunken revels and cock-fights amuse them.
How could we expect a taste for the fine arts in such a
state of society ?
The Australian negro in red livery is distinctly
good. And was it Dr. West or Dr. Garran who
sat for what is obviously intended to be a portrait
of the editor of S.M. Herald? This article was
designed to be the first of the series, but, in the
issue of Chambers’s Journal of the 22nd of tho
following Sept., the editor publishes a note, in
which he says :
The assertions have been flatly contradicted by so many
respectable persons, that, having' no knowledge of the
subject ourselves, we hasten to withdraw from Mr.
Hauser’s statements any support they may be supposed
THRIFT.

All that “Banjo” Paterson has said re
the failure of the British cavalry “ charge ” in
South Africa is endorsed by a soldier correspond-
ent for London D.T. “Banjo” refers to the so-
called charge of the toffy Lifeguards and Lancers
as an astounding fraud—an awful satire on the
pictures that newspaper artists turn out. The
Boers are never in the least danger of being over-
taken by the heavy men on weary geo-gees who
make a show of resolve to get among them and
“cut them up.” Modern rifles in the hands of
marksmen have blown certain gory old warfare
rules to smithereens, and the most shattered rule
of all is the rule of converting retreats into routs
by means of thunderous cavalry charges. The
picture which shows a concerted rush of slashing
routers on fiery steeds was not always untrue. I n
the days when armies advanced in close order to
within 100 yards of one another, and fought until
one army or other was beaten all along the 1 ine, the
victorious side would send forth a reserve body of
mounted sabres and spearsmen to take gruesome
liberties with the enemy's rearguard. But those
days are not now. The Hussars impressed
“Banjo” when he saw them riding out of camp.
Their disciplined canter suggested great possi-
bilities of destructive power. Their “charge”
across the veldt at the close of a tiring day was a
hopeless failure. The Boers leisurely potted a
few of them from afar, so they picked up their
fallen comrades and retired. That’s how “Banjo”
put the case. The London Daily Telegraph
critic, writing as an eye-witness of Buller’s ad-
vance to Ladysmith, says :
Against the Mauser or the Lee-Metford nothing but
infantry can prevail ... It is almost certain that our
cavalry will have to be differently trained, at all events
armed with a long rifle. The day of “ shock ’ action is
practically over. ... In a great measure our cavalry
officers are to blame ; they are behind the times. They
must change their system, I think, if they are to meet an
army furnished with mounted infantry or very mobile
troops. The rifle must he carried, and if the jingle and
the swagger disappear -and very cheap swagger it is 've
shall see in their places soldiers .worthy to take their
places beside the infantry of the Line.
If the Boers have brought discredit upon the
pomp and circumstance of Household Brigades
and other “crack” institutions, the world owes a
great debt of gratitude to those God-fearing cam-
paigners. War emanates largely from swaggah.
The Age of Chivalry (in armor) was a period of
exaggerated martial swaggah. I rom mail-clad
knights we have come down to the heavy dragoon
officahs whose influence—political, social, pictorial
—makes directly and indirectly for “ Wall. ”
Now that those expensive aristocrats of the Ser-
vice are known to he behind the times they will
tend to bring Wah into ridicule. And ridicule is
a great reformer.

“ Either rats are getting scarcer or the sixpenny bonus is losing its
charm. The return for yesterday showed a considerable decline, only 575
being brought in. One of this number came from Government House,
and in accordance with his Excellency’s wish was sent on to the Health
Department Laboratory for examination. It was, however, found to be
quite free from plague." —Sydney daily. May 11. —-
Doubtless this rat had got in by the blue ticket entrance.

The Fearful Summer Heat
in the Australian Back Country
ENERVATES THE STRONGEST MEN AND WOMEN.
An Interesting; Case Illustrating: the Virtues of Clements Tonic-
THE CASE OF Mi tt. ANNIE LEESE.
// (Reported for “ Red fern Times. 3 ')
Not far from the Exhibition Building our
reporter found No. 37 George Street,
Redfern, the residence of M rs. Aunie Leese,
a well known fashionable dress maker.
The lady chanced to be at home and
received her visitor with every courtesy.
“I have called on you, Mrs. Leese,” said
the journalist, “ for confirmation of a state-
ment that has reached me:—That for a
long time you were a great sufferer from in-
digestion or dyspepsia and made quite a sen-
sational recovery by taking Cl ement’s Tonic. ”
“That is quite true,” replied Mrs. Leese.
“Would you be good enough to give me
the details ? ” asked the reporter.
“Certainly; and you may be sure the
circumstances are such that I am never
likely to forget them. We were living at
Nyngan at the time, a town with a fearful
summer heat and most uninteresting
surroundings. I think these circumstances
combined to start my malady. Eirat the
great heat, next the want of exercise, for
there was no inducement to go driving
walking in that bare, monotonous place.’
And I am quite aware that in my sedentary
profession some pi lysicalexcrcise is absolutely
essentia 1 to preserve good health. My
sickness came on gradually, and for some
time ouito unperceived, and for a good time
after the first symptom of trouble I gave
it no thought or attention —I have always
been careless of myself in that way. At
last the insidious trouble could be no longer
and I recognised that I was in a'
very bad way. My whole digestive system
seemed to be thoroughly disorganised and
run down. After eating, or trying to eat,
I experienced all over my body a fearful
sense of fulness ha: d to describe in words.
It was as though up to the throat I was
distended to the point of bursting, a stifling,
straining sensation with pain in the. neck.
And on my chest there was a feeling of
great weight as though an immense load
lay there trying to suffocate me. At my
back, between my shoulder blades, there
was a constant dull aching pain, and lower
down there was another intolerable pain ia
the right side below the ribs. But this was
rot enough—my cup of misery had to be
Ailed to the brim ; my bead constantly
ached and to my disordered vision all
outward objects seemed to go round and
round in endless revolution. My days and
nights were passed in misery and despair.
Then I would long for food with the desire
of great appetite ;"but when it was brought
anil waiting for me I could not eat it.
And how I longed for rest, for a good sound
sleep once again, and escape, if only for a
little time, from my pains and miseries;
but the sleep would nob come. Daily I got
steadily worse and worse, my mind Idled
with gloom and dreadful forebodings, I
came to that pass as to utterly despair of
myself aud the future.”
“ What remedies did you seek, Mrs.
Leese ?” enquired the reporter.
(■ %“ A doctor treated me for some time,
trying first one thing and then another, but
none of his medicines gave me any
permanent relief, my malady seeming
altogether beyond his shill. Then I tried a
variety of remedies so numerous that now I
could not count them, lb appeared 1 was
doomed, that in the whole world nothing
would cure, that my state was beyond
human aid. It chanced one day that my
husband had a fortunate inspiration—he said
to me:—‘Why don't you try Clements
Tonic? you have heard what a splendid
medicine it is, and wlmt wonderful cures it
has effected;perhaps it would cure you.’ ”
“ And did you take Mr. Leese's advic* ?”
enquired the reporter. v
“Yes, and thankful I am to say I did,
for when I had purchased a large bottle and
soon after I experienced a wonderful relief
from all my suffering, and a change so
sudden for the better that was hardly
credible to anyone who had not experienced
it. And as I continued taking Clements
Tonic my serious troubles diminished.
The terrible feeling of inflation, that had
almost stilled me, subsided and troubled me
no more ; the dull pain between my shoulder
blades passed away. My appetite returned
to its normal condition and I could eat and
drink with relish. About the same time
my headache left me, also the disordered
vision that caused all objects in my range
of vision to appear whirling round. Tlefore
taking Clements Tonic the slightest worry
was distressing to me, causing violent
headache, arising, without doubt, from my
shattered nervous system ; but now I was
so braced and strengthened that this
symptom passed with all my other troubles,
and i was once more my old self again.” *
“Then I may count you, Mrs. Leese,”
enquired the reporter, “as one of the large'
majority who swear by Clements Tonic ? ”
■4* “ You may. I know what it can do, and
what it has done. I have constantly
recommended Clements Tonic to my friends
and customers in all cases I thought.it
would benefit.”
“ May I make this testimony public, Mrs.
Leese?”
“You are very welcome, and you may
add that I shall be pleased to confirm my
wordstoanypersonwhomayread what 1 have
said, asd who will take the trouble tq call
at my residence.”

and also contains my full permission to publish tin

be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
One thousand Maorisand 10,000 Europeans
are drawing Old Age pensions in M.L.
Griffiths, M.P. (S.A.), the Westralian
Separationist champion in London, writes to an
Adelaide friend that C.J.’s Way, Darley, and
Griffith will catch it red-hot from the Federal
delegates when they get back to Australia.
Photographer Mason has been commis-
sioned by Vic. Govt, to travel after N.S.W.
Oliver and snapshot the characteristics of N.S.W.
towns which claim to become the Federal capital.
Oliver’s written digest will therefore be supple-
mented by Mason’s pictures.
More people (49,000) voted in favor of
Household Suffrage at the S.A. Referendum a
year ago than are on the Leg. Council roll (48,000).
Yet the Tory Register impudently asserts that
the demand for the broader Council franchise
comes from a minority of the electors.
If the S.A. Council election contest had
Listed but a short time longer it would have grown
most exciting, for apart from Symon’s speech
there were several other fights coming along during
the last days of the campaign. Had Kingston
been on the spot he would have made things hum.
Symon, Q.C. (S.A.), points out that it
was the combination of Miss Shaw, the London
Times, and Joe Chamberlain who brought about
the Jameson Raid and the Transvaal War, and he
considers it significant that they should be joined
again in the conspiracy acainst the Commomvealh
Bill.
Adelaide Register, which boasts of its
fairness in political matters, carefully cut out of
Symon’s speech last week all the appeals he made
to his hearers to show their loyalty to Australia’s
cause by voting for Kingston and Attorney-
General Gordon at the Southern District Council
election.
Melbourne people pick upon ex-Attorney-
General Isaacs as the man who has fomented the
Privy Council trouble in England. Isaacs went
to London long before there was any chance of
the Tramway appeal coming off, and it is averred
that he has whispered much into the ear of Cham-
berlain and others.
How the money goes. A man in a
N.S.W. country town (Mudgee) who broke a
window valued at half-a-crown was committed for
trial. His was the only case, yet a Judge, Crown-
Prosecutor, and others came from Sydney and a
jury was impanelled— merely to hear accused
plead guilty. Cost to the country, £270 !
A Sydney Daily Telegraph correspon-
dent maunders about “ our national and natural
right of appeal to the Privy Council of the Em-
pire.” r lhere is no such “natural and national
right.” Not natural, because created by statute ;
and not national, because the 40,000,000 inhabit-
ants of the British Islands do not possess it.
The C.E. and R.C. chaplains of 40 N.S W.
gaols draw Govt, salaries but no Presbyterian or
Wesleyan chaplain, although doing similar work,
gets a penny from Govt. “Wully” Affleck pro-
poses to force the Govt, next session to put the
latter workmen on the pay-sheet. The proposal
i s equitable. Pay any, pay all proportionated

"Do you know, ” said the passenger with egg on his
whiskers, “do you know what I would do if I
was Lord Roberts and got a hold of old Kruger?
I'd make him dig his own grave; then I'd
make him shoot himself in the back, and when
he’d tumbled into the grave he’d dug for him-
self, Ed make him fill it up! And,” continued
the man with egg on his whiskers, “you can
tell him I said so, if you like !”
According to London Times Kimberley
correspondent (as piously quoted by Sydney
Church Standard), one of the Boers said to him
that “the day of intercession appointed in the
English Church marked the change in our for-
tunes.” And yet the Boers had been praying
hard from the beginning ? Was Providence glad
of a change ?
Many civic potentates in and about Syd-
ney have owed, and still owe, their municipalities
huge sums for arrears of rates, much of which is
no longer recoverable. One of these persons
when sued for long-due arrears, had the cheek to
plead the Statute of Limitations as a bar to their
recovery by the criminally-dilatory body which
was the creditor.

According to the Australian Christian
World, “ the reason we are so well supplied with
‘tote-shops’ is that there is such a thing as legal
betting, that helps to foster and increase this ille-
gal betting.” Just so. If there were no laws,
nothing would be illegal. Therefore the best way
to abolish illegal betting would be to have no
betting-laws. Cock-eyed logic this !
The commutation of the death sentence on
Samuel Ensor, last week, saves Broken Hill for
the second time from disgrace of an execution.
John Vlarke, the only other capitally-convicted
Broken Hillite, escaped also by commutation—
Vlarke to five years, Ensor to life. Both cases
were rather bad ones, but in each case the jury
recommended mercy, and the Govt, is to be con-
gratulated on having regarded the recommenda-
tion.
In the Commons, lately, was made a state-
ment which should interest those Australians who
whoop for a share in the “responsibilities of the
Empire.” “Apart from
our trade and the pro-
tection of our colonies,”
said Bowles, M.P., “we
arc bound by treaties to
defend the international
neutrality of Belgium,
Luxembourg, Switzer-
land, and the lonian Is-
lands ; and of Sweden
against Russia. Also the
Independence of Turkey,
Greece, Chusan, and Por-
tugal.” The speaker
might have added to
the list Afghanistan, the
integrity of which John
Bull has guaranteed, and
Asia Minor, for the good
government of which
Bull, by the Cyprus Con-
vention, made himself
responsible ; also Egypt
and the Soudan. The
prospect of Australia
having to bleed for the
maintenance of all this
should be sufficient for
even Dick Seddon, John
See, and G. Republican
Dibbs.
In parallel columns
of Sydney Telegraph
Dr. Ashburton Thomp-
son assures the public that
Haffkine’s prophylactic gives twelve months’ pro-
tection from plague—and Dr. Carmichael, Hono-
lulu plague-doctor, says the remedy is effective
for 44 from 30 to 40 days.” Which shows how little
is really known about the efficacy of the preven-
tive.
Dear Bulletin.— When the railway from
Cooma to the Vic. border, via Bombala and Dele-
gate, is built, there will be a gap of 30 miles of
good auriferous, agricultural and pastoral country
between it and the Mt. Deddick railway. Then
there will be an agitation to connect and make an
interprovincial railway of it, and Govt, will have
to buy the Mt. Deddick Co. out.— Duncan Mac.
“ The critics of the Commonwealth Bill,
defeated at the Referendum, have yet been persis-
tent enough to frustrate the mission of the dele-
gates in so far as it aimed at securing the complete
acceptance of the federal constitution by the
Imperial authorities, subject to such action as our
Federal Government would take in pursuance of
their wishes. The Federal Convention, the Refer-
endum, the Parliaments, and the Premiers in con-
ference have united in saying one thing, and Mr.
Chamberlain has said another, and so the matter
stands.”— Sydney Morning Herald.
When Ballance started on his long journey
of reform, the Tory press of Maoriland rose up to
howl about the sensitiveness of Capital,
and protested that, if democratic
legislation were • indulged in
said C. would rise up hastily
and flee the country. Huge
deputations of “all the
best men ” waited upon
the Premier to point
out this appalling
fact, but Ballance
only sighed and said
“No such luck!”
And there wasn’t.
And in South Africa
at present we are
having another ex-
hibition of “sensi-
tiveness of capital.”
At the Kimberley
Relief banquet
Colossus -R h odes
calmly remarked
that De Beers Co. intended to fortify Kim-
berley and do away for ever with the chance
that a sudden upheaval or racial war would en-
danger the diamond-mines. And yet it is possible
that the huge Syndicate will get its way, as in
South Africa De Beers seems to be a law unto
itself.
While N.S.W. nominee Leg. Council is
unpaid it is futile to expect from it any hearty
response to the demands of public opinion. Its
natural tendency will be to voice sentiments
antagonistic to those of the paid branch of the
Legislature. No country in the world has success-
fully worked two Chambers thus diversely based.
It may be, as it has been in the past, that the
Leg. Council will on a certain subject move in
advance of the paid Leg. Assembly. The present
N.S.W. Council gave a couple of instances of this
last session. But these are exceptional generosi-
ties —and the exceptions which prove the rule.
An unpaid Parliament sitting beside a paid one
feels it incumbent upon it to conserve
privileges and play stubborn brake to the
wheel of progress.
“ The colonies,” said Chamberlain
iu the House of Commons on April 3,
“although they reverence the person of
the monarch, and are devoted in their
loyalty to the Crown, nevertheless are,
with that exception, independent and
sister-nations.” In that case “ the colonies”
have the right to make laws for their
domestic government, and to decide the
interpretation of those laws, without inter-
ference by British tribunals —and The
Bulletin cites Chamberlain in condem-
nation of his own meddling with the Com-
monwealth Bill.
Rev. Dr. Jeffries,, in an excellent
Sydney address, makes the sound consti-
tutional point that legislation and justice
should be strictly separated; and inasmuch
as the members of the Judicial Committee
of the Privy Council may at the same time
sit in the House of Lords it is a tribunal
constitutionally unsafe. And the pro-
posed new imperial Court of Appeal
appears to perpetuate the same fault in a
graver form.
Dear Bulletin. — Large audience at a
recent Sydney suburban “patriotic” concert
enthusiastically cheered “Soldiers of the Queen ”
and “Sons of the Sea”; warmly bi-encored
“ Absent-Minded Beggar,” and faintly applauded
“Advance Australia Fair”—the best part song
of the evening. Are Australians going to be more
British than the Britons when the Vampire
Calls ?— Bennie.
Symon, Q.C., astonished all S.A. Tories
by coming out at the last moment as a champion
of Kingston at the Council elections. They were
articled clerks together in the office of Chief Jus-
tice Way, with whom Symon was afterwards in
partnership. This, however, did not prevent him
from bitterly condemning the intrigues of Way,
Lainington, and other Imperialists against the
Federation Bill.
The English illustrated weekly is one of
the most potent bulwarks of Royalty : it is mostly
Royalty recorded, “ pictured,” apotheosized. Yet
an enterprising English printing house has
brought out an illustrated weekly entitled The
King. Is this in indecent anticipation of Queen
Victoria s demise and the early coming of
“Tummy?” So much for illustrated loyalty—
the patriotism of “posters”—and printer’s ink !
There was serious dissension in the Hol-
der Ministry over the great Southern District con-
test. Ere Kingston left, his old colleagues pro-
mised, if he didn’t return in time, to do his cam-
paigning for him. The Tory crowd decided to
plump for Stirling, whom Kingston was so anxious
to depose, and thereupon Attorney-General Gor-
don pleaded that his seat was endangered. As a
result, the original idea of stumping the country
was abandoned.
A feature of the election for S.A. Leg.
Council was the attitude of Symon, Q.C., who
deliberately broke away from the Tory party to
support Kingston’s candidature. For the last
three jrears they have not been on speaking terms,
“ but, 55 said Symon, “the issue is now that of the
nation, and Mr. Kingston has stood firm for the
rights of the people." Svmon’s powerful speech,
denouncing Way, Baker, and the rest of the
Backstairs party, 5 ' gained Kingston hundreds of
votes.
Concerning an official dispute :
Vic. Railway-Commissioner Mathieson has just scored
one off the Postal Department: A telephone-line was
erected along the railway between Foster and Fish Creek
(with the Railway Commissioners’ permission), but when
all was ready for use the railway magnate said : “ No you
don’t, unless I get half the returns.” Deputy PG.
Outtrim was aghast, but seeing his opponent held the
winning card, offered 25 per cent. But the Com. stuck-
out for equal shares, and as neither side will give way the
P.O, loses £lOO (cost of line), ami the public, as usual,
suffers by the quanel between two State departments
supposed to be run for its benefit. Little sympathy is
felt for the P. 0., it being noted for working points on
its constituents.
In arguing the Joshua Percy Josephson
case before Sydney Banco Court, last week, Mr.
O'Connor, Q.C., threw a vivid sidelight on the
political patronage game of past years. The point
at issue w;us: Had J. P. Josephson, a temporary
civil servant of nearly 30 years’ standing, and
whose office was alxdished in ’!)5, a valid claim for
a pension ? The distinction between a temporary
and full-fledged civil servant was set out as being
that the latter was appointed by the Govemor-in-
Council and duly gazetted, wfit jioas the former
was simply dropped into a billet at a trifle a week
by some influential politician. Mr. Josephson
was, for instance, appointed by politician J. H.
Young, at (ss. a day, in 1872, and Mr. O’Connor
asserted that 48 per eent. of the entire body of
civil servants were similarly appointed.
