Since I commenced this scribble of impressions and re-
flections I have been in two battles. Small, it is true,
when one looks at the numbers engaged, the losses in-
volved, the results achieved. But battles where the
shrapnel screamed for miles, and burst in clouds of blue
smoke and brown dust against rocky bills and kranzes
held by men whose blood colored the shattered stones ;
where bullets came in little clouds like rain-drops blown
in windy gusts from wet trees, kicking up a score or so of
dusty holes in the burnt ground ; where men were dying
suddenly with tiny punctures in their foreheads, or lin-
geringly with great pieces of flesh and bone torn violently
from chest and shoulder and thigh, past all patching or
any help. I have sat eating a needed meal of bully-beef,
dry bread, and whisky mingled with water opaque with
dust and filth and straw, at the door of a farmhouse, with
our chimney carried away bv a shell, and a Red Cross flag
fluttering uncertainly from the other—a farmhouse trans-
formed into a hospital ! Inside, in rooms from whfch
everything superfluous had been stripped and thrown in a
heap beneath the windows -pictures, drapery, and furni-
ture, ornaments —on mattresses laid upon the floor and

redolent of antiseptics-, poor stalwart fellows, with heads,
or arms,-or knees, or wrists, or ankles swathed in tell-tale
bandages, groaning a little, sleeping a little, cursing a
little ; all, gentle and simple, with that strange, tired
look in their eyes that speaks the thought that
nothing they have hoped for, nothing they have gained
or undergone, is worth the present home of pain and
misery and dejection. And in the kitchen beyond a
floor strewn with oaten-straw, a deal table covered with
waterproof sheets, a naked figure, his senses happily
drowned in ether, a group of surgeons in breeches
gaiters, and flannel shirts, their sleeves rolled to the
elbows, saving a life by taking off a mangled limb. As I
have stood talking to an officer who-was waiting to know
whether his wounded sergeant will survive the operation
an orderly has passed bearing in a basin full of blood the
leg that half an hour since gripped the saddle-flap as he
rode gallantly into action. Down in the hollow a fatigue-
party was digging a trench ; but the line of waterproof
sheets stretched over vague suggestive shapes hard by
guarded by a couple of sentries with fixed bayonets and
hovered round by a khaki-clad chaplain, said plainly that
the trench was a grave.

April Contemporary has a tip for the
Popedom. The four Cardinals apparently in it
are Rampolla, Parocchi, Vannutelli, and Svampa.
But a surprise may be sprung : Capecelatro would
be a great favorite for advanced years. Parocchi
looks to have the best show—a man of distinguished
intellect and a degree of Machiavellian pliability.
Rampolla is the out-and-out Tory, now next to
the Pope, as secretary of state. Vannutelli is the
colorless man, and not of high calibre. Svampa is
the youngest cardinal, fouijueuse, as the French
say, boiling over with zeal and energy.
Machiavellian motives are suggested for
the Bushmen-Contingent muddling of Vic. Govt.
Seeing that George Turner appointed Major-Gen.
Downes to be Commandant of the local Army,
whereas M‘Lean would probably have hearkened
to the prayers of the Col. Bingham faction, the
“Ants” have put forward the theory that the
Bushmen Corps mismanagement was a political
means of discrediting Downes. Which looks very
like a whale. There is more appearance of truth
in the allegation that the selection of Bushmen
officers was mainly a matter of influence and—er,
grease.
The Bulletin has just received from
Maoriland Dept, of Labor a copy of its mcnchly
journal (price 2d.) and a publication every working
man should read. Compared to ttie Fortunate
Isles of Dick Seddon, Australia seems indeed a
benighted and uncivilised land, for on this con-
tinent, when the worker has a dispute with his
owner, he must either submit to the will of the
latter, or take the sack, or organise a strike and
be a marked man for the rest of his life. In
M.L. they settle industrial disputes without de-
priving the youngsters of so much as their weekly
lolly-money. On 24th April the printers of Wel-
lington hauled their employers before the Court
of Conciliation, which made certain recommenda-
tions for the working of the trade and gave both
sides notice that if its recommendations were not
acted on by 4th May it would report that it was
unable to effect a settlement —in which case the
Court of Arbitration would inquire into the dis-
Kand deliver a verdict which should be as
ing as a decision of the Privy Council. Also,
since Sept., ’9B, the boot-trade of Otago has been
working under an award of 26 clauses delivered
by the Court of Arbitration and to which the
dourt has just made three additions. Besides
accounts of these two cases, the little journal
contains a mass of information about the state of
the labor-market in every part of Maoriland,
judging by which the country is in a fairly
prosperous state, and ruled by a government
which watches its people carefully from cradle to
grave.
The general feeling in England was that
Henniker Heaton ought to have got the Post-
inaster-Generalship, in succession to the Duke of
Norfolk, gone to fight.
The recent Fraser shooting case raised a
fine point in surgical ethics which has never been
decided. When the victim was taken to the
Alfred Hospital, Melb., with a bullet in his head,
the hospital staff determined that he must be tre-
phined, though they believed that in any case he’d
not live more than a few hours. Fraser, being a
surgeon himself, knew that this operation is more
often fatal than not, and he refused absolutely to
submit to it. The medicos sought the opinion of
Attorney-General Isaacs as to their power in the
matter, and were told that they might proceed
with the operation if it was necessary to save life.
By this time, Fraser, under the influence of opi-
ates, had become partly unconscious, and it was
alleged or pretended that he had given his consent.
His skull was then cut open, and a sciograph taken
of the bullet in the centre of the brain, from which
it was impossible to extract without causing death.
The wound was closed, and, contrary to expecta-
tion, the sufferer recovered. In the parallel case
of Greer, victim of the Melb. Opera House tragedy
a number of years ago, death followed quickly
upon trephining. If all aeounts are true there is
too much surgery in our midst.

In Auckland (M.L.) district was recently
discovered the last living lineal descendant of Sir
Francis Drake. Mrs. John Angelo (nee Drake)
the wife of a settler, has applied to Chancery for
any moneys lying to her credit, and has also for-
warded to the Queen a letter giving full particulars
of her (Mrs. A.’s) ancestry.
An influential committee of N.S.W. citi-
zens, headed by Lord Beauchamp, is raising funds
(subscriptions limited to a guinea each) for the
purpose of having a portrait of Chief Justice
Darley painted and hung in the Supreme Court.
Subscriptions may be sent to the private secretary
at Government House. It is understood that the
Chief Justice has expressed a desire that the com-
mission be entrusted to Mr. John Longstaff.
Reading between the lines of the latest
accounts of Lord Methuen’s disaster at Magers-
fontein it is not hard to see that the position ought
to have been taken, and that the defeat was owing
to the unlucky accident of the Highland Brigade
running off the field after meeting a withering fire
and losing their leader. The Highlanders are the
finest fighters in the world, but their liability to
panic is rather greater than that of other British
troops. The Duke of Wellington used to say “I
don’t object to the regiment that runs away, but
to the regiment that refuses to return when called
upon.” That shows that running away in war is
a much more common occurrence than the special
correspondents would have us believe. Lord
Roberts has said notning against Methuen, while
his opinion as to the cause of the repulse has been
carefully suppressed.
On a land-reclamation matter :
Editor Bulletin.—An inspection of Koo-wee-rup Swamp
(Vic.) is a trenchant lesson on the awful incompetence of
departmentalism. The swamp embraces 53,000 acres,
and over £200,000 has been spent on it—of which £lOO,OOO
is practically wasted. The drains have been badly con-
structed and pcrpjtually need patching and repairing.
Tile swamp-land is peaty, and the fool departmental
ollicers have planted most of their bridges and culverts
on a level with the water-soaked soil, so that when the
summer comes and scorches up the swamp the said
bridges are left standing disconsolate 3 or 4 ft. above the
ground, while the culverts look like aerial bridges. More-
over, in all these miles of swamp there is not a single road,
and though, this year, the few settlers —battling pluckily
for a living—raised some 10,000 tons of potatoes and
onions they couldn’t get them to market, and last Good
Friday’s Hood destroyed the lot! Of course, no Minister
is directly rcsponsiole for these blunders, but a chief of
department who allows himself to be thus tamely “ run ”
by his subordinates is a dummy and an exc rescence.—F.O.

Discussing late attacks of French publi-
cists on Queen Victoria, a Sydney Q.C. dug up
several cases in which English courts prosecuted
when British subjects libelled foreign rulers. One
Peltier was prosecuted for libelling Napoleon, and
was found guilty in London, but escaped punish-
ment owing to Napoleon a second time declaring
war. Lord George Gordon was found guilty in
London of libel on Marie Antoinette ; and Vint, a
publisher, was convicted in 1801 for libelling the
Emperor of Russia.
Sample of clerical humor, from the re-
ligious column of Sydney Telegraph :
A good story is told of a Presbyterian Moderator, place
of abode not mentioned. Years ago, in a clerical
gathering, a brother had been advocating a very literal
interpretation of the book of Revelation, and when it
came to be Dr. Sample’s turn to express his thoughts he
figuratively brought down the house by remarking,
“ Brethren, I am afraid of this literal theory when applied
strictly. For instance, take the woman who sat upon
seven hills ; taken literally, this statement calls for a
sitting capacity beyond all precedent.” There was no
attempt to argue the point.
And yet the point might have been argued. Any
woman could sit on seven hills at once if she were
large enough and the hills small enough. Besides,
it isn’t absolutely asserted that she sat on all the
hills at once. Even a very ordinary woman can
sit on seven consecutive hills.
“Rodent”: There are about 100 species
of rats, and probably Sydney has not got them
all. The sort which disturbed the sleep of George
I. were a black kind, now almost extinct. They
were ousted by our common or garden variety,
erroneously called the Norwegian rat. The giant
rat of Malabar is 24in. long—one-half body, the
other half tail. The hamster species swarms in
the southern provinces of Russia; also in Hungary
and Germany. For winter use they store up in
their burrows from 12 to 1001 b. of grain in the ear
and seeds in pods, all well cleaned and dried.
This rat lias a pouch in each chepk, not seen when
empty. On one of the creatures being dissected
the pouches were found to be filled with young
French beans, packed ono upon the other so
closely and skilfully that the most expert fingers
could not have economised the receptacle to
greater advantage. When taken out and laid
loosely they formed a heap three times the bulk
of the animal’s body ! The economic rat of the
American and Asiatic shores of the Arctic Ocean
excavate commodious lodging-vaults and store-
houses. In Scotland rats used to be regarded as
symbols of witchcraft ; and the ancient bards of
Ireland had the power of rhyming rats to death.
A reference to the practice occurs in these lines
from “ Rhythmes against Martin Mar-Prelate ”: —
I am a rimer of the Irish race,
And have already rimdc thee staring mad ;
But if thou cease not thy bold jests to spread,
I’ll never leave till I have rimde thee dead.
Rhyming to death is a power that has continued
to this day. Why not put the poets on the job,
and save the small-boy rat-catchers from the
risks they are running ?

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Tatt.’s May meeting at Randwick on Saturday
didn’t draw a big crowd, but most of those who went out
weren’t sorry, for, bar one, all winners were well backed.
Ball opened with hurdles. Old Honeydew went out top
of market—6 to 4 on. Deemster, 4 to 1 against, won
easily. Rival fell at first jump, and the big grey, States-
man’ll, followed suit at the back of the course, breaking
his own neck and the collarbone of the jockey (Edwams).
Flying Handicap was voted a moral for Leapfrog (Hop-
scotch’s brother), who had done wonders on the track.
Backers fell over each other for 6 to 4 and then laid a shade
of odds on— maiden and all though he was. At the
start “8 to 1 bar one” was the burden of the leather-
lunged’s song. But a 15 to 1 chance romped in an
easy winner giving nothing else a show; the hot pot
could’nt get nearer than fourth. Bob s win wasn t looked
for by owner or trainer, but nathless a few big bets were
booked. Tatt’s Stakes brought out a field of 21, and the
Newcastle nag Allinga—backed heavily from 12 s down to
5 to 2—landed a pile for her party m a common canter.
Nothing better than 5 to 2 was going about Melodian at
the start in Winter Stakes, and little more was ever
offered. He got his head in front at the jump and won
easing off—this in striking contrast with his performance
in Rous Handicap at recent A.J.O. Autumn meet., when
he finished one of the last couple among 13 runners. Alice
Mostyn was served strong for the Mile Welter. Threes
were the longest odds obtainable at any time. Punters
took even money freely for a while, but she started at
5 to 2, and passed the post first by three-quarters of a
length after a gruelling struggle with Foley. Several of
those behind were evidently only out for an airing.
• • * *
Writer ran out to Kensington, Sydney, so-called
pony races last Friday. Pony races! Horses with breed-
ing, and big enough to win Melb. Cup. Acorn, the cham-
pion, is a picture—a three-year-old colt by Abercorn
from Penitence, stands half-an-inch under 15 hands, and
ran his six furlongs easily in 1.17}, with 8.9 up. As
a comparison, at Randwick next day Bob, 4 years, with
8.3, took 1.18 to run his six furlongs in the Flying Handi-
cap, a performance which immediately made him a red-
hot favorite at 3 to 1 for Sydney Turf Club Royal Stakes.
Some other sires represented are Carbine, Manton, Realm,
Puschka, Clan Stuart, Fusilier, Beauchamp, Russley,
Metal, Niagara, Lochiel—in fact, the blood on both sides
of the house, for which the Britishers plank down their
thousands.
• * *
Just before the City and Suburban was run
English racing prophets spoke of The Grafter as a “ has-
been ” likely to prove as disappointing as Survivor.
• • * *
The impolitic action of the Hawkesbury Show
people last year in placing visiting trotting horses on
prohibitive marks bore fruit this season, as the show was
left severely alone by the owners of good animals, and
the contests confined to local horses. Time was
when Hawkesbury Trot was a big event, but this year it
was little better than a farce, one horse distancing the
field, and the best he could do was about a 3.20 gait.
Matt. Dunhey, the well-known steeplechase rider, caused
some diversion by mounting an alleged mule outlaw ; but
all the moke could do was to give a few big jumps,
and Matt, earned a very soft two guineas.
* * * *
The generous M.L. metropolitan trotting-club
did a very safe thing in offering a £lOO purse for any
horse breaking the Australasian mile-record. The best
M.L. trotters are far from equal to it. And it was well
known that champion Fritz would not be sent over to
improve on his own brilliant time, 2.ISj.
* * * *
Recent Adelaide racing furnished a splendid
advt. for the tote, and Melb. men were once more im-
pressed. An investment of £1 on every horse running
during the three-days’ meeting would have produced a
net profit of £lOl. Whereas Moses and co. won’t pay-out
more than 20 to 1, the machine twice paid over 60, and
for Adelaide Cup returned £27 for £l. The good old
machine is returning to the public what would have gone,
in the old days, in buying diamond rings for Ikey.
Recent race-meeting at Norseman (W.A.) takes
the cake. Previous heavy rain had played up with the
course. One portion was a regular quagmire. Several
nags fell there during the afternoon, and—remarkable
coincidence —all were from the same stable. “Snip”
(running in the interests of a syndicate) was a starter
three times, and on each occasion fell at the same place
with same rider up. When Snip went down the third
time the order of the programm had been altered to let
him start in the last race.
* * * *
‘ ‘ Old-Timer” : Saw battle between pugs. Nathan
(Melb.) and Casey (Sydney) at Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney)
Saturday night. Nathan’s frank good-looking boyish face
made it seem cruel to put him to the game, but when he
got going the tiger showed out. Casey, blocky-shaped,
good-tempered and grit to the core, took much the
larger share of gruel during the first dozen rounds till
Nathan’s stamina petered out. Even then the Melb. lad’s
left operated freely round about Casey’s face. Nathan
slipped or was rushed off his feet frequently towards the
end of the 20 rounds stipulated, but was never knocked
down. Referee called it a draw, but if points went for
anything writer would like to wager that Nathan had a
wide margin in his favor.
Little Pluto, the colored pug. who fought G-riffo
100 odd rounds at the old Melb. Athletic Club, is back
from Klondike more prosperous than ever. Pluto is
almost the only Vic. pug. who has come out of the game
well. Going to the West early he made enough by fights
to get into several tip-top shows, and, about a year later,
returned to Melb. with a tolerable fortune. He bought
several terrace properties, took unto himself a handsome
white wife, built a fine villa at Middle Park, and used to
run the nattiest dog-cart about Melb. But the spirit of
mining had entered Pluto, and on the outbreak of Klon-
dike he had to cross over. Originally a wharf laborer,
Pluto at the age of 30 is a prosperous retired gentleman.
Before he is 40, he will probably be a millionaire with a
seat in the Senate.
• * * *
Trouble in South Africa doesn’t seem to make
much difference to pugs. Melbourne Tom Duggan and
Jack Lalor (a local smasher) had a go at the Cape early
last month for £l5O and the middleweight championship.
Fight was sensational from inception to conclusion of a
score of rounds. Duggan had his man very weak, but
Lalor’s cleverness saved him. The Australian won on
points. * * »
Late files tell that Australian middleweight
champion Tim Murphy has been matched to fight crack
Californian Neill at ’Frisco, Aug. 27. Pugs, to get 72 per
cent, of gross “gate.” Clubs competed keenly for the
battle, sealed bids being sent in. Successful tenderers
offered 2 per cent, more than next highest. Winner is to
get 70 and loser 30 per cent, of fighters’ whack.
’Rastlers Harry Dunn and giant Teuton Ren-
nert are not likely to get to it. Rennert challenged in
Sydney. Dunn planked down a “ pony ” with Greymouth
(M.L.) Star and expressed willingness to find £2OO if
necessary. Rennert has since been dumb as an oyster.
Marvellous how money up and a businesslike attitude
silences ’em—particularly the wrestling bluffer.
* * » *
Dave Strickland, who came across from Menzies,
W.A., at Easter, to compete in two foot-races—Stawell
Easter Gift, 130yds. handicap (£7O), and the Eight-Hours’
Sheffield Handicap £3o)—cantered home a winner in both
events. His only performance hitherto was his annexa-
tion of a £l5 handicap in W.A., three years ago, and it
was but natural that the best peds. in Australia, whom he
met at Stawell, in their anticipatory tips, gave him no
mention. When, however, the lanky, 13st., fair-haired,
modest youth of 22 won his several heats, almost every
other man left in the final accused him of “cronk” per-
formances, threatened to have his scalp, and declared
that, if he won, they’d have him “ rubbed out.” On the
following day, however, when the final was run, these
same accusers had the nerve to approach him and say,
“ Save a ‘ tenner ’ with you, Strickland ? ” “ No, thanks,”
was the reply ; “ I’ll have the whole hog or nothing ! ” and
now, in the teeth of protests and interprovincial enquiries,
Strickland is still calmly waiting to receive “ the whole
hog,” and it’s a guinea to a gooseberry he gets it.

certain big athletic olub asking ped. to send “sisters’
names (if any).” You say, “ Why- not cousins and aunts.”
Ped. did send cousins and aunts, also grandfathers, grand-

to connect him with “Hutchens or Price” (presumably
Harry and Joe of that ilk). Why not black Charlie
Samuels ?—Sponddlix.
* * * *
Sydney football season opened Saturday, and
district matches were in evidence for the first time.
Players lacked condition woefully and combination was
almost as bad. Of first-grade matches, “Uni’s.” looked
to have a cinch on South Sydney, but latter sprung a big
surprise and won by 11 to 5. Glebe scored 2 points more
than E. Suburbs ; Newtown went under to W. Suburbs
(6 to nil), and N. Sydney won from Balmain in the last
few minutes by Bto 5. This match was played on the
loser’s ground. Balmain barrackers made a dead set
against the referee, and gave him a parlous time from end
to end. At the close he had to be escorted away, and
could only get out of the suburb disguised. If a referee
at a bruising match were treated in this way there’d be a
howl throughout the land.
Hawksburn, the erstwhile popular juniors, suc-
ceeded in winning the Vic. cricket championship amongst
the senior teams this year. With the usual conservatism
of selection committees, little notice was taken of the
coming premiers, and out of the score of players who re-
presented Victoria against N.S.W., S.A., and Tasmania,
the club had not a solitary rep.
* * m *
English cricket season opened on Easter Monday.
Briggs has quite recovered, and plays again for Lancashire,
which will have MacLaren right through. Major Poore
and F. S. Jackson are in S’Africa. A. E. Stoddart has
finally retired from big cricket. G. L. Jessop has been
elected captain of Gloucester, vice Troup. Season will be
dull, with nothing but county fixtures, and M.C.C. alone
expects takings at Lords to drop several thousands.
* * ■* *
E. W. Milligan, one of the best of Yorkshire’s
cricketers, was killed in the last attempt by Plumer to
relieve Mafeking. Milligan played for his county in 1899,
and did good work with both bat and ball, his fast bowl-
ing being frequently useful.
• * * *
New sculling luminary Tressider figured at
Port Hunter regatta (N.S.W.), recently, and had a big
task set him in the Professional Scullers’ Handicap, but,
all the same, another 100yds. would, perhaps, have seen
him win. A. F. Chapman (scr.) passed the flag first, with
Tressider (38sec. behind) second, and Pellman (15sec.
behind) third. Tressider caught Pullman at the mile,
where Chapman led by 15 lengths. Half-a-mile further
it looked as if Tressider had a chance of just snatching
the race out of the fire, but Chapman set-to so effectually
that he reached the finish a bare length to the good.
Tressider’s efforts stamp him a champion—he pulled
up without having turned a hair.
# * » »
Turns out now that veteran Peter Kemp, who
trained the Wallsend (N.S.W.) sculler, Worboys, for
his match with Tressider, thought former had a soft snap,
and caused money to go on him. Two days before Wor-
boys went over the course very fast—and left the race
there. Worboys worked himself up to a high pitch of
anxiety just prior to the struggle, and old Peter’s hopes
fell. A brisk walk had a good effect, but when the big
Wallsender went to the start, he got a bad attack of the
shakes, which grew worse as Tressider gradually made up
the big handicap conceded. Had Worboys been cast in
the Bill Beach mould, the race would have been a moral
for him. Beach could go dead to sleep an hour before
his biggest matches.
* * * *
“Crab”: Prospects of a world’s sculling struggle
as far as Gaudaur (present holder) is concerned are appa-
rently remote. Hunter River (N.S.W.) crack, Towns,
writes from ’Ome that he has frequently offered to go to
Toronto and meet the Canadian if exes were allowed, but
Gaudaur won’t give a cent and refuses to row anywhere
bub at his own door (250 miles from Toionto Bay).
’Twould cost a lump to get there with boat and gear, but
if his benefit and that London Sportsman subscription
pan out, Towns will make the trip rather than let the
wily Canuck’s bluff go. The calm, dead waters of Rat
Portage will go against Towns, as he revels in it when
rough, but it’s odds on that the Cornstalk will have his
journey for nil, as Gaudaur evidently doesn’t want to pull
any more. Not improbable that another big sculling
race will be seen on the Parramatta ere long. Tressider,
who put Worboys down so easily t’other day, is eager to
have a cut at Stanbury, and the latter has said he doesn’t
mind tackling the game again provided his old backers
will come up. Tressider, ’bis said, can stake £2OO. Stan-
bury looks every bit as well as of yore, and is still far
from being old. Stanbury has been a Govt, contractor of
late and has chiefly been engaged on building telegraph-
lines.
* * * *
Plugger Bilmartin has been wbooping things
up a bit in W.A. A-top of Fremantle successes, he
scooped three and finished second in another out of five
events at Perth recently.
* * * •
Racing cyclists have strangled their game.
Firms retaining riders met in Melbourne t’other day and
practically decided not to go on, so that the abolition of
paid wheelmen is a “ cert.” An exodus follows in con-
sequence. Bill Martin goes to “ Yurrup ” from W.A.
Dusky-hued Steve Gordon lately left Sydney for ’Murka.
Walne is off ’Ome, and Bob Lewis talks of keeping him
company.
* • « •
Acting-Judge Johnston, at Bendigo County
Court, passed some pertinent remarks on the bike-race
subject. Will Matthews, runner-up in last Austral
Wheel Race, was sued by his trainer for a balance of £l2
due on a “one-third” agreement re the stake money.
Judge Johnston, ere finding for plaintiff with costs,
“ commented on the shameless manner in which Matthews
had admitted having competed dishonestly in various
events, and thereby deceived those people who were
foolish enough to back men of his stamp.” Are the com-
mittee of the L. V. W. listening, or is the “ sport ” so dirty
that they have given up all idea of cleansing it ?
* * • •
M. League of Wheelmen made a beautiful
bungle over the disqualification of Sutherland, picked to
go to Paris and “ passed out” for demanding “appearance-
money” from two sport bodies. Sutherland’s counsel
pointed out that the League had acted illegally and that
his client should have been heard in defence. Matter was
consequently re-opened, and a charge was also brought
against E. Reynolds and the Canterbury A. and 0. Club,
on the ground that the club had paid Reynolds’ boat-fare
from Auckland to race at their meeting. League con-
sidered that if Sutherland were suspended several other
riders and clubs would have to be similarly treated, re-
sulting in a deadlock in sport. Also, if the Canterbury
clubs were suspended, four or five members of council
would be prevented from acting. Ultimately decided that
the best way out would be to condone all breaches of
rules re exes and appearance-money up to date. Suther-
land and Reynolds will now visit Paris, and two Auck-
landers, Tierney and Draffin, may go “ on their own."
• • • *
Farce of League of M.L. Wheelmen disquali-
fying Sutherland for seeking “appearance-money” is
being laughed at everywhere. One could understand an
amateur being thrust aside for seeking appearance-money,
but the idea of disqualifying a professional for looking
after his living is a bit too much for the ordinary every-
day mind.

N. Bowling Association’s rink supremacy
was won by Balmain, Saturday, with 5 points (29-24).
City was the other club represented in the final. Last
year City Club owned all three blue-ribbon events ; this
season Balmain has taken two away—lndividual and
Rink. , . , ,
Final of Tas. Individual Bowling Championship,
played at Hobart the other day, was a game worth
seeing. Contestants were Mapley and Cnsp ; Mapley got
home last year. Crisp had held the title previously. Both
showed exceptional form. Up to the 36th end they had
tied four times and were 26 all. 40th end saw another tie
—3O all. Then came the tussle, and Crisp finished one
point ahead (31-30). m t *
Premier cueist Fred Weiss showed brilliant
form in Sydney last week, and emphasised his claim to
pre-eminence south of the line. Conceding 1000 in 2500
to that old warrior and ex-champion of the mother pro-
vince, Ted James, Weiss (first night) compiled breaks of
161, 150, 87, and 66 (unfinished). Scores then stood :
Weiss (in play), 834 ; James, 1271. James’ best were 41
and 35. Second night Weiss tacked 31 to his unfinished
66, and, playing sure and deadly, owned the table nearly
the whole of the evening ; his biggest breaks were 173,
126 (beautifully played from a double-baulk), 88, 83,74,60,
and several others in the 40’s and 50’s. Balls, as a rule,
were left safe. James got to the end of his tether with
46, 40, and 37. Scores : Weiss (in play), 1666 ; James,
1432. Friday night saw the end. Weiss fairly excelled
himself. Compilations of 125, 103, 86, 62, and 57 were
notched by him, and, finally, a remarkably quickly-put-
together 175. James did better this time with breaks of
77, 62, 44, and 34. Weiss won with 611 in hand over and
above the start of 1000 allowed. A match between Weiss
and Smith (Victoria’s crack) is on the tapis.
» • » •
The cable that Mr. Leslie Walford, Sydney,
had been winning at London (Eng.) Gun Club matches,
did not come as a surprise to Sydney shooters. Ere leav-
ing Sydney a few months ago, Mr. Walford showed
himself a good shot, and was improving every week. He
was on a very handy mark, and as the London and
Sydney Clubs are reciprocal in the matter of handicaps,
he must have had a good time against the Britishers.
* * « •
Sydney athletic clubs have surfeited the pug.-
going public with cheap and nasty novice tourneys ; nence
more often than not there are scores of empty benches
round the ring. Novices, too—that is novices with any
heart for the game—are becoming fewer every week, so
that events cannot be drawn out as before, which means
that profits are scant. One purse offered recently dwin-
dled down in fivers from £25 to £lO as the affair pro-
gressed, and the ultimate winner, it is stated, could only
be persuaded to refrain from “ ohucking up ” the whole
thing and “blabbing” to the papers by having a soft
snap in the semi-final guaranteed nim. Nice 1
* « * »
“F.”: Re rat-killing and sex-considerations.
Some 14 years ago, “ Montie,” an ex-London light-weight
pug. and dog-fancier, used to ran a most successful rat-
pit in Wakefteld-street, Auckland (M.L.). Every sport
brought his own dog, and there was healthy betting and
a jovial crowd. But none of us, with our crack “ foxies,”
could look near Montie’s own little toy bull-terrier,
Peter,” which had all his teeth drawn but the canines,
and never made two bites at a rat. When ratting was
over, for a little shilling pool Montie would put Peter and
his sister into the pit for a scrap. They boxed like two
Griffos, standing on their hind legs and sparring away till
one or other would get a “ holt,” and then they’d roll
over and worry. No consideration of sex—they went
“ all out."
* • * •
Apropos cyclist Hales’s gruelling grind of 100
miles a day (Sundays excepted) for 12 months, now in
progress at ’Ome and successful so far, a Yank is engaged
wheeling 100,000 in five years. He started four years ago,
and in the first 12 months covered 12,497 miles; next year,
26,252 were thrown behind ; in ’9B he traversed 30,025.
What was done last year hasn’t yet been given to the
hungering world.
Another Duke of Athol yarn: “We wasn’t
after the Dook at all; old Jimmy (Gemma-di-Vergy) was
our cut; but the old horse ’ud have nothing to ao with
strangers, and was playin’ up, so we left him an’ took the
Dook. We got frightened about the Dook, him bein’ so well
marked ; so we done for ’im, and fixed ’im up just as he
was found.”

Several gen’emen of color have joined the noble
army of punters, and at Randwick, on Saturday, they
made a big race with the Chinamen to be the first to have
their tenner on the good things. The white trash were
not in it with them.
V # * #
“ Scrip” : I came across a well-to-do individual
the other day who backs his fancies —if he has any—at
every race-meeting, no matter where held. He is a close
follower of the game, and goes simply on paper form.
Told me he hadn’t been on a racecourse for nine years !
A veritable stay-at-home backer.
• * * •
An unattractive-looking, ungroomed prad saun-
tered home in a two-mile trot at Ashburton (M.L.) lately,
and paid £26 12s. tote-div. Owner of second horse rushed
wildly to secretary, and protested on ground that win-
ner’s rider had weighed-in over weight without having
previously declared to that effect. ’Twas then found
that second’s rider was in same predicament, and objec-
tion was withdrawn. Excited owner of the third then
protested against first two on ground already stated, and
when it was shown that he too was in same boat, he
retired crestfallen and grieved silently in a corner of the
paddock.
r * * » •
There’s always a possibility of getting a div. in
a trot in M.L. however slow-going and common. Riders
getting excited in close finishes in trots, are apt to urge their
mokes too much and make ’em break. A thumping div.-
paver—only 30s. on him—was streaking for home (at
Wellington, M.L.) when an opponent broke him up.
’Twas near the post and the pair galloped for it, the un-
backed one getting there. Stewards passed both out, and
gave stake to third horse, an innocent-looking nag with
the bushranging name of Steve Hart. Even when an
owner isn’t on the job and hasn’t backed his horse he is
apt to get the stake foisted on to him in Maoriland.
W. C. Jackson, better known as “Newhaven,”
was the most successful rider in Australia for the season
just ended. Since October last Jackson has banked some
£B5O as the result of his season’s riding, having won thirty
races, besides numerous seconds and thirds. “New-
haven,” like the majority of the world’s cracks, is a firm
believer in “ Dunlops,” and all his victories during the
past season have been secured on these popular tyres.*

When they put a man in

enjoymentof life is limited.
He cannot eat what he
wants to. He is limited to
a very frugal diet. He is
alive'to oe sure, but life
doesn’t possess very many
advantages. Are not all
these things equally true of
a dyspeptic? For all of
the real enjoyment he gets
out of life he might as well
be in gaol. He cannot eat
what lie likes, nor as much
of it as he would like. If
he transgresses any of the
rules of his diet, he is pun-
ished for it. He suffers
much; gets little sym-
pathy. Dyspepsia starts
with indigestion, and may
lead to almost anything.
Indigestion meansa variety
of things—it shows itself
in many ways. At first,
perhaps, a little heaviness
in the stomach, a little
sourness, windy belchings,
and heartburn. Headaches
begin to come pretty soon after that, and bilious-
ness and a foul taste in the mouth in the morning.
Chronic constipation is almost inevitable, and it is
probably tlie most serious trouble that ever takes
hold of a man. Its seeming simplicity is the thing
that makes it most dangerous, because it leads to
neglect. Constipation means that the body is
holding poisonous, impure matter that should be
gotten rid of. The poison is being re-absorbed
into the blood, and the whole body is being filled
it. Impurity in the blood may lead to almost any
disease. There is no telling what may come of it.
Constipation is the start of it all. And yet people
are careless about it. It is the most serious thing
in the world, and the easiest to cure if you go
about it right. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
cure constipation. Cure it positively, certainly,
infallibly. Cure it so it stays cured. Cure it so
you can stop taking medicine. And that is some-
thing that no other remedy in the world will do.
They positively cure biliousness, indigestion, dys-
pepsia, sallow complexion, liver and kidney
troubles, piles, pimples and blotches, a perfect
blood purifier, and for female ailments they stand
alone as a woman’s best friend. Sold by chemists
and storekeepers, price Is. l|d. per bottle, or six

N.S.W. Postmaster-General Crick will not
be happy until he holds the portfolio of Lands,
which he is thoroughly competent to administer.
The latest scheme was to appoint burly Hassall as
“Commercial Agent” in London, and thus enable
the P.M.G. to get his billet. But the Ministerial
supporters and the public did not respond to the
feelers thrown out, and the move is off for the
present.
The action of J. R. Dickson, the late
Premier of the Bananaland Continuous Ministry,
in opposing the efforts of the Federal delegates,
with Barton at their head, in their struggles in Lon-
don, is a beautiful satire on the “Wait for
Queensland ! ” cry of a year or so back. On the
first occasion on which N.S.W.’s chosen represen-
tative needs all available help, it is “ Our Natural
Ally ” who turns dog on him.
A complete return of expenditure under
the head of plague expenses is to be called for as
soon as N.S.W. Parliament meets. All sorts of
statements are in circulation about how the money
has been going, and in their own interests the
Ministry must make a full statement. Mr.
M ‘Creche, superintendent of the cleansing of the
quarantined areas, has been engaged at a very
high figure and other expenses are in proportion.
St. Kilda newsagent to a Bulletin man :
“ I’m not a pro-Boer because I find that when
people buy a paper, or a pound of sausages, or a
bar of soap from a man they expect to get his im-
mortal soul as well, and if they don’t get the soul
thrown in they go elsewhere. The other day in
talking war to an old customer, I chanced to re-
mark that I did not think Mafeking would be able
to hold out. He gave me a look of unspeakable
loathing and wheeled out of the shop. Ten
minutes later he sent a note by messenger to say
that I need not trouble to supply his household
with papers and periodicals for the future as he
only bought off patriots.”

In the signing of a testimony in R.
D. Meagher’s favor by the Speaker of N.S.W.
Assembly, in his official capacity, was there not some
savour of an indecent approaching of the Bench by
the Legislature ? It is a matter in which even the
appearance of evil cannot be too carefully avoided.
The Acetylene Gas Company of Austra-
lasia, Ltd., is to be congratulated on its success
at the Royal Agricultural Show, having secured no
less than twenty-five first prizes for its exhibits,
consisting of automatic, non-automatic and port-
able generators, also buggy, miner’s, stevedore’s,
bandsmen’s, firemen’s, bicycle, table, hand and
other lamps for acetylene gas. Ordinary carriage-
lamps converted into acetylene lamps; correspond-
ence invited on all outdoor and indoor lighting.*
Since the war began there have been
nearly 100 casualties among the N.S.W. troops in
S Africa: 9 killed in action ; 19 died from disease;
46 wounded, missing, 25.
It is pleasant to learn, on Q. Federal dele-
gate Dickson’s own authority, that, since he turned
dog on Australia in England, he has been boycotted
by the other Federal delegates.
M'Kinley will probably be re-elected U.S.
President, for the countless votes brought to
Bryan by his advocacy of the Boer cause will be
severely offset by his pro-silverism and the solid
opposition of the great monopolistic interest.
Dewey has no real chance, and never had ; his
show was in the first instance somewhat pre-
judiced by his having married a R.C. wife—at
any rate the incident was artfully used against
him for all it was worth, and matters have not
been improved by the ambitious lady publicly re-
nouncing the Pope and going back to Protestantism
after being prominent in the R.C. camp for years.
It is already being effectively said that a man who
changes his politics and a woman who changes her
religion for the purpose of being President and
Presidentess of a Republic can never wave a vic-
torious banner in the political arena of the U.S.
The long-expected list of increments in
N.S.W. Public Service dating from July 1 last
appeared on May 18. Out of £14,000 appro-
E mated by Parliament for these increments only
alf has been allotted, some 600 officers partici-
pating. As the number eligible under the Board’s
own regulation is more than double this number,
they (the Board) practically declare that a
tremendous percentage of public servants are not
fit for promotion to the extent of £lO each.
Strange to say, the officers concerned do not agree
with this view. The portioning out of these in-
crements is absolutely at the discretion of a sub-
Board under the P.S. Board, and the Star
Chamber character of the whole business has so
dissatisfied the Service that an effort is being
made to ventilate the matter in Parliament. The
Public Service Association will also be appealed
to, and the result will probably decide the future
of that body.
The publicity given to the fact that
Messrs. Lyne and See intended to appoint their
sons to be lieutenants in the new Sydney half-
squadron of N.S.W. Lancers brought such a storm
about their heads that the nominations have boon
hurriedly withdrawn. Mr. Darley, son of the
Chief Justice, who is not so strongly objected to
by the troopers, will probably succeed in holding
on to his commission. The whole affair, rendered
possible by the present peculiar military pro-
cedure, which seems to be quite at the mercy of
any politician temporarily m power, has excited
an uneasy feeling throughout volunteer circles. It
has also considerably alienated the support of the
volunteers from the Ministry, who up to now have
stood well in their regard owing to their ultra-
jingoistic action re the S’African contingents.
Unfortunately for themseives Ministers have ven-
tured to presume upon their supposedly invulner-
able popularity.

“The Relief of Mafeking ” was such a
splendid exouse for a general drunk that it is
almost a pity to dash the enthusiasm of the
brokers and other boys by pointing out that —even
if the British yarn, per the Rhodesian press, is to
be relied upon—the Mammoth of the war was
only about the size of an average plague-rat, after
all. Boiled down, there were about 1600 British,
regulars and irregulars, fighting in Mafeking ; and
probably about the same number—or a few more
—or a good many more—outside. No one knows
how many Boers there were; but they could not
have numbered very many more than the be-
sieged—for the simple reason that all the Boers
who could possibly be got together, and all the
best of the Boer forces, were down South and
East fighting the greatly superior forces of Methuen
and Buffer; and Cecil Rhodes is firm in his
asservation that the Boers had not more
than 35,000 fighters at any time to oppose
England’s 200,000 odd “ the biggest army
ever transported across seas.” What Boers
there were to spare—the ruck of the Boer
army—stayed round about Mafeking, and fired
guns at it occasionally without doing much
damage. Occasionally there was a perfunctory
sort of attempt at assault; but the armed Boer
peasants, like other armed peasants, are not the
men to attack a garrison partly composed of regu-
lars—and they have no bayonets. Cronje seemed
to put a little heart into the “ siege ” while he was
there; but when he was gone it seems to have just
drifted on for form’s sake. One measure of the
importance of a siege is the number of lives lost
in defence; and Baden-Powell’s losses were
apparently so trifling that they are not mentioned.
“Baden-Powell sent out bulletinssays one
Sydney paper, “as though the whole siege was a
vast picnic party.” Well, as a siege, it was. The
British hadn’t much to eat; but that was the
worst of it. Their health was good ; they played
polo and other games; the investment was so open
that they were always able to get communications
through—Baden-Powell even revised the proofs of
his book on scouting, and sent them to his London
publisher; and the thing drifted on for seven
months tiff British reinforcements came, and the
Boers melted away. But to compare Mafeking
with Gibraltar ! Saragossa ! ! Sebastopol! ! !
Plevna ! ! ! ! —what are the Jingoes giving us ?

The Commonwealth Bill.
The Commonwealth Bill is now awaiting its
second reading in the House of Commons. And
the Chamberlain amendments, as far as can be
judged from the imperfect synopsis cabled, have
crystallised to these :
1. The original preamble says Australia has agreed to
unite under the Grown and “ under the Constitution
hereby established.” The words “ under the Constitution
hereby established ” are struck out. The Crown brooks
no rivalry with a mere popular Constitution.
2. The original Bill says “ This Act shall bind the
Crown.” That is struck out. The Crown declines to be
bound. It is treating with Australia as with a dependent
vassal, not as with an independent subject.
3. Boom is left for Westralia to come in.
4. The original Bill provides that the Bill itself, and the
laws of the Federal Parliament, shall bind the Courts,
Judges, and people of the States ; and shall be in force on
all British ships, except war-ships, if their first port of
clearance and port of destination are in the Common-
wealth. This is amended to give the Crown the right to
grant special leave of appeal from any judgment of the
Federal Supreme Court. Which secures that Australian
justice is finally decreed in England.
5. The “ Colonial Laws Act, 1865," is applied to the Com-
monwealth laws. The essence of this Act is that if a
“ Colonial ” law conflicts with an Imperial Act, the
“ Colonial ” law is invalid to the extent of the conflict.
The application to the Commonwealth means that we
federate, not under the Crown, but under the British Par-
liament ; since the British Parliament will have the
power by contrary legislation to nullify any law of the
Commonwealth.
6. Clause 74 (the clause most of the trouble has been
about) of the original Bill says no appeal shall be per-
mitted to the Queen in Council in matters involving the
interpretation of the Constitution or of the Constitution
of a State. Chamberlain struck this out altogether. He
now restores it, with the addendum “ unless the Gover-
nor-General or the State Governors, with the consent of
the Executive, agree to an appeal to the Imperial Court."
This leaves constitutional questions in the hands of the
Federal Supreme Court, unless the Federal Executive per-
mits an appeal.
The same clause provided that, though the right to
appeal to the Queen in Council remained, the Federal
Parliament might limit the use of the right. Chamber-
lain struck this out. He now restores it, with addendum
that the Federal Parliament’s limitation is subject to the
Queen’s veto. This leaves the British money-lender’s
privilege intact, unless the Queen agrees that it be taken
away from him.
• • • •
Apart from the Crown’s jealous stickling for its
supremacy—its natural refusal to treat with the
Australian people as with an equal—the two im-
portant points in this list are the reservation of
Privy Council appeals and the application of the
“Colonial Laws Act.” The maintenance of a
clause reserving Australian constitutional ques-
tions for an Australian court’s final decision,
unless the Australian Federal Executive grants
special leave of appeal, has apparently satisfied the
Federal delegates, and Chamberlain takes credit
for a “ concession.” He concedes nothing. The
Bill approved by Australia, the Bill whose com-
plete acceptance by the British Parliament Aus-
tralia had reason and right to demand, is still
mutilated and its intention thwarted ; and no
credit accrues to the British Govt, because the
mutilation might have been greater. If the Bill
is passed by the Commons as it stands at
present, and the alteration is confirmed by an
Australian referendum, then it should become an
unwritten law of the Federal Executive to grant
no leave of appeal, but to preserve the authority
of the Federal Supreme Court as it stands in the
original Bill. Otherwise the Court may be in-
fluenced to give judgment with an eye to. the
Executive, and such an influence cannot fail to
injure the Court’s value and independence. As
regards the application of the Colonial Laws
Act, the Federal delegates are said to be ‘ im-
pressed by the amendment,” whether favorably
or unfavorably does not appear. But if
its scope has been accurately transmitted,
then it is an amendment which should be
fought as energetically as was the amendment to
the Privy Council clause. Within the Common
wealth’s territory the Commonwealth’s laws should
be unhampered by Imperial Acts. Either we do,''
or we do not, exercise tne right of self-government
within our own boundaries; and the whole effect of
the Commonwealth Bill is to reiterate that we do.
Then why should an Imperial Act invalidate a
Commonwealth law where the interests of the
Commonwealth are exclusively concerned ? Why
should we Federate, not only under the Crown,
but also under the British Parliament ? It is
easy to say that the power of the House of Com-
mons is sure to be discreetly exercised, but why
give it the power ? There will be so many leaks
of “ prerogative,” and “ privilege,” and “reserva-
tion ” in the Commonwealth Bill that, as a vessel
of self-government, it will be legally unseaworthy.
* * • *
British politicians have been accustomed to
boast that, in so far as concerns “ her colonies,”
Britain’s attitude is directly opposed to that of
France or Germany. These countries, we are
told, use their “colonies” for the benefit of the
central power; and value them as they value com-
mercial speculations, by the profit which
they pay on capital invested. Britain, on the
other hand, rules India for the benefit of the
native races—is a sort of Hindoo fairy
godmother, making roads, administering laws,
feeding the famine-stricken and healing the
plague-stricken ; and if Britain takes yearly from
India some £25,000,000 in taxation, if she finds in
the Indian army and the Indian civil service a
happy hunting-ground where Englishmen may
make their fortunes, if she secures in India a rich
market for British manufactures, then it is all for
the profit of India, and as a means of showing
further benevolence to the Hindoos. And
doubters are referred with pride to the increasing
population of India—just as a warlike tribe of
ants, if they had the intelligence, might refer
you to the increasing number of meek aphides
whom they enslave and milk. But the gradual
extermination of the population of Fiji, on the
other hand, is not to be referred to British inter-
ference ; but to the visitation of God in the shapes
of religion, rum, and disease. The fact of the
matter is that, however the means of France and
Germany and Britain may differ, their ends are
precisely the same—the “colonies and dependen-
cies” are valued by all in proportion as they
yield profit to the central power. Australia
and Canada are not so well regimented and
officialised as French Algeria and German
Africa; but, as is affirmed by exponents of
the Imperial idea, it is equally their function to
be in peace-time a source of commercial profit,
and in war-time a recruiting-ground for soldiers.
Britain is using Australia and Canada for her own
benefit as relentlessly as she was using America
when the taxed tea was thrown into Boston har-
bour, and the right of Britain to milk her
dependent colonies was supported by the
force of British arms. This century’s method
differs from the last century’s, but the
result aimed at is precisely the same. Hence
the jealousy lest any diminution of the British
supremacy, or even of the nominal British supre-
macy, may be caused by the Commonwealth Bill;
and hence the manner in which the Common-
wealth Bill has been mended, and amended, and
re-amended in the interests of Britain—though it
represents a popular vote of the Australian people
at a time when Australian money and Australian
lives have been recklessly spent in the British in-
terest. The Bulletin is proud to believe, in the
interest of Australian nationality, that the British
effort is in vain. It is as certain as any human
forecast can be, that at the close of the next cen-
tury, if the present scanty population shall have
increased to even half that of America at the pre-
sent day, Australia may be Britain’s ally, but will
no longer be her utensil and dependent.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The law of treasure-trove recently received a
new exposition, when Vic. Full Court decreed that the
drawer of a crossed cheque—subsequently lost—must,
notwithstanding he had stopped payment, duly honor the
same. The cheque in question, drawn by a Melb. firm of
fish-salesmen, had gone missing, and then had presumably
been found by an individual wbo cashed it with a country
publican. The Full Court upheld Judge Gaunt’s decision
“ that the holder of a negotiable security, transferable by
delivery can give a title, which he himsel f does not .possess,
to a bona fide holder for value, and that negligence does
not invalidate the title of a person taking a negotiable
instrument in good faith and for value.” It was admitted,
however, by defendants that plaintiff received the cheque
bona-fide.
* • • •
That was an ugly-looking case in Brisbane, the
other day, where an important clerical official of the engi-
neering branch of the Railway Department, with a salary
of £5OO, compounded with his creditors, amongst whom
were many railway-contractors. One of his secured
creditors (for £270) was an ex-railway-contractor, and to
ein-ht cont ractors and ex-contractors he unsecuredly owed
about £7OO. Also, he was rather heavily indebted to
two Ms.P. He explained at the meeting that the con-
tractors weren’t doing business with the department when
the debts were incurred, and the creditors resolved, by
£2799 against £641, to grant him his discharge forthwith.
Albury storekeepers wbo order goods from
Sydney via Melbourne to save freight have discovered
that articles, particularly of a bottled liquid kind, are
frequently missing. Sydneyside repudiates responsibility,
so the aggrieved traders propose to sue Vic. Govt., as the
authorities there tender a form admitting receipt of such
and such packets from Sydney.
It is usual on long stock-routes for firms to wire
money ahead to their drovers for necessary expenses ;
therefore a certain Australian Govt, is bemoaning the
unauthorised absence of a cunning telegraphist. Mr.
Dotdash being due for six months’ leave, proceeded
to raise the wind. Just before he was relieved he wired
ahead to three different offices telegraph money-order
advices for £5O, each purporting to be from Station Blank
—code-word, etc., being of course all in order. When he
got to each town on his way he produced at the local
office a wire issued at his own office (where there was no
money-order branch) advising him to call at So-and-So for
£5O wired thither. Of course the money was paid, and
the returns duly forwarded to Head Office. Dotdash then
lit out for parts'unknown. Owing to distance and absence
of any original messages it was weeks ere the skein was
disentangled. The enterprising one is said to be in
S’Africa, but as it would cost about £2OO to bring him
back and collect the necessary witnesses, the Department
simply passes on to the next item.

In one Maoriland city many attempts have been
made to oppose the incompetent evening newspaper
which locally bosses things. And it really does boss
things, for should a business man dare to advertise in a
new journalistic venture the lordly evening print at once
refuses to accept his advts. 1 Which just about takes the
cake.
N.S.W. Railway Dept, sometime ago resumed
a portion of certain land which had been sold on terms at
£4 per acre by a bank to the occupier. When the latter
got his account from the Dept., it was much under the
amount he expected. He visited the bank, but the
officials professed inability to explain the discrepancy.
He then approached the Railways, where he was informed
that part of the land was a reserved road, of which he
knew nothing but had bought with the rest, and no com-
pensation could be allowed for it. And the Crown had
only received ss. per acre on the rest, and the balance of
15s. due thereon had been deducted from the amount paid
for resumption. If a private individual did business like
this he would soon hear something drop.
THE LATEST “HUM.”
SUBURBAN SWAGMAN: “ Lydy, could you assist a
pore man wot’s come to misfortune through no
fault of ’is own?”
LADY: “ Why, how did you come to this position?”
S. S.: “I had a great longin’ to serve me Queen
and country and bash them there Boers; so I
jiaed the Himperial Bushmen, and was thrown
out at the last moment and left friendless and
far from ’Ome. ” (Sobs.)
(Departs a moment later laden with tucker.)
Sydney spirit-morchants aver that April was the
best month known for years—infant, some say it was a
record, though singularly enough the publicans grumble
more than ever about bad times. Causes assigned for the
increased consumption are plague, early closing, the date
of Easter this year, Contingent departures, and general
jubilation over the turn of the tide in S’Africa.
* • • *
The compositor and all the rest of the staff
rarely fall in so badly as they did the other day in an
Adelaide newspaper office. A firm was advertising the
virtues of its bonedust ; and alongside “ bonedust ” was
the direction to the printer— “ large and heavy.” In
other words, “ use big, black type.” But that staff
wasn’t an expert in bonedust. It didn’t know that if the
grains are coarse or large they choke the drills, and that
if the stuff is heavy it doesn’t go so far to the acre. So it
just announced that (say) Brown’s bonedust was “ large
and heavy ” ; and a few hours afterwards it wondered
what the man was makiug such a row about in the front
office.
* * * *
An important case for squatters and other con-
tractors for sale and delivery was decided by N.S.W.
Supreme Court, May 16. Agents of A. G. Finlay, Clyde,
Q., in May, 1899, sold sheep to Reilly Bros., Te Aro,
N.S.W., on this contract: ' i wethers running on
Clyde Station, number approximately only ; but to be the
whole of the wethers on Clyde.” Plaintiffs complained
that number delivered was considerably short of 16,000,
and brought action for breach of contract. Jury awarded
£248, and defendant appealed for new trial on the ground
that the reference in the contract to the number of
wethers was only an estimate and not a guarantee of
16,000: and that’ what he contracted to sell was the
whole of the wethers on Clyde.” The Court held that
these words formed the dominant portion of the contract,
and unanimously granted a new trial. Darley, C.J., said
that if there had been evidence of misrepresentation or
fraud—if, for example, defendant had tried to deliver
only 1000 or as many as 40,000 sheep, then plaintiff might
have complained he had been misled ; but as there was
no suggestion of that kind he was bound by the plain
terms of the contract.

“ Been There ” : Re “ Suity’s” approving refer-
ence to the average-striking method of jury-assessment
(B. 12/5/’00). But, if there be one conscienceless friend of
plaintiff on the jury—one who, under the circumstances,
would be sure to vote for the highest possible amount
within the limits of the claim —his ballot would neutralise
the votes of his fellow-jurymen, who had based their
assessments on an unbiassed view of the equities of the
case.

A small Melbourne creditor of a Q’land insol-
vent’s estate got a cheque for his first dividend the other
week. With the cheque came a receipt form to be signed
and posted back to the liquidator at the cost of a 2d.
stamp. Also, the exchange on the cheque was Is. The
man paid the cheque into his account instead of lighting
his pipe'with it, though it would have come to the same
thing either way. He had to make a loss of Bd. on the
transaction, provided that he sent along the receipt.
His dividend was only fid. all told.

In Sydney, recently, a man who had had some
clothes washed at a Chinese laundry called for them,
snatched the parcel when produced, and bolted without
paying. The P.M. ruled that this was theft of what,
until the washing had been paid for, was the laundryman’s
property. Which may have been practical justice, but
does not look like law.
• * *
It is a hard row that the fraudulent or forgetful
insolvent has to hoe in S. Australia. What with the acute
and prompt Commissioner Russell on the Bench, and a
persistent official, “ Solicitor to the Court of Insolvency,”
at the table, a bad time awaits the man who files and
then hopes to get through on the plea that he must have
drunk and gambled all the money he can t account for.
“ Just go to gaol a while and think the matter over,” is
becoming almost a formal way of adjourning a case.

Several big Australian newspapers have lately
handled in denunciatory vein the Yankee system of com-
bines or trusts. But what is the arrangement under
which cables are supplied to those very papers at the
present time but an application of the trust principle in
its worst form ?
* » • *
“ Albion ” : A man runs up against some queer
risks in railway-travelling. Know a way-back storekeeper
who carries gunpowder with him because he “can’t see
the force of paying 10s. for having 15 bob’s worth of ex-
plosives put into compartment set apart for the purpose.”
He sticks the powder carelessly under the seat to be
kicked by everybody’s heels. Also, I once travelled from
Cootamundra with a case of typhoid. It was in my arms
most of the way, as the compartment was crowded. It
was “going to Sydney hospital.” Again—travelled to
Goulburn with fat old lady with an offensive smell in a
carpet-bag. Officials—suspecting defunct baby—seized
the bag, but found in it only a lot of shells and decom-
posing sea-weed, which the old party had collected at
Manly Beach. The authorities should ordain that all
parcels shall set forth on the outside the nature of their
contents, and surely there should be separate compart-
ments for fever-patients as much as for explosives.
Another night, near Campbelltown, a man’s muzzle-
loading gun went off and shattered a parson’s bottle of
gin in the rack. “ Hum 1” said the owner of the gun,
coolly ; “ thought I had her at half-cock 1”

One big Australian tow.i l ately went out to the
nearest vacant place and kicked itseli hard several times.
It had lately been struck by a gentleman “ walking round
the world for a wager.” “One of the conditions,” it was
very explicitly stated, “is that he must neither beg,
borrow, nor spend money.” Ahead of him went a
manager, who billed the town, hired the local theatre and
a band, and arranged three lecture performances. Two
lectures were given—and then the people who hadn t been
paid wanted some money. Applying to the manager they
were given smiles and —“ But, my dear fellow, I’m merely
an agent; you must see my principal.” And the princi-
pal gave them smiles, too, and —“ But, my dear sir, you
knew the terms on which I am travelling, didn’t you
Creditors withdrew to think ; and the gentleman who is
“ walking round the world ” promptly left by next
express train. And from the terminus of that line he
walked on—as far as the deck of the next outgoing
steamer.

. • * *
The law of lost cheques. Somebody (the B.H.
Miners’ Association, it was) drew a cheque, leaving, it
open. It was lost. A publican cashed it—gave four six-
penny beers and £24 18s. for it. Payment stopped. Pub-
lican sued drawer for £25. Defence: Publican didn’t
exercise reasonable care in accepting a £25 cheque in
payment for four drinks. Verdict for publican. And a
moral : Cross your cheques. Even if they aren’t crossed
and you are going to bank them, cross them yourself.
“ Long ’Un ”: Recently heard one of the Chosen
telling another how he travelled from Adelaide to Broken
Hill for a shilling. Holiday time—bought second-class
ticket for 20s. Met a friend travelling “ first,” and
bought hix return half for 21s. Then at Silverton sold his
own return for 155., and upon reaching B.H. got rid of
the “ first ” return for 255. “ Veil,” said his friend, in-
stead of applauding, when he had finished, “ you van a
chump to go drowin’ avay a shillin’ over a sirable liddle
dransaedion like dat 1”

“ T.G.F,” : More Govt, promptitude !
Amongst the Gazette notices in a recent Sydney
D.T. appeared the announcement —“George
Alfred Thrum—retired.” Now, said G.A.T. has,
to my knowledge, been dead nearly three years.
Very much “retired” !
A would-be suicide was lately found re-
clining in the shallow water of the Queen River
(Tas.). It was raining steadily, and he told his
rescuer that he ‘ ‘ was waiting for the river to rise
to put an end to his wretched existence.” This
species of self-destruction will never become really
popular on the droughty mainland of Australia.
S.M. Herald’s strictures on the way the
Glebe Island Bridge is being built are deserved.
Immense mounds of stony earth are heaped up
across the entrance to Blackwattle Bay (from
which tumuli are to be stretched the bridge-
girders), thus leaving but a narrow water-way,
which will inevitably cause stagnation of the bay’s
water —into which, by-the-way, nine sewers already
discharge.
Next naval war will be a “corker.”
T’other day the armament of the new U.S. war-
ship, Kearsage, was tested in a heavy sea-way.
Three guns from one turret were fired simul-
taneously, when all the shots struck the same
target, throwing up a single wave. No crockery
was broken on board, aiid no deck-bolts were
started. “The American navy now has five ships
whose simultaneous discharge no existing vessel
can withstand.”
A war item :
.... After the battle I came on some of the wounded
enemy. The Scandinavian in command had a bayonet-
thrust through his stomach, and was dying. He signalled
to me that he was thirsty. I lifted him up and gave him
a cup of condensed milk. A wounded companion lying
by his side said in very good English : “ It’s no use giving
it to him, sir, it only runs out of the hole in his stomach.”
This was true, but still the poor fellow had the sensation
of the refreshing draught passing down his throa't. It
was his last drink. I can see his eager, hungry look even
now, and though an enemy, I wish I could have done
more for him.
Story of Queen Victoria’s visit to Dublin.
An Englishman bought from an old-woman news-
vendor in Graft,on-st. a Dublin evening paper
which suppressed all report of the visitation—and
complained. “ Look .here,” he said, “this paper
has nothing in it about the Queen’s visit; it’s a
fraud.” “ Lor’ save ye, sur,” instantly replied the
old lady, “Lor’ bless ye, there’s the shootin’ of
the Prince o’ Wales in it. How much more does
your honor want for a halfpenny?”
War in South Africa once again proves
that disease kills more than bullets; also that
disease is generally the offspring of bad food or
no food. A starving army is a disease-smitten
army; an army half-fed or fed on badly-cooked
food is dyspeptic and woeful. The same applies
to the busy citizen engaged in the wearing toils of
precious peace. Why die of bad cookery when
the best of health can be easily and cheaply as-
sured by means of M ‘Lean Bros, and Bigg’s
Primus Chief Stove? Costs only Is. to Is. 3d. per
week for oil or gas, and bestows an untold wealth
of health and happiness. Send for catalogue or

Victoria’s State-aided attempts to start a
beet-sugar industry seems to be collapsing through
the lack of interest taken in it by the local farmer.
Last year the Turner Govt, appointed an expert
to show the man on the land how to grow beet,
and offered prizes for the best results. The results
demonstrated—firstly, that sugar-beet of the best
quality can be grown at Maffra, and secondly, that
the local farmer won’t trouble himself in the
matter. He either goes in for dairying or he
fattens cattle ; in either case he objects to hustle.
There were big profits for him in the successful
establishment of the industry, for, in addition to
the gains from sugar-producing, the pulp was the
very thing needed to keep cows in milk when grass
is scarce. But the expert’s engagement has ex-
pired, and the Govt, won’t waste any more money
in trying to help the farmer who won’t help him-
self.

At the Primrose meeting, Premier Salis-
bury gave away a favorite patriotic excuse for the
war when he said, as per cable :
Under the brilliant guidance of Lord Roberts and Lord
Kitchener, the largest army that had ever been sent across
the same expanse of sea was reducing to obedience to the
Queen territories which ought never to have been released
from that obedience, and was restoring to South Africa
its only chance of peace.
The last few lines are a candid admission of the
independence of the Boers, which, if made by a
Minister in Australia, would have called down
upon his head the virulent abuse of a Jingo public.
Big Donald MacDonald’s war stuff for the
Argus lost grip immediately he was let out of the
little familiar hell in Ladysmith. Whilst fever-
worried and full of famished rats, his writing
knocked emphatically at the soul of man, but then
came a series of articles, evidently reminiscent,
and the intensity and soul-pressure were gone.
Mac’s anxiety to do justice to the Boer seemed
always bearing up hard against office traditions,
but he gets in bits like this re Cronje :
“It is impossible to look upon Cronje’s face, though,
and not feel well disposed towards him. There is nothing
at all in it that repels, very much that attracts, and is the
outward evidence of the crusty, contradictory kindliness
of the man.”

J. H. Witheford, who won the vacant
Auckland (M.L.) city seat by a majority of more
than 2000 —polling nearly as many votes as the
four candidates (one of whom, by the way, had
the special blessing of Premier Seddon) —has had
a somewhat remarkable career. His start in life
was in a tiny tinsmith’s business in an Auckland
suburb, from which he gravitated to the employ
of Auckland Star. In the more modest days of
Proprietor Brett Witheford became Brett’s general
utility-man. He has been known to collect a stiff
account, report a municipal meeting, sell a gold-
fields water-right and buy a kauri-forest—for
Brett —all in the one day. Three or four years
ago Witheford visited England to float a mine at
Coromandel. His mission was enormously suc-
cessful to himself personally, and the Maoriland
mining boom resulted. During his stay in Eng-
land lie took it upon himself to interview the
Lords of the Admiralty re the equipping of Auck-
land as a naval station. The haughty personages
who direct the naval destinies of the Empire at
first regarded the nervoful colonial as an interesting
biological freak, and bluffed eim and generally
treated him after the disdainful manner of their
kind. But Witheford worried them to the verge
of exhaustion, gained his point, and now gets into
Parliament as a grateful city’s reward.
Nervous sufferers will find ifc worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*
