Jones, the Genius Hunter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The biggest man in Mulga Town —he ruled the
WaSKLY Grunter,
Was known to all the country round as “Jones,
the Genius Hunter ” ;
That he could tell a geniiu, whether loud he spoke
i or meekly
Wai the pro ad boast of Jabez Jones who ruled the
Grunter weekly.
Now, Jones within the local pub. was aye this
lesson giving,
That beer and brains together went, and learning
meant loose living;
That geniuses were always drunk, and drunk men
geniasses ;
And by these signs he knew his prey and roped
them in in masses.
If Brown indulged in rosy wine and on his face
. did show it,
It proved to Mr. Jabez Jones that he must be a
poet.
If Smith unto a lovely dame unlawfully was mated,
Smith had the artist-temperament and must be
cultivated.
It therefore was not strange that Jones could
boast no writeresses,
Save one, a country maid, who penned warm
things about caresses,
And verses wrote of love and wine and of the
devil maybe,
That she might buy her nephews shoes, and some-
thing for the baby.
Now, Mary’s lips were thin and close, and Mary’s
hair hung straightly,
On Mary’s face shone common sense, and Mary
walked sedately;
When Mary came to Mulga Town and faced the
Genius Hunter—
She found her fame grew weaker in the pages of
the Grunter.
Till once she ohanced to overhear J. Jones his
lesson giving—
“ That Beer and Brains together went, and learning
meant loose living.”
So Mary set about to force the Grunter to
believing
She had lived down her sinlessness, her blameless
past retrieving.
Forthwith she took to qaaffing beer, and strove to
quaff it cheerily,
And paffed the gentle cigarette—none guessed she
puffed it wearily ;
To hint at bright-pink sins that lit her past she
, was not chary;
Likewise she showed affection for a married man
—M‘Gary.
New Mary hated cigarettes, colonial beer, and
shandy;
Her hidden tastes were all along for lemonade and
candy;
Her sinless past it suited well her praenomen of
Mary,
And there was nothing wrong at all between her
and M‘Gary.
When Jabez heard his writeress had started cigar-
etting,
He murmured that her work of late a Firmer
Touch was getting;
When Jabez heard his writeress drank beer or
spirits nightly
He swore, “Her work improves by bounds, her
pen moves muoh more lightly.”
But when he heard that scandal linked her name
with Jack M‘Gary
His admiration knew no bounds ; he made a place
for Mary;
He gave her four-pound-ten a week, and in the
township hailed her,
“The Coming Genius of the Age, whose vigor
never failed her.”
So Mary to the nephews small more boots and
toys is giving,
And still hides from a callous world the sinless
life she’s living;
And if her virtues don’t leak out and blight her
reputation
She hopes to gather wealth beyond all human
expectation.
N.S.W.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
After all, the man who had the job to
supply the Westralian Govt, with 200 cats,
delivered at Israelite Bay, at 15s. a head could
only muster 159. Bigjohn, who, like the rest of
them, isn’t so keen on cats as he was, has forgiven
the contractor the other 41.
Hobart Mercury publishes a letter from
a Tasmanian warrior who is furious at the gallant
Roberts’ courteous treatment of Cronje at the
surrender. “If I had my way I’d give him ten
inches of cold steel, the dog!” says the young man
from Yandemonia. The phrase smacks of Port
Arthur.
Concerning the decorum of Melbourne
crowds, thus a Bulletin correspondent
I am sick of hearing and reading of the extreme de-
corum and amazing respectability of Melb. crowds. The
May Day assemblage on Yarra bank, like many other in-
stances that might be cited, shows that the so-called
decorum may be mere pusillanimity, or a blind and con-
temptible disregard for their own private and public
rights on the part of a great concourse A huge crowd,
mainlv of workers in their Sunday best, gathered round
the lorrv platforms on May Day apparently anxious to
hear the speakers, but these hundreds of men stood
auietlv by whilst a stupid push of half-pint larrikins
numbering not more than & score, utterly demoralised
the meeting. The fatuous youths (all mechanics evi-
dently) imagined that something horribly disloyal was
intended, and they rolled up armed with sticks fluttering
Union Jacks, and drove through the crowd and back
bellowin'' “Gorsave” and “ Rulebrit.,” and other pat-
riotic slush for over an hour, sweeping the people before
them. A d >zen able-bodied men might have donkey-
licked the mutton-headed disorderlies into the river, but
the proletariat took it lying down. The speakers were
mostly spineless and vap : d, but the assembled men
wished to hear them, and the latter’s grinning apathy as
the larrikin patriots walked all over them was shockingly
sheep-like.
Vila’s Version,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Quick ! Quick !” cried a woman dashing through
the surf towards the incoming boat.
“ What’s up, Mrs. Evans ?” asked Ware, wading
ashore with her in his arms.
“ Let me down and come to my husband.
There’s going to be a row ! ”
Ware heard the yells on the hillside and, drop-
ping the girl, he ran up to the store.
The crowd of natives parted to let him through
because they feared him —he was quite a different
man from Evans, who, although he greeted his
friend cheerily, appeared not altogether happy
now.
“You see, Ware, its my ‘reception day !’”
“ Yes —but I don’t like the look of your visitors.
What’s the matter ?”
“ Simple enough. You remember that imitation
pig’s tusk of mine? Well, Remben, the
chief, wants it.
“ Give it to him, then.”
“Not me ! Why, a German buyer in Sydney
will give me a fiver for it.”
“Are your lives not worth more than that?”
asked Ware, as pretty little Vila came up.
“It’s not that bad,” exclaimed Evans, kissing
his wife. “ Remben promised not to harm us, and
I believe him.”
“But, mate,” answered Ware, impatiently,
“don’t you know these native promises are worth
nothing? Even if he doesn’t murder you himself,
he can always, with an easy conscience, hire others
to do it; and, from what I saw coming through,
there’s a lot of ‘bush-men’ here.”
“ Nonsense ! You are an alarmist. Let’s have
dinner. Come, Vila.”
Ware followed the couple inside, and when they
were seated at the table he tried again to per-
suade Evans to give in, or, at any rate, move up
North to his station until the row blew over.
Evans laughed scornfully, but his wife seemed
interested.
Ware noticed her eyes were fixed upon him, and
it was with a smothered curse that lie turned away
to his plate again. In vain, though, for he could
not eat. Vila was still watching him—he felt it.
She was waiting, expecting him to speak.
At last, the involuntary murmur escaped his
lips. “ It’s a damn pity, anyway ! ”
She heard. “Tell me. You must!” she said,
eagerly.
“Yes,” answered Ware, “you had better know
what to expect. Listen to me, Evans.” Then he
told them of the horrible indignities and degrada-
tions which the wife of a Samoan “teacher” had
undergone after her husband’s murder—just a few
miles from where they sat. She lived witli the
natives for three months, and, when rescued, was
an idio.t.
Mrs. Evans had drunk in every word, and as the
story ended with, “See, Evans, what you are ex-
posing your wife to,” she half rose—then, with a
ory of terror, sank to the ground.
The men rushed to her.
Disregarding her husband, she clasped the knees
of Ware, and begged him to take her away. “ Let
us leave at once. I will tell your men. Oh, I can-
not wait! Remember the Samoans !”
“What do you mean by frightening the girl
with your stupid talk !” exclaimed Evans angrily.
“ It’s the truth.”
“Rot! Anyway, she stays here while I’m
alive.”
“ And afterwards 1”
“She will know to prefer death to dishonor,”
cried the husband boastfully.
“I hope so,” answered Ware. “I’m going to
get my boat ready. I’ll come back to say good-
bye.”
When he returned he found Mrs. Evans waiting
on the verandah ; her husband had gone out for a
moment.
“You believe all I told you this morning,
Vila?” asked Ware. The woman nodded and
moved near to him.
“And you know as well as Ido what these
inland natives are down here for ? ”
She nodded again, and muttered:—“ He will
not come away.”
“He said you would rather die than suffer as
that Samoan did.”
Vila shuddered and clutched his arm.
“ Well, I have brought you my rifle—it will not
make a mistake.”
Her head sank upon his shoulder. He kissed
her, then said, “ Come, you must not be a coward.
Let me show you how to use it.”
The girl leant against the post and watched
Ware’s movements. She tried herself then, but
the lock was stiff, and her attempt was a very poor
one.
“ That won’t do,” said Ware ; “ you might just
as well have a native musket—l’m afraid .”
“ Load it —I only want one,” she murmured.
She would not let go the stock, so Ware loaded
it in her hands. “ You are a plucky woman,” he
said admiringly, and again he kissed her.
“So, this is your game, is it !” said a voice
behind. “This is why you want us to move up
to your station ! Now, clear out, an 1 next time
you take a holiday don’t come South ! ”
“Thanks to your cursed stupidity, it won’t bo
necessary. The natives will be here before I’m
clear of the bay,” answered Ware, releasing the
girl.
He had just reached the beach when a rifle shot
rang out.
“ That’s my Snider !”
A moment after, Vila came running down the
hill. She caught his arm and dragged him towards
the boat—crying, “Come quickly. Remember
the Samoan.”
“ But your husband —and my rifle ? ”
“ There together—up above,” she answered.
Ware smiled grimly at the wife’s fulfillment of
the husband’s boast.
She understood his thoughts and whispered,
“ Why should two die ? ”
“WINDA."
T. J. Allison, a well-known shipping man,
for many years Sydney manager of the China Steam
Navigation Co., has entered the service of Gibbs
Bright and Co., and will devote himself specially
to the Chinese shipping business of that firm.
Nobody this side would have growled at
Labour-member Sleath for going to S. Africa if
only he had resigned, and so left someone to look
after Wilcannia’s interests, writes a local elec-
tor to The Bulletin. As matters stand,
just when the pastoral question is under con-
sideration, the electorate which has suffered as
much as any from drought and other things is
without a representative ; while the unpaid magis-
trates at White Cliffs, who months ago wrote to
him a letter involving the either retractation ot
some remarks made by a Judge in deciding an
appeal or their resignation, have not received so
much as an acknowledgment.
An Old Kettle.
LARA, my dear,”said
her mother, “ I think
we will have a cup of
tea before we go out.”
“Just the very
thing, mother,” an-
swered her daughter,
as she rang the bell
for the maid. “Make
some tea, Mary, as
mother and I have de-
cided to have a cup.
Don’t be long, Mary,
for we have little time
to spare.”
. fifteen minutes two cups of tea were brought
into the sitting-room, but neither mother nor
daughter could drink it. Mary was summoned
into the room, and was asked what was the matter
with the beverage. She acknowledged that it
had a foreign taste, but she could not account for it.
The reason was discovered the next day. The
kettle was one year old, and certain substances in
it had corroded. And so it is with our system, but
Bile Beans for Biliousness will regulate it. We
allow the system to go too far without cleansing it,

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was the annual race-club ball—an hour after it
had opened. Girls’ eyes were gleaming seduc-
tively, men were growing gentle and confidential;
sentimental knots grew and melted; the motley
assembly of farmers, grooms, jockeys, and book-
makers were fusing their separate individualities
into the Spirit of the Moment.
The musicians began a quick, lilting tune that
set even the feet of the wall-flowers tapping. The
Spirit of the Moment seized the dancers, and
merged them into a mob of whirling, smiling,
leaping men and women; faster flew their feet,
higher wailed the music. An excited woman’s
voice was heard at intervals in an ecstatic little
scream, and with each scream the abandon was
greater. When lovers were partners they held
each other closer and closer, until they hugged
openly and unashamed. The wall-flowers gradu-
ally ceased talking, their eyes grew brighter, and
they were lost in the Spirit of the Moment of by-
gone balls. The crowd of dancers began to per-
spire and pant, but still louder wailed the music.
A girl laughed with uncontrollable joy, and was
led away hysterical.
The music jarred, a fiddle-string snapped, the
other two instruments stopped involuntarily.
There was a momentary shuffling of many feet,
• then a dead silence. It was then that Miss Malley,
the postmistress, saw Bryan O’Shea kiss Nance
Carthy under the shadow of the ferns ; the happi-
ness within her snapped like the string of the
fiddle, and awakened the latent devil of malice.
The dancers segregated into farmers, grooms,
jockeys, and bookmakers once more.
• * * *
One hot north-wind day a small brother of
Nance Carthy’s was sent to the township for a
loaf of bread and the mail. He trudged the dis-
tance between his home and the township with a
sturdy indifference to lizards and butterflies, and
did not even stop when he called out to Billy
Smith, enviously, to get off and shove, as the said
Billy rode disdainfully past on his pony. On the
outskirts of the township a stray horse and a handy
stone tempted him into thinking that the first
would jump if the second hit it. It nearly jumped
on top of him. The baker’s buns next glad-
dened his eyes, but he thought he would get the
letters first. Next door to the post-office was a
shop with a fine broad verandah. On the verandah-
Eost nearest the post-office Tommy saw some cow-
air caught in a splinter. He picked up a small
stick and prized the splinter open an additional
quarter of an inch. Then looking up the post, he
saw another and larger splinter, where it seemed
possible to do f urther damage to property. Tommy
proceeded to climb the post forthwith. Reaching
the top, he had a bird’s-eye view into the post-
office through the upper and unfrosted half of the
window—and forgot the splinter. He saw Miss
Malley stand ng in the middle of the office, in-
tently reading a letter. His glance wandered to
the shiny telegraph instrument, then back to Miss
Malley, who at the same moment looked up to
find his two solemn eyes staring at her. She
turned red, hastily crammed the letter into her
pocket, and flustered out to the amazed Tommy.
“ What are you doing there, you nasty little
wretch ? ” His foot being within reach, she pulled
him roughly down by it, whereat Tommy gave a
scream, and began bellowing for his mother, fran-
tically holding -his leg, which was bleeding
slightly from a long scratch given by the hairy
splinter. Through the medium of a bun he had
forgotten all about it in ten minutes. By the
crumpled condition of her letter, Nance thought
Tommy must have played football with it.
* • * *
Mm. Carthy was sorely troubled. Vague
rumors were flitting round the township that
Nance was not all she ought to be ; and Tommy
was sick. She watched Nance anxiously for
those signs by which a troubled conscience is
known, but Nance went about her work with the
serious but happy face of a woman absorbed in her
first love. Tommy got worse and became the
greater trouble—so great as to finally drive all
worry about Nance out of her head.
For four days queer muscular twitchings
afflicted Tommy with increasing intensity—on one
occasion they caused him to throw his tea over his
shoulder. Mrs. Carthy hoped they were but a
passing trouble, but they gradually got worse.
She called in the doctor at last. He sounded
Tommy’s chest, thumped his ribs, took his tem-
perature, looked wise, and was hopelessly puzzled.
He came the next day and found the twitchings
more marked, pulling down the bed-clothes to
examine the violent jumps in Tommy’s legs, he
saw the long scratch made by the splinter.
“ How did you get that, Tommy?”
“ Off Smiff’s verandah-post.”
“ Climbing ?”
“ Miss Malley pulled me down, an’ the splitter
went inside me trousers an’ scratched me leg.”
“What did she pull you down for ? ”
“ I dunno,” he answered thoughtfully. “ But I
fink she didn’t want ter be seen readin’ a letter.
She was awful mad.”
The doctor was no longer listening. But Mrs.
Carthy went on questioning Tommy, pleased to see
him take an interest in things; to her it was a
hopeful sign. The doctor wrote out another pre-
scription, and went away, thinking deeply. Near-
ing nome, his face brightened a little.
“I think I have it —anyway I’ll see,” he
muttered.
He went straight to the post Tommy had
climbed. Seeing the cow-hair, he collected some
with his knife, and took it home in his watch-case.
He put it into some very rich soup and kept it
warm ; later on he examined a drop of the
soup under the microscope, and saw a lot of
little fleshy rods shaped something like shortened
drum-sticks.
“Tetanus!” he muttered, and got up feeling
very sorry for Tommy.
♦ * * *
It was a month after Tommy’s death that the
doctor explained that the tetanus had got into
Tommy through the long scratch, never thinking
that a medical fact could have anything but a
medical application. When it reached Mrs.
Carthy’s ears, she instantly recollected Tommy’s
prattling before his death. Then she remembered
that Tommy had brought home a crumpled letter
for Nance the day he got the soratoh. It flashed
into her mind that it was the letter Tommy saw
the postmistress reading. She called Nance and
asked to see the letter. Nance hesitated.
“ I have my own reasons, and it is important
that I should see it,” the mother said in explana-
tion. It was just an ordinary love-letter; Mrs.
Carthy was puzzled.
“Nance,” she said, ‘was there ever anything
between Miss Malley and Bryan ? ”
Nance looked troubled.
“ I don’t know. I think there was—something
—once. But Bryan won’t tell.”
Having found a fitting motive for Miss Malley’s
roughness to Tommy, Mrs. Carthy concluded that
Miss Malley was the source of the ugly rumors
about Nance also. She immediately laid the
whole blame of Tommy’s death at her feet, too;
not seeing that Miss Malley was as ignorant of
how tetanus could be caught as herself. Then
Mrs. Carthy’s dark-blue eyes turned black under
a scowl of silent fury. But through all her rage
she was thinking she could manage the township
gossip more effectively than Miss Malley. And
she did.
* * • •
Miss Malley was fond of children. It was a
fortnight after the doctor’s explanation, when the
postmistress called several urchins to her that she
might give them lollies. The children liked lollies
very much, but to her surprise they drew shyly
back, and when she approached they gave evident
signs of running away. Very much hurt, she re-
turned to the office. Next day it was the same.
When she turned to the mother of one for an ex-
planation, she was met with a stony stare. Ob-
serving her acquaintances more closely, she found
cold hostility in their looks. She thought it was
a passing feeling due to some mistake, but the
coldness became more marked and the hostility
more open. She made another attempt to be
friendly with the children.
“Yah! ’Oo killed Tommy Carthy?” bawled
one dirty little scamp ; and the jeer was taken up
as a chorus. The people near listened, but
appeared to be deaf. Miss Malley could scarcely
believe her own ears, through utterly failing to see
what she had to do with Tommy’s death.
“Yah ! ’Oo reads letters?” was the next jeer
of the children. She turned white witli fear —and
she half-uttered a wish that Tommy had died before
hehadspoken. But she knew there was no real proof
of her having read the letter. So she went about
for a week with an air of dignified rectitude. This
shocked the moral residents into covert insults,
and resulted in them taking great pains to have
their letters posted and delivered elsewhere. A
further decrease in business would mean investiga-
tion from head-quarters, which meant probable
dismissal and starvation. Fear broke down the
last shred of defiance in Miss Malley.
She called on the doctor, to learn how she might
bo connected with Tommy’s death.
“They are blaming me for it,” she cried, after
the doctor had explained.
“That’s nonsense. You are no more to blame
than the cows.”—Miss Malley was sobbing —“in
fact, the whole township is to blame for allowing
the cows to roam in the main street.” The doctor
was nervous at sight of her tears. “ But it has
cut-up Mrs. Carthy badly.”
Miss Malley determined to apologise to Mrs.
Carthy for her share in the death of little Tommy.
She actually thought her cunning was a touch of
genius.
Mrs. Carthy herself answered the knock, and
her eyes turned black again at sight of her visitor.
“ I have come to see you privately,” said Miss
Malley, witli wretched hesitation at sight of Mrs.
Carthy’s angry face.
She was asked to come in.
“It was only half-an-hour ago,” began Miss
Malley when they were seated, “that! heard I
had in a way something to do with Tommy’s death.
laa so sorry. I never dreamt a simple scratch
could be so terrible. It’s dreadful to be jeered, at
as a murderess .... Even when you know
it isn’t true. ... I am so fond of children. . . .
Ask me to—to —do anything. . . . Do stop those
horrible rumors about me.”
“ Why did you pull my son down so roughly ? ”
“He was so high up that I thought he might
fall and hurt himself.”
“ That’s a lie ! It was because he saw you read-
ing my daughter’s letter. ”
Miss Malley had half expected the accusation,
and had determined to quietly mention a slander-
action ; but Mrs. Carthy’s anger flustered her.
“How did you know it was her letter?” she
began. Then recognising what she had admitted,
she fell on her knees and begged for pardon.
Mrs. Carthy was shaking with anger and con-
tempt. The postmistress grovelled to her.
“If I am dismissed I shall starve. ... I have
nothing to fall back upon. ... Oh ! do forgive
me.” She dropped her head on a chair sobbing.
“ I’ll promise anything,” she moaned.
Mrs. Carthy’s face was white with passion, and
ugly with vengeful feeling. It was one of those fits
of rage of which the intensity itself kept the body
rigid. Had she slackened her muscular tension
she would have lost control of herself altogether,
and beaten Miss Malley to the floor. But she was
thinking furiously that the postmistress should
starve—should be held up to public scorn. She
glared at the crouching figure at her feet, and let
her grovel. At intervals Miss Malley looked up
imploringly. The intervals gradually widened.
Noting which, unconsciously, Mrs. Carthy began to
take a cat-like interest in making her more abject.
“ Do you admit that you’re a liar ? ” asked Mrs.
Carthy at last.
“ Yes,” moaned Miss Malley.
“ That you deserve dismissal ? ”
“Yes.”
“Come to me on your knees, and swear by all
you hold most dear, that you will always speak
respectfully of my daughter.”
She crawled to Mrs. Carthy slowly. “ I swear
it by my father and mother.”
“Now swear that, if I ever ask it, you will
apply for another district.”
“ I swear it.”
“And that you’ll never again try to influence
Bryan O’Shea against my daughter through lying
rumors. ”
“I swear,” repeated the post-mistress, faintly.
“And that you will come to my daughter’s
wedding.”
“ Miss Malley looked up suddenly, with a gleam
of tortured hate in her eyes ; glared at the implac-
able woman standing over her, for a full minute.
Then her head sank, lower and lower ; she bent
and shrunk into a mere bundle of quivering
clothes, hopelessly beaten.
“ You may go to my room and tidy yourself.”
Mrs. Carthy’s manner stung Miss Malley by its
Eerfect indifference. She knew it consummated
er defeat; she rose listlessly, wearily, seeming
to unbundle herself gradually, and crept away.
W. BRENTA.

Re the banana par. in a late issue. It may
be of interest to note that a recent Theosophical
work called the “ Story of Atlantis ” cites the fact
of the seedless banana being found in both the
Eastern and Western tropical regions as evidence
of a sunken continent once uniting the now ocean-
sundered E. and W. The same book claims the
seedless banana as a triumph of prehistoric horti-
culture as practised .by the Mahatmas of the
period. Theosophists also claim that wheat was
not only discovered but invented by said sages in
Atlantis or Lemuria, and cite in proof (?) thereof
that wheat has never been found in the wild state,
although all the other cereals are.
